Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The body is a permanent, unchanging entity.
Correction: Although the body appears stable, it is constantly losing and replacing parts, as shown by the reappearance of dirt after bathing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The dirt on your skin is a sign that your body is constantly decaying. If you know that all of this will be left behind, what are you doing today to prepare for the Day when only your deeds remain?
?
Al-Razi shows that the body is always changing and dissolving. You will not take a single cell of this body with you to the grave. So what are you living for—this dissolving body or something that lasts forever?

The Self is Permanent: Al-Razi's Three Proofs
Al-Razi establishes the permanence of the self with three proofs and concludes it is distinct from the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's three proofs for the permanence of the self and why it is distinct from the body.

Core Idea
The self remains the same across time, proven by direct awareness, knowledge retention, and memory.

Al-Razi begins his second premise by asserting that every person's sense of 'I' refers to something permanent throughout life. To prove this, he offers three rational arguments drawing on immediate self-knowledge, the continuity of learning, and the persistence of memory. Let's examine each proof and see how they lead to the conclusion that the self is an immaterial entity distinct from the body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the passage to grasp that the self (the referent of 'I') is asserted to be permanent from birth to death.
The passage begins by stating the premise clearly.
2
Examine the first proof
Notice that Al-Razi appeals to direct self-awareness: you know you are the same person as twenty years ago. This is immediate knowledge.
This proof relies on the universal experience of self-identity over time.
3
Examine the second proof
Consider the epistemological argument: if the self perished, you would need to re-acquire knowledge, but you retain past learning without reacquisition.
This shows the locus of knowledge (the self) must persist.
4
Examine the third proof
Reflect on memory: if the self changed entirely, you would have no memory of the past, but you do, so the self is permanent.
Memory requires a continuous subject.
5
Conclude with distinction from body
Al-Razi concludes that since the body changes but the self remains, they must be distinct entities.
This supports the immateriality of the soul.

وأما المقدمة الثانية: وهي قولنا: إن المشار إليه لكل أحد بقوله: (أنا): باق دائم من أول العمر إلى آخره. فالأمر فيه ظاهر، ويدل عليه وجوه: الأول: إني أعلم بالضرورة: أني الآن عين ما كنت موجوداً قبل هذا بعشرين سنة. وقد بينا في أول هذا الكتاب: أن أظهر العلوم عند الإنسان: علمه بوجود نفسه، وكما أني أعلم أني أنا موجود الآن، فكذلك أعلم أني أنا الذي كنت موجوداً قبل هذا بعشرين سنة. بل أنا الذي ولدت في السنة الفلانية. وهذا يدل على أن العلم الضروري حاصل لكل أحد بأنه باق من أول العمر إلى آخره. والوجه الثاني في تقرير هذا المطلوب: هو أنه لو لم يكن ذلك الإنسان المعين باقياً من أول العمر إلى آخره، لاحتاج في تحصيل العلوم في كل حين وأوان، إلى كسب جديد، لأن الذي تعلم العلم وحصله، إذا فني وزال، وحصل بعده شيء آخر، فهذا الحادث ما حصل ذلك العلم، وما اكتسبه، فوجب أن لا يحصل له ذلك العلم إلا بكسب جديد. وحيث لا يحتاج إلى هذا الكسب الجديد، علمنا أن محل العلوم باق من أول العمر إلى آخره. والوجه الثالث: أنه إذا فني الأول وجاء الثاني فهذا الثاني غير الأول ولا خبر له من حال الأول البتة فوجب أن لا يحصل للإنسان علم بأحواله الماضية، وأن لا يحصل عنده من العلوم إلا العلوم بالأحوال الحاضرة. وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا أن المشار إليه لكل أحد بقوله: «أنا» شيء باق من أول العمر إلى آخره. فثبت بهذه البيانات: أن الجسد غير باق، لا بمجموعه ولا بشيء من أجزائه، وثبت: أن الإنسان باق. ومن العلوم البديهية: أن الباقي لا يكون عين ما هو غير باق. وهذا يقتضي أن الإنسان شيء مغاير لهذا البدن، وشيء مغاير لهذه الأعضاء المحسوسة التي منها يتولد هذا البدن. وهو المطلوب.
As for the second premise—which is our statement that the referent of everyone's saying 'I' is permanent and enduring from the beginning of life to its end—the matter is evident, and there are proofs for it: First: I know necessarily that I am now the very same I that existed twenty years ago. We have explained at the beginning of this book that the most evident knowledge for man is his knowledge of the existence of his self. Just as I know that I exist now, so too I know that I am the one who existed twenty years ago. Indeed, I am the one who was born in such-and-such a year. This shows that necessary knowledge is present in everyone that he is permanent from the beginning of life to its end. The second proof for this claim is that if that specific person were not permanent from the beginning of life to its end, he would need to acquire knowledge anew at every moment, because the one who learned and acquired knowledge, if he perishes and disappears and something else comes after him, this new thing has not obtained that knowledge nor acquired it, so it would necessarily not obtain that knowledge except by new acquisition. Since he does not need this new acquisition, we know that the locus of knowledge is permanent from the beginning of life to its end. The third proof: if the first perishes and a second comes, this second is not the first and has no information at all about the state of the first, so necessarily man would have no knowledge of his past states, and would only have knowledge of present states. Since this is not the case, we know that the referent of everyone's saying 'I' is a permanent thing from the beginning of life to its end. Thus, it is established by these evidences that the body is not permanent, neither in its entirety nor in any of its parts, and it is established that man is permanent. It is self-evident that the permanent cannot be identical to what is not permanent. This entails that man is something other than this body, and something other than these sensible organs from which this body is generated. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage establishes the second premise and provides three proofs. The claim is that the self (the referent of 'I') is permanent. The first proof is based on direct self-knowledge: one knows intuitively that he is the same person as years ago. The second proof is epistemic: if the self perished, one would need to re-acquire knowledge, but that doesn't happen, so the self is permanent. The third proof is similar: if the self changed, one would have no memory of the past, but we do, so the self is permanent. The conclusion is that the body is not permanent and man (the self) is permanent and distinct from the body. This is a classic argument for the immateriality and persistence of the soul.

Key Terms
المشار إليه
أنا
باق
العلم الضروري
محل العلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The self is identical to the body or its parts.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the self is permanent while the body constantly changes, so they cannot be the same.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why would one need new acquisition of knowledge if the self perishes?
A) Because knowledge is stored in the brain
B) Because the knower would no longer exist to retain it
C) Because knowledge is acquired through senses
D) Because the body must learn anew

Key Takeaway
The self is a permanent, immaterial entity distinct from the body, proven by self-awareness, knowledge continuity, and memory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi says you know by necessity that you are the same person now as you were years ago. When was the last time you truly felt that continuity? Is your daily life driven by that permanent 'I' or by the temporary desires of the body that constantly changes? What would change if you lived from your permanent self instead of your fleeting whims?
?
The second proof says if the self were not permanent, you would need to relearn everything each moment—yet you retain knowledge. Think of a piece of knowledge that has shaped who you are. Are you living in a way that honors that knowledge as belonging to your lasting self? Or do you act as if you are a new person each day, ignoring past lessons?
?
The third proof shows that memory requires a permanent self. Consider a sin you have committed. If you were a new self now, you would have no memory of it and no responsibility. But you do remember. Does that memory weigh on your conscience? How can you use that permanent self to seek forgiveness and change, knowing that the 'I' that sinned is the same 'I' that can repent?

Objection: The Self as Subtle Body and al-Razi's Partial Response
Objection and partial answer regarding the self as subtle body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the self might be a subtle body permeating the physical body, and how al-Razi begins to respond by suggesting bodies may differ in essence.

Core Idea
The self as a subtle body faces the same dissolution problem, but bodies may differ in kind.

This lesson presents an objection to the view that the self is distinct from the body. The objector proposes that the self might be a subtle body permeating the physical body, like fire in coal. However, if that subtle body is still a body, it would be subject to dissolution, leading to the same problem. al-Razi begins his answer by suggesting that bodies can differ in essence, implying some bodies may not dissolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the analogies used: fire in coal, rose water in rose, sesame oil in sesame.
These analogies illustrate a subtle body permeating a physical one.
2
Understand the logical problem
The objector argues that even if the self is a subtle body, it would still be subject to dissolution (like all elemental bodies), so the permanence of the self demands it be something different.
The objection is: if the self is a body, it dissolves; but the self is permanent, so it cannot be a body.
3
See al-Razi's first move
al-Razi begins his answer by suggesting that bodies can differ in essence (māhiyya). Some bodies are elemental (subject to dissolution), but others may be different—perhaps not subject to dissolution.
The answer is cut off, but this sets up a possible distinction.

We say: We have two positions: First: Why is it not permissible to say that bodies differ in essence, some being these elemental bodies, their simples and compounds, and some being bodies opposite to them? If so, that subtle body would still be a body and subject to dissolution, leading to the same problem. The author begins to answer by suggesting that bodies can differ in essence, implying that the soul might be a different kind of body not subject to dissolution. The answer is cut off at the end of the page. This lesson includes the objection and the beginning of the answer.

Refutation of the Philosophers' Objection on Bodily Parts
Objection and answer regarding preservation of bodily parts by the Free Agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers refute the theological objection that God preserves bodily parts from dissolution, using the principles of essential similarity and universal effusion.

Core Idea
God cannot arbitrarily preserve some bodies over others without a cause.

This lesson presents a classic philosophical-theological exchange. The objector concedes bodily similarity but appeals to God's free will to preserve parts. The philosophers respond with a rigorous argument based on two premises: all bodies are essentially alike, and God's effusion is universal, precluding arbitrary preference. Understanding this argument sharpens one's grasp of natural theology and the limits of divine action in philosophical frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector grants that bodily parts are similar to other bodies in essence but says the Free Agent preserves them from dissolution until death, then rewards or punishes them.
2
Identify the first premise
The philosophers have proven that all bodies are similar in their complete essence. This premise is taken as established.
This relies on an earlier proof not reproduced here.
3
Identify the second premise
God's effusion is universal, so He cannot distinguish one similar from another without a cause. This is a purely philosophical premise.
4
Follow the deduction
Since original and subsidiary parts are equal in essence, the conditions causing dissolution apply equally to all. Thus preservation of some without a cause is impossible, and the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقام الثاني : وهو أنا نسلم أن تلك الأجزاء التي هي الإنسان ، ممائلة لسائر الأجسام في تمام الماهية ، إلا أن الفاعل المختار يبقيها في داخل البدن مصونة عن التغير والانحلال ، من أول وقت الحياة إلى آخر وقت الممات ، ثم إنها عند الموت تنفصل . والفاعل المختار يفعل ما بها ما يشاء . إما بأن يوصل إليها الروح والراحة والريحان، وإما بأن يعذبها بأنواع العذاب والخسران والجواب : اعلم : أن الفلاسفة يدفعون هذا الاحتمال ، بناء على مقدمتين : فالمقدمة الأولى : إنهم أقاموا الدلالة على أن جميع الأجسام ، متماثلة في تمام الماهية. وقد سبق ذكر هذا الدليل والمقدمة الثانية : إنه سبحانه وتعالى عام الفيض ، ويمتنع أن يخص أحد المثلين بخاصية وأثر [ لا يحصل في الثاني ] وهذه المقدمة فلسفية محضة إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن تلك الأجزاء الأصلية البدنية مساوية للأجزاء الفرعية القابلة للانحلال في تمام الماهية. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : نسبة الأحوال الموجبة للانحلال والذوبان إلى جميع الأجزاء الموجودة في هذا البدن : على التساوي . ويمتنع أن يقال : إن إله العالم خصص بعض تلك الأجزاء بالإبقاء والصون عن الانحلال والفناء ، من غير سبب مخصص . لأنا ذكرنا : أن الفيض عام ، وأن الترجيح من غير مرجح محال [ في العقول ] وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب حصول الذوبان والانحلال في جميع الأجزاء البدنية ، وحينئذ يندفع هذا السؤال [ ويتم هذا الاستدلال . والله أعلم ] .
And the second position: that we concede that those parts which are the human being are similar to other bodies in their complete essence, except that the Free Agent preserves them inside the body, protected from change and dissolution, from the beginning of life to the time of death; then at death they separate. And the Free Agent does with them what He wills: either He grants them spirit, rest, and fragrance, or He torments them with various kinds of punishment and loss. The answer: Know that the philosophers reject this possibility, based on two premises: The first premise: They have established evidence that all bodies are similar in their complete essence. This proof has been mentioned previously. The second premise: He, exalted and high, is of universal effusion, and it is impossible that He should distinguish one of two similars with a special property and effect [that does not occur in the second]. This premise is purely philosophical. If this is established, we say: Those original bodily parts are equal to the subsidiary parts susceptible to dissolution in their complete essence. And if this is established, we say: The relation of the conditions causing dissolution and melting to all parts present in this body is equal. And it is impossible to say that the Lord of the world singled out some of those parts for preservation and protection from dissolution and annihilation without a specific cause. For we have mentioned that effusion is universal and that preference without a preponderant is impossible [in the intellect]. And if the matter is thus, then dissolution and melting must occur in all bodily parts, and thereby this objection is repelled [and this argument is completed. And God knows best].
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector (apparently a theologian) concedes that the bodily parts are similar to other bodies in essence but argues that the Free Agent (God) preserves those parts from dissolution until death and then rewards or punishes them. The answer (attributed to the philosophers) provides a refutation based on two premises: (1) all bodies are similar in essence (so no intrinsic distinction), and (2) God's effusion is universal, meaning He cannot arbitrarily favor one similar over another without a cause. The author then deduces that if all parts are essentially equal, the conditions for dissolution apply equally to all, so preservation of some without cause is impossible. Therefore, the objection fails and the argument (presumably for the soul's immateriality or reward/punishment) stands. The commentator notes that this is a purely philosophical argument.

الفيض (al-fayḍ)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies God's power to preserve whatever He wills.
Correction: The argument does not deny God's power; it denies arbitrary preference without a cause, based on the principle of universal effusion.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise used by the philosophers to reject the objection?
A) All bodies are similar in essence.
B) God's effusion is universal and He cannot favor one similar over another without cause.
C) The Free Agent preserves all parts equally.
D) The soul is distinct from the body.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' refutation shows that without a cause, God cannot arbitrarily preserve one similar body over another, based on the universal nature of divine effusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot preserve a body without a wise cause, then every preservation and every trial in your life has a reason. Do you truly believe that, or do you sometimes think God is indifferent? Will you actively seek to understand the wisdom behind His decrees starting today?
?
The argument insists that God's effusion is universal — He does not single out without cause. Reflect: have you ever felt that God has singled you out for suffering while others are spared? Does this lesson challenge your perspective to trust that there is a just cause, even if hidden? What would change in your heart if you fully accepted that?
?
The philosophers claim that 'preference without a preponderant is impossible.' This is an intellectual principle. But in your daily life, do you live as if God's actions are arbitrary or as if they are perfectly wise? What is one area where you need to replace doubt with trust in His wisdom?

The Second Proof: Self-Awareness Shows the Self is Not the Body
Proof that self-awareness while unaware of body shows self is distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how immediate self-awareness, even when unaware of the body, proves that the self is distinct from all physical parts.

Core Idea
Your awareness of 'I' persists even when you forget your body — proving the self is not the body.

This second proof takes a different angle from the first. Instead of external or internal bodily changes, it focuses on our direct awareness of ourselves in moments of deep concentration. Sanusi argues that this awareness itself reveals the self's independence from the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's two parts
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The proof is divided into two clear sections: the first establishes that we know the self while unaware of the body; the second argues that what is known must be distinct from what is unknown.
2
Examine the first part (المقام الأول)
Understand the demonstration: a person absorbed in a serious task can say 'I thought, I saw, I heard' without attending to any body part. This shows the self is known independently of the body.
The example illustrates that self-awareness does not require awareness of body parts.
3
Examine the second part (المقام الثاني)
The second part states that what is not attended to (the body parts) is different from what is known (the self). This is called self-evident; denying it leads to contradiction. Conclusion: the self is not the body.
The argument relies on the principle that awareness and unawareness cannot apply to the same thing in the same respect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في المسألة : أن [ نقول () ] : إن المشار إليه لكل أحد بقول «أنا» ، قد يكون معلوماً له ، حال كونه غافلاً عن جميع الأجزاء الظاهرة والباطنة . والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم ، فوجب أن تكون النفس مغايرة الجميع هذه الأجزاء الظاهرة والباطنة . أما المقام الأول : فالدليل عليه : أن الإنسان حال اهتمامه بالمهم الشديد ، قد يقول تفكرت وأبصرت وسمعت ، مع أنه يكون حال تكلمه بهذا غافلاً عن وجهه ويده وقلبه ودماغه وسائر أعضائه . وذلك يدل على ما ذكرناه . وأما المقام الثاني : وهو أن المغفول عنه ، مغاير للمعلوم . فهذا بديهي . وإلا لزم اجتماع النقيضين، وهو محال . وحينئذ يظهر أن النفس غير هذا الجسد .
The second proof on the matter: That we say: That which is referred to by everyone by the statement 'I' is known to him while he is unaware of all the apparent and hidden parts. And that which is known is different from that which is not known, so the self must be different from all these apparent and hidden parts. As for the first part: The proof for it is that a person, when intensely occupied with a serious matter, may say 'I thought, I saw, I heard,' while at the moment of saying this he is unaware of his face, hand, heart, brain, and all his limbs. This indicates what we have mentioned. As for the second part: That that which is not attended to is different from that which is known. This is self-evident; otherwise, the conjunction of two contradictories would follow, which is impossible. And thereby it becomes clear that the self is other than this body.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for the distinction between the self (nafs) and the body. The proof is structured in two parts: (1) The referent of 'I' is known to a person even when he is unaware of all his bodily parts (both internal and external). This is demonstrated by the fact that a person deeply engaged in thought can say 'I thought, saw, heard' while not attending to any bodily part. (2) That which is unknown (the body parts in that moment) is distinct from that which is known (the self). This is said to be self-evident, otherwise a contradiction would arise. Therefore, the self must be something other than the body. The argument relies on the principle that awareness of something implies its distinction from what one is unaware of.

النفس (nafs)
الجسد (jasad)
المشار إليه (al-mushāru ilayh)
الغفل (al-ghafla)
المعلوم (al-maʿlūm)
المغفول عنه (al-maghfūl ʿanh)
اجتماع النقيضين (ijtimāʿ al-naqīḍayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being unaware of body parts does not prove the self is non-physical.
Correction: The argument is not about unawareness itself, but that the immediate awareness of 'I' continues independently of any awareness of body parts, showing the self is not identical to any body part.

Checkpoint
What is the second part (المقام الثاني) of the proof?
A) The self is known while unaware of the body.
B) That which is not attended to is different from that which is known.
C) A person says 'I thought, I saw, I heard' while unaware of limbs.
D) The self is a spiritual substance.

Key Takeaway
Immediate self-awareness, independent of any bodily awareness, shows that the self is not the body but a distinct entity.

The Soul as One Perceiver: Razi's Unified Proof
Razi enumerates four cognitive domains and uses a common principle to prove their unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi proves that the soul is a single entity responsible for all cognitive acts, from sensation to universal judgment.

Core Idea
All cognitive acts — sensing, imagining, estimating, thinking — belong to one soul.

Razi presents a powerful argument for the unity of the soul. He shows that whenever we compare two things (like a sensed object and an imagined one), the same judge must be aware of both. This principle, repeated throughout, leads to the conclusion that one entity performs all mental operations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp Razi's flow.
Notice the repeated phrase: 'والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما'.
2
Identify the repeated principle
Razi uses the same principle for each cognitive act: the judge of two things must have both present. This is the core of the proof.
3
Follow Razi's application
Trace how he applies the principle to sensation/imagination, estimation, thinking (composition/analysis), and universal judgment.
Each step concludes that a single entity must be responsible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final statement asserts that the one described with all perceptions must be one thing. Consider why this is significant for understanding the soul.
The phrase 'وهو المطلوب' marks the end of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن ذلك الحاس هو بعينه المتخيل. وذلك لأنا إذا تخيلنا صورة زيد، أو تخيلنا صوته، أو تخيلنا طعم العسل أو رائحته، أو حرارة النار، أو برودة الماء. فإذا أحسسنا بإحدى الحواس الخمسة أحد هذه المحسوسات حكمنا: بأن هذا المحسوس هو بعينه ذلك المتخيل، والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فوجب الإقرار بوجود شيء يكون بعينه الحساس ويكون هو بعينه ذلك المتخيل. ثم نقول: إن القوى الوهمية عند القوم: عبارة عن قوة تحكم على الأشخاص المحسوسة بمعان جزئية غير محسوسة، مثل: الحكم على هذا الشخص بأنه عدو، وعلى ذلك الآخر بأنه صديق، والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فوجب الاعتراف بوجود شيء واحد يكون هو بعينه مدركاً لهذه الصور ولهذه المعاني، حتى يمكنه أن يحكم على تلك الصور بهذه المعاني. ثم نقول: القوة المفكرة هي التي تقوى أولاً على تركيب الصور بعضها بالبعض، وثانياً على تركيب المعاني بعضها بالبعض، وثالثاً على تركيب بعض الصور ببعض المعاني، ورابعاً على تحليل بعضها عن البعض. ولا شك أن هذا التركيب والتحليل حكم على البعض بثبوت البعض الآخر أو بسلبه عنه، والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فلا بد من شيء واحد يكون هو الحساس للكل، ويكون هو بعينه المتخيل للكل، ويكون هو بعينه المدرك للصور والمعاني، ويكون هو بعينه المتصرف فيها بأسرها بالتركيب تارة وبالتحليل أخرى. ثم نقول: إنه يمكننا أن نحكم على هذا الشخص بأنه إنسان وأنه ليس بفرس. والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فلا بد من شيء واحد يكون هو بعينه مدركاً لهذا الجزئي ولهذا الكلي، حتى يمكنه أن ينسب ذلك الكلي إلى ذلك الجزئي تارة بالإنحلال وتارة بالإيجاب وتارة بالسلب. فظهر بهذا البرهان الذي لا ريب فيه: أن الموصوف بجميع الإدراكات الكلية والجزئية، وأن المتصرف فيها بالتحليل والتركيب، يجب أن يكون شيئاً واحداً. وهو المطلوب.
That sensor is precisely the imaginer. This is because when we imagine the form of Zayd, or imagine his voice, or imagine the taste of honey or its smell, or the heat of fire, or the cold of water, then if we sense with one of the five senses one of these sensed things, we judge that this sensed thing is precisely that imagined thing. And the judge of two things must have the two judged things present to it, so it is necessary to affirm the existence of a thing that is precisely the sensor and is precisely that imaginer. Then we say: The estimative faculty (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) according to them is a faculty that judges about sensed individuals with partial, non-sensed meanings, such as judging that this person is an enemy and that other is a friend. And the judge of two things must have the two judged things present to it, so it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of one thing that is precisely the perceiver of these forms and these meanings, so that it can judge about those forms with these meanings. Then we say: The thinking faculty (al-quwwa al-mufakkira) is that which is capable, first, of composing forms with each other; second, of composing meanings with each other; third, of composing some forms with some meanings; and fourth, of analyzing some from others. There is no doubt that this composition and analysis is a judgment about some things by affirming or negating others. And the judge of two things must have the two judged things present to it, so there must be one thing that is the sensor of the whole, and is precisely the imaginer of the whole, and is precisely the perceiver of forms and meanings, and is precisely the disposer of them all, sometimes by composition and sometimes by analysis. Then we say: It is possible for us to judge about this person that he is a human and that he is not a horse. And the judge of two things must have the two judged things present to it, so there must be one thing that is precisely the perceiver of this particular and this universal, so that it can relate that universal to that particular, sometimes by dissolution, sometimes by affirmation, and sometimes by negation. So it becomes evident through this proof, in which there is no doubt, that the one described with all universal and particular perceptions, and that disposes them by analysis and composition, must be one thing. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the unity of the soul (or self) as the single perceiver behind all cognitive acts. The author argues step by step, showing that the same entity that senses also imagines, estimates, thinks, and judges universals. He begins with the relationship between sensation and imagination: when we imagine something (e.g., Zayd's form) and then sense it, we judge that the sensed object is the same as the imagined one. This judgment requires the presence of both the sensed and the imagined in the judge, so the judge must be one thing that is both sensor and imaginer. Next, he discusses estimation (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya), which judges non-sensed meanings (e.g., hostility) in sensed individuals. Again, the judge of the form and the meaning must be one thing. Then he considers the thinking faculty, which composes and analyzes forms and meanings. This composition and analysis is a judgment of relation, again requiring a single judge. Finally, he treats judgment of universals, e.g., judging that Zayd is human (affirmation) or not a horse (negation). Such judgment relates a universal to a particular, necessitating a single perceiver of both. The conclusion is that the one described with all perceptions and dispositions must be one thing. The phrase 'وهو المطلوب' marks the end of the proof.

Key Terms
الحاس
المتخيل
القوى الوهمية
القوة المفكرة
الإدراكات الكلية والجزئية
التركيب والتحليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that each cognitive faculty (sensation, imagination, estimation, thinking) operates independently without a common core.
Correction: Razi shows that all faculties require a single judge, because judgments that compare two items (e.g., a sensed object and an imagined one) demand a unified perceiver aware of both.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key principle used repeatedly to prove the unity of the perceiver?
A) The imagination is stronger than sensation.
B) The judge of two things must have both present to it.
C) The thinking faculty is independent of the senses.
D) Universals cannot be perceived by the senses.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that the soul is a single entity that performs all acts of cognition, from sensation to abstract reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sense an object you previously imagined, do you experience a single 'you' that both imagines and senses? How does Razi's proof challenge any feeling you might have that your mind is fragmented or multiple?
?
If your soul is truly a single perceiver behind every thought, feeling, and judgment, then every act of cognition—even your secret thoughts—is witnessed by the same self that will be held accountable before Allah. How does this awareness change the way you approach your inner thoughts today?
?
Razi argues that judging two things requires them to be present to one thing. Consider a moment when you judged yourself as sinful yet beloved to Allah. What does that judgment reveal about the unity of your soul? Are you willing to accept that this one perceiver is entirely responsible for both the sin and the repentance?

The Perceiver is the Agent of Actions
Razi argues that the perceiving self is identical with the agent of voluntary actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that the same self that perceives is also the agent of voluntary actions.

Core Idea
The perceiver and the agent of action are one and the same self.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a rigorous argument for the identity of the perceiving self with the agent of voluntary actions. He analyzes the nature of voluntary action, showing that desire and aversion stem from beliefs about benefit and harm, and then demonstrates that these states must belong to the same entity that perceives and believes. This sets the stage for a unified understanding of the human soul's faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is the second premise in a larger argument.
2
Identify the causal chain
The passage defines voluntary action as arising from belief, which produces either desire or aversion, leading to action or refraining. Trace this chain step by step.
This chain is crucial for the reductio argument that follows.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Razi argues that if desire/aversion were in a separate entity from perception, then the desire would not be caused by the belief. This leads to a contradiction. Understand why.
He also adds that desiring something implies awareness of it.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes by linking agent = intender = perceiver, showing the identity. Verify each step of the transitive proof.
The passage ends with 'this is a decisive proof that...' implying the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في بيان أن ذلك الشيء الواحد الذي هو المدرك يجب أن يكون بعينه هو الفاصل لجميع الأفعال: والدليل عليه: أن الإنسان إنما يفعل بالقصد والاختيار. والفعل الاختياري معناه: أنه إذا اعتقد في الشيء أنه مشتمل على نفع خالص أو راجح أوجب هذا الاعتقاد حصول رغبة في ذلك الفعل، وإذا حصلت تلك الرغبة صار القادر فاعلاً. وأما إذا اعتقد أن في الفعل الفلاني مفسدة خالصة أو راجحة أوجب ذلك الاعتقاد حصول نفرة عن ذلك الفعل وإذا حصلت تلك النفرة صار القادر ممتنعاً عن الفعل. وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت أن ذلك المدرك بعينه يجب أن يكون هو الموصوف بالرغبة تارة وبالنفرة أخرى، إذ لو كان الموصوف بالرغبة والنفرة شيء مغاير لذلك المدرك لكانت هذه الرغبة والنفرة حاصلة لشيء خالي عن الإدراك والاعتقاد. وحينئذ يكون حصول تلك الرغبة وتلك النفرة لا لأجل ذلك الاعتقاد. وقد فرضناه معللاً به. هذا خلف. وأيضاً: فالراغب في الشيء أو النافر عنه قد أشار فهمه إلى ذلك الشيء المرغوب فيه أو المهروب منه فثبت: أن المريد للفعل أو الترك يجب أن يكون هو بعينه مدركاً لذلك الشيء. وظاهر أن الفاعل للفعل هو الذي أراد ذلك الفعل، ولما ثبت أن الفاعل هو بعينه ذلك الشيء الذي أراده، وثبت: أن ذلك المريد هو بعينه الذي أدركه، ثبت أن الفاعل هو بعينه ذلك المدرك. وهذا برهان قاطع على أن
The second introduction: To clarify that that one thing, which is the perceiver, must be precisely the agent of all actions. The proof of it is that man only acts with intention and choice. Voluntary action (al-fi'l al-ikhtiyari) means that when he believes that something contains pure or preponderant benefit, this belief necessitates the occurrence of desire (raghba) for that action, and when that desire occurs, the capable being becomes an agent. But if he believes that in such an action there is pure or preponderant harm, that belief necessitates the occurrence of aversion (nufra) from that action, and when that aversion occurs, the capable being refrains from the action. If this is established, it is established that that perceiver must be precisely the one described sometimes with desire and sometimes with aversion, because if the one described with desire and aversion were something other than that perceiver, then this desire and aversion would occur in something devoid of perception and belief. Then the occurrence of that desire and aversion would not be because of that belief, but we assumed it to be caused by it. This is a contradiction. Also, the one who desires something or is averse to it has directed his understanding towards that desired or fled-from thing, so it is established that the one who intends the action or its abandonment must be precisely the perceiver of that thing. And it is apparent that the agent of the action is the one who intended that action. Since it is established that the agent is precisely that same thing that intended it, and it is established that that intender is precisely the one who perceived it, it is established that the agent is precisely that perceiver. This is a decisive proof that...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the identity of the perceiving self with the agent of voluntary actions. The author introduces a second premise (المقدمة الثانية) to show that the one thing that perceives is also the efficient cause of all actions. The proof analyzes the nature of voluntary action: a voluntary act depends on a belief about benefit or harm, which generates desire or aversion, leading to action or refraining. For this to be coherent, the entity that desires or is averse must be the same entity that perceives and believes, because otherwise the desire or aversion would arise in something without perception, contradicting the causal role of belief. Additionally, the one who desires is aware of the object, so the desirer is a perceiver. Since the agent is the one who intends, and the intender is the perceiver, the agent and perceiver must be one. The argument concludes with 'وهذا برهان قاطع على أن' (this is a decisive proof that...), implying the conclusion that the agent is the perceiver. The proof uses logical reasoning (reductio ad absurdum) and transitive identification.

Key Terms
الفعل الاختياري
الرغبة
النفرة
الاعتقاد
المريد
الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that desire for an action could arise from a separate, non-perceiving part of the soul.
Correction: Razi argues that if desire were in a separate entity, it would not be caused by belief, contradicting the causal assumption. Thus, desire must belong to the same perceiving self.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the cause of desire for an action in voluntary action?
A) A purely physical impulse.
B) A belief that the action contains benefit.
C) The presence of the action itself.
D) The absence of aversion.

Key Takeaway
The self that perceives and believes is the very same self that desires, intends, and acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your actions are driven by your beliefs about benefit and harm. Right now, is there a belief you hold that is leading you to an action you know is wrong? What would it take for you to change that belief, and why haven’t you yet?
?
If your perceiving self and acting self are truly one, then you cannot blame your desires on your body or circumstances. Where in your life have you been pretending that your actions are separate from what you truly believe?
?
Razi’s proof shows that your intention to act comes from your own perception. Today, will you let a truth you know about Allah (like His awareness or His promise) actually move you to action, or will you let it sit as a dead belief?

Proof from Mutual Opposition of Body and Soul
Proof from mutual opposition of body and soul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutual opposition between body and soul serves as proof that the soul is a distinct substance from the body.

Core Idea
The soul's states oppose the body's, proving their distinctness.

This lesson presents a concise argument from a classical text that highlights a simple yet powerful observation: the states of the body and soul are inversely related. When the body is alive and happy, the soul is in a state of suffering or unconsciousness (like sleep), and vice versa. This mutual opposition indicates that the soul is not the same as the body or any of its parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's claim.
Notice the parallel structure: body's life vs soul's death, etc.
2
Identify the opposing states
List the pairs: (life of body → death of soul), (happiness of body → misery of soul), (wakefulness of body → sleep of soul).
The text mentions three pairs, though the first may be corrupted.
3
Understand the logical inference
The author argues that this inverse relationship is strong evidence that the soul is not identical to the body.
If they were the same substance, they would not have opposite effects.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the soul is something other than the body and its parts.
This is the desired outcome of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توجب موت البدن وشقاوته وقوته . وأن حياة البدن وسعادته ويقظته ، توجب. موت النفس وشقاوتها ونومها . وذلك من أدل الدلائل على أن النفس شيء غير البدن ، وغير كل واحد من أجزائه وأبعاضه وهو المطلوب
It necessitates the death of the body and its misery and its strength [؟]. And that the life of the body and its happiness and its wakefulness necessitates the death of the soul and its misery and its sleep. And that is among the strongest proofs that the soul is something other than the body, and other than each one of its parts and its limbs, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a proof based on the mutual opposition between the states of the body and the soul. The author states that something (presumably the soul's activity) causes the body's death, misery, and strength [or sleep?], while the body's life, happiness, and wakefulness cause the soul's death, misery, and sleep. This inverse relationship demonstrates that the soul is a distinct substance from the body and its parts, not identical with it.

Key Terms
نفس
بدن
أجزاء
أبعاض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is identical to the body or a physical part of it.
Correction: The mutual opposition between body and soul shows they cannot be the same substance, as they have opposite effects.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim is among the strongest proofs that the soul is other than the body?
A) The soul's existence after death
B) The mutual opposition between the states of body and soul
C) The complexity of the body
D) The fact that the body dies

Key Takeaway
The mutual opposition between body and soul is strong evidence that the soul is a distinct, non-physical substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you indulge your body in pleasure, do you notice a deadening in your soul? What specific examples can you recall where bodily happiness led to spiritual misery?
?
If the soul is truly distinct and opposed to the body's states, why do you live as if pleasing the body is the only way to happiness? What would change if you prioritized your soul's life over your body's?
?
The proof shows that wakefulness of the body causes sleep of the soul. How much of your daily life is spent 'awake' to worldly matters but spiritually asleep? Do you even notice the sleep of your soul?

The Objection of Interpenetration and the First Refutation
Author presents the interpenetration objection and the first refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the soul could be a body interpenetrating the body with parts assigned to each organ, and the first refutation showing this would make the human a mere aggregate.

Core Idea
The soul cannot be divided into parts without destroying the unity of the human self.

The author has already proven that if the soul were a body inside the body, equality of control over organs would be impossible. But an objector might propose that the soul is a body that interpenetrates the body, with each part of the soul assigned to a specific organ. This lesson examines that objection and the first reason why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objector tries to salvage the idea of a bodily soul by distributing its parts to different organs.
2
Understand the first refutation
The author argues this would make the human being a collection of scattered parts, contradicting the previously established unity of the self.
This connects to the earlier proof that the human is a single subject of knowledge and action.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the unity of the human agent is essential and how the interpenetration view undermines it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد دللنا : على أنه لو كان جسماً داخلاً في البدن ، لما حصلت هذه التسوية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن لا تكون النفس جسماً موجوداً في البدن . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : النفس جسم مشابك للبدن ، فبعض أجزائه مشابك في العين ، وبعضها في الأذن ، وبعضها في القلب وبعضها في الدماغ وبهذا التقدير فيتولى كل جزء من أجزاء النفس ، جزءاً من أجزاء البدن . فنقول : هذا باطل من وجوه الأول : إنه على هذا التقدير يكون الإنسان عبارة عن مجموع أجزاء كثيرة متفرقة في أعضاء مختلفة . ونحن قد دللنا في الفصل المتقدم على أن الإنسان يجب أن يكون شيئاً واحداً موصوفاً بجميع العلوم وبجميع الإدراكات ، وأن يكون هو بعينه فاعلاً لجميع الأفعال . وذلك يبطل هذا القدر ، ويجميع الذي ذكرتم الوجه الثاني في الجواب : إن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كما ذكرتم ، إلا أنه يلزم أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء مستقلا بالتصرف في ذلك العضو المخصوص ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون اشتغال بعض تلك الأجزاء بفعله الخاص به ، مانعاً للجزء الآخر من فعله الخاص به . ومعلوم : أن هذا حاصل . فإن الإنسان إذا اشتغل بالغضب ، امتنعت الشهوة عليه ، وإذا اشتغل بالشهوة ، امتنع الغضب عليه وإذا اشتغل بالفكر امتنع كلاهما عليه والوجه الثالث في الجواب : إن هذا السؤال إنما يتم لو قلنا : إنه حصل مع كل جزء من أجزاء البدن : جزء من أجزاء النفس . حتى يقال : بأن كل واحد منها يحرك ذلك الجزء من غير احتياج إلى شيء من الآلات ، فحينئذ تكون نسبة النفس إلى جميع الأعمال على السوية. ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل وإلا لزم أن تكون جميع الأعضاء متساوية في كونها عضواً واحداً رئيساً ، مستقلاً بقبول قوة الحياة . ومعلوم : أنه ليس كذلك . لأن القلب والدماغ أقوى في تعلق النفس بهما من سائر الأعضاء . فثبت بهذه البيانات : أنه لا يجوز أن تكون النفس جسماً سارياً في البدن . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : النفس جسم مغاير للبدن وغير داخل فيه ، فهذا بعيد عن العقل ، ولم يقل به أحد . ولما بطلت الأقسام الثلاثة بقي القسم الرابع ، وهو أن النفس جوهر مجرد عن الجسمية وعلائقها ، وأن نسبته إلى جميع الأجزاء على السوية ، فلم يكن اشتغاله باستعمال بعض الأجزاء ، أشق عليه من استعمال سائر الأعضاء وهو المطلوب .
And we have proven that if it were a body inside the body, this equality would not occur, so then it follows that the soul is not a body existing in the body. For if they say: Why is it not permissible to say that the soul is a body interpenetrating the body, so some of its parts interpenetrate in the eye, some in the ear, some in the heart, and some in the brain, and with this estimation each part of the soul takes charge of a part of the body? We say: This is false in several respects: First: On this estimation, the human being would be an aggregate of many parts scattered in different organs. And we have proven in the preceding chapter that the human being must be a single thing described with all sciences and all perceptions, and that he himself is the doer of all actions. And that invalidates this capacity, and [؟] that which you mentioned. The second aspect of the answer: Even if the matter were as you mentioned, it would still necessitate that each of those parts be independent in disposing of that specific organ. And if that were the case, it would be necessary that the occupation of some of those parts with its specific action does not prevent another part from its specific action. And it is known that this occurs. For if a human is occupied with anger, desire is prevented from him; and if he is occupied with desire, anger is prevented from him; and if he is occupied with thought, both are prevented from him. The third aspect of the answer: This question is only valid if we said that there occurs with each part of the body a part of the soul, such that it is said that each of them moves that part without need of any instruments, then the relation of the soul to all actions would be equal. And it is known that that is false; otherwise it would necessitate that all organs be equal in being a single chief organ, independent in accepting the power of life. And it is known that it is not so, because the heart and the brain are stronger in the attachment of the soul to them than the rest of the organs. So it is established by these evidences that the soul cannot be a body permeating the body. As for the third division: that it is said the soul is a body separate from the body and not inside it, this is far from reason and no one has said it. And since the three divisions are invalid, the fourth division remains, which is that the soul is a substance abstracted from corporeality and its attachments, and that its relation to all parts is equal, so its occupation with using some parts is not more difficult for it than using the rest of the organs — and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that the soul is a body interpenetrating the body (القسم الثاني), followed by the affirmation of the correct view that the soul is a non-bodily substance (القسم الرابع). The author first restates his earlier proof that if the soul were a body inside the body, the equal control over all organs would not occur. Then he addresses an objection: why not say the soul is a body that interpenetrates the body, with parts assigned to each organ? The author responds with three objections. The first objection: this would make the human being a collection of scattered parts, contradicting the earlier proof that the human is a single unified subject of all knowledge and actions. The second objection: if each part of the soul were independent, one part's activity should not prevent another's; yet we observe that anger prevents desire, etc., showing they are not independent. The third objection: this objection only works if each body part had a soul part equal in power, making all organs equally chief, but the heart and brain are stronger in soul attachment, so not equal. Thus the soul cannot be a body interpenetrating the body. The author then dismisses the third division (soul as a body separate and not inside) as irrational and unheard of. With all three divisions invalid, the fourth remains: the soul is a substance abstracted from corporeality and its attachments, relating equally to all parts, making the use of any organ equally easy.

نفس (nafs)
جسم (jism)
بدن (badan)
تسوية (taswiya)
مجموع (majmoo')
أجزاء (ajzaa')

D) A body separate from the body

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot be a body with parts because that would destroy the unity of the human self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson insists that your true self must be a single unified entity. In what area of your life today will you actively resist that fragmentation and realign your entire being under the command of Allah?
?
The author argues that the human self is one, responsible for all perceptions and actions. If that is true, then you cannot compartmentalize your life into 'spiritual' and 'worldly' parts. Where have you allowed a division between your actions in prayer and your actions in daily work? How will you unify your intention (niyya) so that every act is for Allah alone?
?
This lesson shows that a 'soul' made of parts cannot be the true self—it would be a mere collection. Are you living as a collection of scattered duties and distractions, or as a single person wholly devoted to knowing and serving your Lord? What one commitment will you make today to stop letting external demands split your focus away from your ultimate purpose?

The Second and Third Refutations of the Interpenetration View
Author gives second and third objections against interpenetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two further reasons why the soul cannot be a body interpenetrating the body: the independence of parts would prevent interference yet we observe interference, and the inequality of organs contradicts the required equality.

Core Idea
If the soul had independent parts, one part's action would not prevent another, but we see anger preventing desire, showing the soul is one.

The author continues his refutation of the interpenetration view with two additional objections. The second objection points out that if each part of the soul were independent in its organ, then one part's activity should not interfere with another, yet we experience interference (e.g., anger prevents desire). The third objection argues that the interpenetration view would require all organs to be equal in being chief, but heart and brain are stronger in soul attachment. These arguments further demonstrate the impossibility of a bodily soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Notice how the author uses the observed interference between emotions to argue against independent soul parts.
2
Read the third objection
See how the author argues that the interpenetration view would require equality of all organs, which is false.
3
Connect to the conclusion
These objections together show that the soul cannot be a body interpenetrating the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد دللنا : على أنه لو كان جسماً داخلاً في البدن ، لما حصلت هذه التسوية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن لا تكون النفس جسماً موجوداً في البدن . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : النفس جسم مشابك للبدن ، فبعض أجزائه مشابك في العين ، وبعضها في الأذن ، وبعضها في القلب وبعضها في الدماغ وبهذا التقدير فيتولى كل جزء من أجزاء النفس ، جزءاً من أجزاء البدن . فنقول : هذا باطل من وجوه الأول : إنه على هذا التقدير يكون الإنسان عبارة عن مجموع أجزاء كثيرة متفرقة في أعضاء مختلفة . ونحن قد دللنا في الفصل المتقدم على أن الإنسان يجب أن يكون شيئاً واحداً موصوفاً بجميع العلوم وبجميع الإدراكات ، وأن يكون هو بعينه فاعلاً لجميع الأفعال . وذلك يبطل هذا القدر ، ويجميع الذي ذكرتم الوجه الثاني في الجواب : إن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كما ذكرتم ، إلا أنه يلزم أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء مستقلا بالتصرف في ذلك العضو المخصوص ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون اشتغال بعض تلك الأجزاء بفعله الخاص به ، مانعاً للجزء الآخر من فعله الخاص به . ومعلوم : أن هذا حاصل . فإن الإنسان إذا اشتغل بالغضب ، امتنعت الشهوة عليه ، وإذا اشتغل بالشهوة ، امتنع الغضب عليه وإذا اشتغل بالفكر امتنع كلاهما عليه والوجه الثالث في الجواب : إن هذا السؤال إنما يتم لو قلنا : إنه حصل مع كل جزء من أجزاء البدن : جزء من أجزاء النفس . حتى يقال : بأن كل واحد منها يحرك ذلك الجزء من غير احتياج إلى شيء من الآلات ، فحينئذ تكون نسبة النفس إلى جميع الأعمال على السوية. ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل وإلا لزم أن تكون جميع الأعضاء متساوية في كونها عضواً واحداً رئيساً ، مستقلاً بقبول قوة الحياة . ومعلوم : أنه ليس كذلك . لأن القلب والدماغ أقوى في تعلق النفس بهما من سائر الأعضاء . فثبت بهذه البيانات : أنه لا يجوز أن تكون النفس جسماً سارياً في البدن . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : النفس جسم مغاير للبدن وغير داخل فيه ، فهذا بعيد عن العقل ، ولم يقل به أحد . ولما بطلت الأقسام الثلاثة بقي القسم الرابع ، وهو أن النفس جوهر مجرد عن الجسمية وعلائقها ، وأن نسبته إلى جميع الأجزاء على السوية ، فلم يكن اشتغاله باستعمال بعض الأجزاء ، أشق عليه من استعمال سائر الأعضاء وهو المطلوب .
And we have proven that if it were a body inside the body, this equality would not occur, so then it follows that the soul is not a body existing in the body. For if they say: Why is it not permissible to say that the soul is a body interpenetrating the body, so some of its parts interpenetrate in the eye, some in the ear, some in the heart, and some in the brain, and with this estimation each part of the soul takes charge of a part of the body? We say: This is false in several respects: First: On this estimation, the human being would be an aggregate of many parts scattered in different organs. And we have proven in the preceding chapter that the human being must be a single thing described with all sciences and all perceptions, and that he himself is the doer of all actions. And that invalidates this capacity, and [؟] that which you mentioned. The second aspect of the answer: Even if the matter were as you mentioned, it would still necessitate that each of those parts be independent in disposing of that specific organ. And if that were the case, it would be necessary that the occupation of some of those parts with its specific action does not prevent another part from its specific action. And it is known that this occurs. For if a human is occupied with anger, desire is prevented from him; and if he is occupied with desire, anger is prevented from him; and if he is occupied with thought, both are prevented from him. The third aspect of the answer: This question is only valid if we said that there occurs with each part of the body a part of the soul, such that it is said that each of them moves that part without need of any instruments, then the relation of the soul to all actions would be equal. And it is known that that is false; otherwise it would necessitate that all organs be equal in being a single chief organ, independent in accepting the power of life. And it is known that it is not so, because the heart and the brain are stronger in the attachment of the soul to them than the rest of the organs. So it is established by these evidences that the soul cannot be a body permeating the body. As for the third division: that it is said the soul is a body separate from the body and not inside it, this is far from reason and no one has said it. And since the three divisions are invalid, the fourth division remains, which is that the soul is a substance abstracted from corporeality and its attachments, and that its relation to all parts is equal, so its occupation with using some parts is not more difficult for it than using the rest of the organs — and that is what is sought.

Affirming the Soul as an Abstract Substance
Author dismisses the third division and affirms the fourth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul cannot be a body separate from the body (the third division) and that the correct view is the soul as a non-bodily substance relating equally to all parts.

Core Idea
The soul is not a body at all; it is an abstract substance with equal relation to all organs.

After refuting the idea that the soul is a body inside the body (first division) and the idea that it interpenetrates the body (second division), the author briefly dismisses the third division—that the soul is a body separate from and not inside the body—as irrational and unheard of. He then affirms the fourth division: the soul is a substance abstracted from corporeality, relating equally to all organs, making all actions equally easy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third division
The author dismisses the idea of a soul as a separate body as far from reason and never advocated.
2
Recognize the correct view
The soul is a substance abstracted from corporeality and its attachments.
3
See the implication
The soul's relation to all parts is equal, so using any organ is equally easy.

6 min
Tenth Proof: Soul Not Body or Organ
Author presents the tenth proof that the soul is not the body or an organ, linking to resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the tenth proof that the soul is not the body or an organ, and why this is necessary for resurrection and divine justice.

Core Idea
If the soul were the body, bodily resurrection and just reward would be impossible.

This lesson examines a powerful argument that the soul cannot be identical to the physical body or any of its parts. The author shows that such a view would undermine the very possibility of resurrection, reward, and punishment. By tracing the logical consequences, we see why the soul must be a non-material substance that persists after bodily death.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'. Let the argument unfold naturally before diving into analysis.
Pay attention to the words 'لو كانت' (if it were) – this signals a hypothetical.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Find the main conditional: 'If the soul were this body or an organ of it, then resurrection and reward and punishment would be impossible.' This is the core claim.
The author then adds two additional consequences: humans would be lowest animals and creation would be futile.
3
Recognize the false consequences
The author asserts that these consequences are false (باطلة). Since the consequences are false, the antecedent (soul being body/organ) must also be false. This is a classic modus tollens argument.
Consider why these consequences are considered false – they contradict religious teachings about resurrection and divine purpose.
4
Understand the first premise
The author begins to establish the first premise: If the soul were the body or an organ, then resurrection would be false. The explanation is that the body decays and perishes completely, so there would be no persisting subject for resurrection.
This premise is the foundation for the proof; the full argument continues beyond this passage.
5
Connect to eschatology
Reflect on why this matters: bodily resurrection requires a continuous subject (the soul) that survives death. If the soul were material like the body, it would perish and could not be resurrected.
This connects to Islamic beliefs in the afterlife and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو كانت النفس هذا البدن ، أو عضواً من أعضاء هذا البدن ، لامتنع الحشر والنشر والثواب والعقاب ، ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكان الإنسان أخس الحيوانات ولكان تكوينه وإيجاده عبثاً محضاً . وهذه اللوازم باطلة ، فكان القول بأن النفس عين هذا البدن ، أو عضو من أعضائه : باطلاً . فنفتقر في تقرير هذا الدليل إلى تقرير مقدمات المقدمة الأولى : في بيان أن النفس ، لو كانت عبارة عن هذا البدن ، أو عن عضو من أعضائه ، لكان القول بالبعث والقيامة باطلا . فتقريره : إنا نشاهد أن هذا البدن مع جميع أعضائه وأجزائه يتفرق ويتمزق ، ويصير فانياً
The tenth proof: If the soul were this body, or an organ of this body, then resurrection and gathering and reward and punishment would be impossible. And if that were the case, then the human being would be the lowest of animals, and his formation and existence would be pure futility. And these consequences are false, so the statement that the soul is identical to this body, or an organ of it, is false. So we need, in establishing this proof, to establish premises: The first premise: In explaining that if the soul were this body, or an organ of its organs, then the statement of resurrection and standing would be false. Its explanation: We observe that this body, with all its organs and parts, disperses and disintegrates and becomes perishing.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof that the soul is neither the body nor an organ of it. The argument is structured as a conditional with false consequences: if the soul were the body or an organ, then bodily resurrection (الحشر والنشر) and reward and punishment (الثواب والعقاب) would be impossible. This would make the human being the lowest of animals and creation futile. Since these consequences are false (resurrection and reward are affirmed in religion), the premise that the soul is the body or an organ must be false. The author then states that to establish this proof, several premises are needed. He begins with the first premise: that if the soul were the body or an organ, resurrection would be false. The explanation: we observe that the body decays and perishes entirely, so if the soul were identical to it, there would be no persisting subject for resurrection or reward/punishment. The proof continues beyond this page, but the core of the argument is clear: the soul's persistence after bodily death is necessary for eschatological events.

Key Terms
حشر (hashr)
نشر (nashr)
ثواب (thawab)
عقاب ('iqab)
بعث (ba'th)
قيامة (qiyama)
بدن (badan)
نفس (nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that bodily resurrection requires the soul to be material; this argument shows the soul must be non-material to persist after bodily decay.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that if the soul were material (body/organ), resurrection would be impossible because the body perishes. Therefore, the soul must be non-material to survive death and be the subject of resurrection.

Checkpoint
What does the tenth proof consider a necessary consequence of the soul being identical to the body?
A) The soul would be immortal.
B) Resurrection and reward/punishment would be impossible.
C) The soul would be non-bodily.
D) The human being would become an angel.

Key Takeaway
The soul must be non-material and persist after bodily death, otherwise the Islamic doctrines of resurrection and divine justice would be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul were just your body, what happens to your accountability after death? Do you truly believe you will be judged for actions beyond this life? How does that change how you live today?
?
The proof says if soul were body, creation would be futile. If you live as if only this body matters, are you not in practice denying the resurrection? What would it mean for you to live today in light of the soul's persistence?
?
When you sin, do you ever think 'it's just my body, not my real self'? How does the truth that you are a soul using a body affect your sense of responsibility in this moment?

The Proof from Possessive Attribution
The author enumerates the proof for the soul's distinction using possessive attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the valid attribution of body parts to oneself proves that the soul is distinct from the body.

Core Idea
Attributing body parts to 'me' makes sense only if 'me' is not the body.

This lesson presents a powerful argument from linguistic attribution. When we say 'my heart,' the possessive implies a distinct owner. The author argues that since attributing a thing to itself is impossible, the soul must be other than the body and its organs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author starts by stating that the attribution of body parts to oneself is among the most evident truths.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof has two premises: (1) attribution of organs to 'I' is valid by scripture and rational intuition, (2) attribution of a thing to itself is invalid by rational intuition.
Write them down in your own words.
3
See how the conclusion follows
From these two premises, it follows that the 'I' to which the organs are attributed must be distinct from them. This 'I' is the soul.
Reflect on why this argument is considered 'most manifest'.
4
Consider a potential objection
If you think of possible objections, hold them for the next lesson. This passage is the foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقلبي كذا كذا، ودماغي كذا [وأذني كذا وسمعي، وبصري كذا وكذا] وكل من نازع في صحة هذه الإضافة فقد نازع في أظهر العلوم وأكملها وأجلاها. وأما المقام الثالث: وهو أن هذا يدل على أن النفس غير البدن، وغير كل واحد من هذه الأعضاء: فالدليل عليه: إن هذه الإضافة صحيحة بمقتضى القرآن، والخبر، وبمقتضى بديهة العقل. وأما إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه فإنها باطلة بمقتضى بديهة العقل. ويلزم من مجموع هاتين المقدمتين: أن الشيء الذي أضيفت كل هذه الأعضاء إليه، إضافة الملكية، يكون مغايراً لها، وذلك يقتضي أن تكون النفس مغايرة للبدن ومغايرة لجميع أعضائه وأقسامه. فإن قيل: فهذا أيضاً وارد عليكم، لأنه يصح أن يقال: روحي ونفسي وذاتي وحقيقتي، وهذا يقتضي أن تكون [نفسه] مغايرة لنفسه، وأن تكون ذاته مغايرة لذاته. وإنه محال. والجواب: أما قوله: «نفسي» فهذا يقتضي حصول التغاير بين المضاف والمضاف إليه، فحملنا المضاف على البدن، وحملنا المضاف إليه على تلك الذات المخصوصة، المشار إليها بقوله: «أنا»، وحينئذ تصح هذه الإضافة. وأما قوله: «روحي» فيحمل الروح على هذه البخارات المخصوصة، إما في القلب، وإما في الدماغ. وحينئذ تصح الإضافة. وأما قوله: «ذاتي وحقيقتي» فنقول: لا شك أن الذوات والحقائق متشاركة بأسرها في كونها ذوات وحقائق، ومتخالفة بكون كل واحدة منها تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة، فقد حصلت المغايرة من هذا الوجه، فلا جرم كفت هذه المغايرة في صحة هذه الإضافة. أما لو كانت النفس عبارة عن مجموع هذا البدن، أو عن عضو مخصوص، فحينئذ كانت تلك النفس عين ذلك البدن، وذلك البدن عين تلك النفس، فكان قوله: «بدني وقلبي» يوجب إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه بالاعتبار الواحد. وذلك محال. فظهر الفرق بين البابين. والله أعلم.
And 'my heart such and such, and my brain such, [and my ear such and my hearing, and my sight such and such].' And whoever disputes the validity of this attribution has disputed the most manifest, most complete, and most evident of sciences. As for the third point: that this indicates that the soul is other than the body, and other than each one of these organs. The proof of it: that this attribution is valid by the requirement of the Qur'an, the tradition, and by the requirement of the immediate intuition of the intellect. As for the attribution of a thing to itself, it is invalid by the requirement of immediate intuition. And from the combination of these two premises it follows that the thing to which all these organs are attributed, by way of possessive attribution, is distinct from them, and that requires that the soul be distinct from the body and distinct from all its organs and parts. If it is said: 'This also applies to you, because it is valid to say: my spirit, my self, my essence, and my reality, and this requires that [his self] be distinct from his self, and that his essence be distinct from his essence, and that is impossible.' The answer: As for his saying 'my self,' that requires the occurrence of distinctness between the attributed and the attributee; so we interpret the attributed as the body, and the attributee as that particular essence indicated by his saying 'I,' and then this attribution is valid. As for his saying 'my spirit,' it is interpreted as referring to these particular vapors, either in the heart or in the brain, and then the attribution is valid. As for his saying 'my essence and my reality,' we say: there is no doubt that essences and realities all share in being essences and realities, and differ in that each one is that particular reality, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ; so distinctness has occurred from this aspect, and thus this distinctness suffices for the validity of this attribution. But if the soul were the sum total of this body, or a particular organ, then that soul would be identical to that body, and that body identical to that soul, so his saying 'my body and my heart' would necessitate the attribution of a thing to itself in the same respect, and that is impossible. So the distinction between the two cases becomes clear. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the soul's distinction from the body, followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins by noting that the valid attribution of body parts to 'me' (e.g., 'my heart,' 'my brain') is among the most evident truths. The third point is that this valid attribution proves the soul is not identical to the body or any organ. The proof has two premises: (1) The attribution of organs to oneself is valid by scripture and rational intuition; (2) The attribution of a thing to itself is invalid by rational intuition. From these it follows that the subject to which the organs are attributed (the 'me') must be distinct from them—thus the soul (nafs) is distinct from the body and all its parts. An objection is raised: the same reasoning would apply to phrases like 'my self' (nafsī) or 'my essence' (dhātī), which would require a thing to be distinct from itself, an impossibility. The author answers by clarifying each term. For 'my self' (nafsī), the attribution is between the body (the attributed) and the particular self indicated by 'I' (the attributee), so distinctness holds. For 'my spirit' (rūḥī), 'spirit' refers to specific vapors in the heart or brain, so again it is not self-attribution. For 'my essence' (dhātī) and 'my reality' (ḥaqīqatī), the shared aspect (being an essence) differs from the particular aspect (the specific essence), so distinctness is present. Finally, the author shows the consequence if the soul were identical to the body: then saying 'my body' would be self-attribution, which is impossible. Thus the distinction is clear.

Key Terms
النفس
البدن
الإضافة
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possessive language like 'my heart' is merely figurative, not a logical proof.
Correction: The author contends that such attribution is among the most evident truths (azhar al-ulum), and its validity is affirmed by scripture and rational intuition.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise of the proof that the soul is distinct from the body?
A) The soul is immaterial.
B) Attributing a thing to itself is invalid.
C) The body is composed of organs.
D) The Quran mentions the soul.

Key Takeaway
The logical structure of possessive attribution provides a rational proof for the soul's distinction from the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'my body' or 'my brain,' do you truly feel that these belong to you, or do you identify with them? How does this affect your understanding of who you are beyond your physical form?
?
If your soul is distinct from your body, then your true identity is not your age, health, or appearance. What does this mean for how you view aging, illness, and death? Are you living as if your soul is the real 'you'?
?
The proof relies on the impossibility of self-attribution. Have you ever falsely attributed something to yourself that is not truly you—like your status, possessions, or even your flaws? What is one thing you need to stop claiming as your identity so you can focus on your soul?

Defending the Proof: Objection and Answer
The author responds to an objection and provides three clarifications to defend the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from self-attribution is answered and why it does not undermine the proof.

Core Idea
Saying 'my self' also implies distinctness between body and soul upon careful analysis.

An opponent might argue that phrases like 'my self' involve self-attribution, which would contradict the proof's premise. The author responds by showing that in each case, there is a genuine distinction between the attributed and the attributee.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection and the answer. The objection claims that phrases like 'my self' would require a thing to be distinct from itself, an impossibility.
2
Examine each answer
The author gives three answers: for 'my self,' 'my spirit,' and 'my essence.' Understand each interpretation.
Notice how the author consistently finds a real distinction.
3
Consider the consequence
If the soul were identical to the body, then 'my body' would be self-attribution, which is impossible. This reinforces the proof.
The final paragraph makes the distinction clear.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Now you have a complete picture: the proof is sound, and the objection is resolved by careful analysis of language.
