Mutual Influence of Soul and Body
Al-Razi argues for mutual soul-body influence using choleric temper example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the example of choleric temperament to demonstrate the mutual causal influence between the soul and the body.

Core Idea
The soul and body continuously affect each other—anger can be both a result of and a cause of bodily bile.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a classic philosophical argument for the interaction between the soul and the body. He uses a simple, observable phenomenon: the relationship between the choleric temperament (dominance of yellow bile) and the emotion of anger. This example illustrates a two-way causal connection, challenging any view that sees the soul and body as entirely separate or that causation flows only one way.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Note the structure: a general statement followed by an example and a conclusion.
2
Identify the two parts of the example
Al-Razi gives two observations: (1) those with choleric temperament get angry often, (2) those who get angry often accumulate yellow bile. Notice the bidirectional causality.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that each affects the other. This is the key point: mutual influence.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the quiz question to confirm you grasp the example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مزاج الأخلاط في النفس، مساوي لفعل اختلاف النفس (۱)، وتفسيره: إن من كان الغالب عليه هو المزاج الصفراوي، فإنه يغضب كثيراً وأيضاً: إذا غضب مراراً كثيرة، غضباً شديداً، فإنه يجتمع في بدنه شيئاً كثيراً من الصفراء. وهذا يدل على أن النفس والبدن، كل واحد منهما مؤثر في الآخر.
The mixture of humors in the soul corresponds to the action of the soul's variation (1). Its explanation: whoever has the choleric temperament dominant over him becomes angry a lot, and also if he becomes angry many times with intense anger, a large amount of yellow bile accumulates in his body. And this indicates that the soul and the body each affect the other.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents an argument demonstrating the mutual influence between the soul and the body. Al-Razi begins with a general statement: the mixture of humors (mizaj al-akhlat) in the soul corresponds to the soul's emotional variation. He then explains by giving an example: if a person has a dominant choleric temperament (al-mizaj al-safrawi), he becomes angry frequently. Additionally, if he becomes angry many times with intense anger, a large amount of yellow bile (safra') accumulates in his body. From this, al-Razi concludes that the soul and the body each affect the other. The argument is based on observable correlation: the body's humoral state affects the soul's emotion (anger), and the soul's emotion (anger) in turn affects the body by producing more bile. This is a classic proof in philosophical psychology for the interaction between the two substances.

Key Terms
مزاج
اختلاف النفس
الصفراوي
غضب
النفس
البدن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul and body only affect each other in one direction.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly argues for mutual influence: the body's temperament affects the soul's emotions, and the soul's emotions affect the body's humors.

Checkpoint
What example does al-Razi give to show that the soul and body affect each other?
A) The melancholic temperament causes sadness.
B) The choleric temperament causes frequent and intense anger, and anger increases yellow bile.
C) The phlegmatic temperament causes laziness.
D) The sanguine temperament causes cheerfulness.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses the choleric temperament and anger to show that the soul and body continuously influence each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel anger rising, do you tend to blame it on external triggers, physical fatigue, or even your 'personality'? How does this passage’s proof of mutual influence challenge you to take ownership of your inner state and its physical effects? What is one concrete change you can make today to address a recurring anger pattern?
?
This passage shows that a choleric temperament leads to anger, and anger in turn produces more choler (bile). Is there a habitual emotion or reaction in your life that has created a similar self-reinforcing cycle? What would it look like to interrupt that cycle at the level of both soul and body?

The Second Brain Cause: Movement of the Worm-Like Body
Explains how the worm-like body's movement affects speed of understanding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the movement of the worm-like body in the brain affects the speed of understanding according to classical Islamic physiology.

Core Idea
The worm-like body's movement controls the flow of spirit and determines quickness of mind.

This lesson examines a fascinating classical theory that links physical brain structures to mental abilities. The author describes a worm-like body that opens and closes a passage between brain cavities, allowing spiritual substances to carry mental images. By understanding this mechanism, you will see how medieval scholars connected temperament, brain anatomy, and cognitive speed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the cause-and-effect structure.
The passage is a continuous explanation.
2
Identify the two states of the worm-like body
The passage describes two states: extension (opens the passage) and contraction (blocks the passage). Understand the consequence of each.
3
Analyze the temperament connection
The author correlates the substance of the worm-like body (thick/cold vs. sharp/hot) with its movement speed, which then affects the person's quickness of understanding.
Focus on the 'if...if' structure.
4
Reflect on the theory's context
Consider this as a pre-modern physiological explanation using humoral theory. How does it compare to modern neuroscience?
The worm-like body is not literal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخرى. فإذا امتد ودق، انفتح المجرى الذي بين التجويف المقدم وبين التجويف المتوسط، فنفذت الروح من التجويف المقدم إلى التجويف المتوسط، وتأدت الصور المتخيلة إلى القوة الفكرية. وأما إذا انقبض هذا الجسم الشبيه بالدودة، فحينئذ يقصر ويغلظ ويسد المجرى، فلم تنفذ الروح من التجويف المقدم إلى التجويف المتوسط، فحينئذ يمتنع وصول الصور الخيالية إلى القوة المفكرة. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: حركة هذا الجسم الشبيه بالدودة في الامتداد والانقباض مختلفة في الناس على قدر أمزجة الأدمغة. فإن كان جوهره غليظاً بارداً، كانت حركته بطيئة فلم ينفتح المجرى بسرعة، فلم تتأد الروح من التجويف المقدم إلى التجويف المتوسط بسرعة، فيكون هذا الإنسان بطيء الفهم قليل الفطنة متأخر الجواب. وإن كان هذا الجسم سريع الحركة حادها، كان هذا الإنسان سريع الفطنة سريع الجواب حاد الفهم.
Another [cause]. When it extends and becomes thin, the passage between the anterior cavity and the middle cavity opens, and the spirit (al-rūḥ) passes from the anterior cavity to the middle cavity, and the imagined forms (al-ṣuwar al-mutakhayyala) reach the intellectual faculty (al-quwwa al-fikriyya). But when this worm-like body contracts, it shortens and thickens and blocks the passage, so the spirit does not pass from the anterior cavity to the middle cavity, and thus the arrival of the imaginative forms (al-ṣuwar al-khayāliyya) to the thinking faculty (al-quwwa al-mufakkirat) is prevented. Now that you know this, we say: The movement of this worm-like body in extension and contraction differs among people according to the temperaments of their brains. If its substance is thick and cold, its movement is slow, so the passage does not open quickly, and the spirit does not pass quickly from the anterior cavity to the middle cavity, so this person is slow of understanding, lacking in intelligence, slow in answering. If this body is quick in movement and sharp, then this person is quick in intelligence, quick in answering, sharp of understanding.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage explains the second brain cause that affects the speed of human understanding. The author describes a physical mechanism involving a 'worm-like body' inside the brain that controls the passage of spirit between the anterior and middle cavities. When this body extends, it opens a passage for the spirit to carry imagined forms to the intellectual faculty; when it contracts, it blocks the passage. The speed of this movement depends on the brain's temperament—whether the substance of the worm-like body is thick and cold (slow) or sharp and hot (fast). This directly correlates to quickness of understanding, intelligence, and speed of response. The passage does not name the first cause but implies it was discussed earlier.

Key Terms
الجسم الشبيه بالدودة
الروح
القوة الفكرية
القوة المفكرة
التجويف المقدم
التجويف المتوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The worm-like body is an actual worm.
Correction: It is a theoretical anatomical structure in classical physiology, not a real worm.

Checkpoint
What happens when the worm-like body contracts?
A) The spirit passes quickly to the intellectual faculty.
B) The passage is blocked and the spirit cannot pass.
C) The imagined forms are sharpened.
D) The person becomes quick of understanding.

Key Takeaway
The speed of the worm-like body's movement, influenced by brain temperament, determines a person's quickness of understanding and response.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Skull Shape and Brain Functions
Describes skull shape effects on imagination and memory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars linked skull shape to the faculties of imagination and memory.

Core Idea
The shape of the skull mirrors the faculties of the brain.

This passage from Al-Matalibul Aliyah explores how the external shape of the skull corresponds to internal brain functions. The author describes an ideal skull shape and explains how deviations affect imagination and memory. This lesson unpacks that relationship, grounding it in classical physiological psychology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the author’s description of the ideal skull shape and the specific protrusions mentioned.
The author uses a very concrete analogy: a sphere pressed from the sides.
2
Identify the two protrusions
Identify the front protrusion (نتوء القدام) and the back protrusion (نتوء الخلف). Understand that each is linked to a distinct mental faculty.
Imagination (التخيل) is linked to the front; memory (التذكر) to the back.
3
Analyze the cause-effect relationships
The passage states what happens when each protrusion is lost. Summarize the consequences: loss of front -> weak imagination; loss of back -> weak memory; loss of both -> loss of both faculties.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this theory is important in the context of classical Islamic scholarship. How does it integrate anatomy with psychology? Consider that this reflects Galenic and Avicennan influences.
This shows an early attempt to localize brain functions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبب الثالث من الأسباب الدماغية: شكل قحف الرأس، فإن الشكل الجيد أن يكون القحف شبيهاً بكرة غمز عليها من الجانبين، حتى يظهر نتوء في القدام وفي الخلف، ولطاء من الجانبين. فهذا الشكل موافق لحال الدماغ. فأما إذا فقد نتوء القدام، ضعف التخيل. وإن فقد نتوء الخلف ضعف التذكر. وإن فقد النتوءان جميعاً، فقد التخيل والتذكر.
The third of the brain causes: the shape of the skull. The good shape is that the skull resembles a sphere pressed from the two sides, such that a protrusion appears in the front and in the back, and flatness from the two sides. This shape is in accordance with the state of the brain. But if the front protrusion is lost, imagination (al-takhayyul) weakens. If the back protrusion is lost, memory (al-tadhakkur) weakens. If both protrusions are lost, imagination and memory are lost.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the third brain cause: the shape of the skull. The author describes the ideal shape as spherical but slightly compressed on the sides, producing protrusions at the front and back. This shape aligns with the brain's natural configuration. Each protrusion corresponds to a mental faculty: the front protrusion is linked to imagination, the back to memory. Loss of either protrusion weakens the respective faculty; loss of both eliminates both. The passage treats imagination and memory as distinct faculties localized in different brain regions, reflecting classical physiological psychology.

Key Terms
قحف الرأس
نتوء القدام
نتوء الخلف
التخيل
التذكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Memory weakens.
B) Imagination weakens.
C) Both imagination and memory are lost.
D) The brain becomes inflamed.

Key Takeaway
Classical scholars linked skull shape to specific brain faculties: the front protrusion supports imagination, the back supports memory.

The Fourth Brain Cause: Sizes of Head and Body
Enumerates four head-body size combinations and their effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relative sizes of the head and body affect temperament, intelligence, and the strength of the heart and brain according to this classical theory.

Core Idea
Head and body size ratios determine temperamental perfection or deficiency.

In this passage, the author discusses the fourth brain cause: the relationship between head and body size. He outlines four possible combinations and their effects on heart and brain strength, influencing rashness, anger, and understanding. This classification helps explain differences in human temperament and perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the four distinct cases the author enumerates.
The passage is a clear classification, so pay attention to each case's wording.
2
Identify the four combinations
List the four possibilities: both large, both small, head small and body large, head large and body small.
The author uses a logical structure of 'either...or' to cover all possibilities.
3
Note the effects of each combination
For each case, note the state of the heart and brain, and the resulting temperament or cognitive outcome.
Observe how the balance between heart and brain is key.
4
Consider the link to soul's perceptions
The passage concludes that these four are the temperamental causes that necessitate differences in how souls perceive.
This ties physical causes to psychological differences.
5
Reflect on the concept of 'perfection'
Note that 'perfection' here refers to an optimal temperament, not moral or spiritual perfection.
This is a common misunderstanding to avoid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبب الرابع: الرأس والبدن. إما أن يكونا كبيرين أو صغيرين، أو يكون الرأس صغيراً والبدن كبيراً، أو بالعكس. أما الأول وهو أن يكون الرأس والبدن كبيرين، فحينئذ يكون القلب قوياً والدماغ قوياً. وههنا يحصل الكمال. وأما إن كانا صغيرين فههنا يحصل النقصان. وأما إن كان الرأس صغيراً والبدن كبيراً، فحينئذ يكون القلب قوياً حاداً ويكون الدماغ صغيراً لا يقوى على مقاومة القلب. فحينئذ يكثر الطيش والغضب. وأما إذا كانت الرأس كبيرة والبدن صغيراً، فحينئذ يكون كبر تلك بسبب الرطوبات الفصلية، وهذا الإنسان يكون قليل الفهم ضعيف القلب مختل الأفعال. فهذه الوجوه الأربعة هي الأسباب المزاجية الموجبة لاختلاف حال النفوس في كيفية الإدراكات.
The fourth cause: the head and the body. They are either both large or both small, or the head is small and the body large, or the opposite. As for the first—that the head and body are both large—then the heart is strong and the brain is strong. Here perfection (al-kamāl) is attained. If they are both small, then deficiency (al-nuqṣān) is attained. If the head is small and the body large, then the heart is strong and sharp, and the brain is small, unable to resist the heart. Thus rashness and anger increase. If the head is large and the body small, then its largeness is due to superfluous moistures, and this person is of little understanding, weak of heart, confused in actions. So these four aspects are the temperamental causes that necessitate the differences in the states of souls (al-nufūs) regarding the manner of perceptions (al-idrākāt).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth brain cause: the relative sizes of the head (brain) and body, which influence heart strength and brain capacity. The author lists four possible combinations. When both are large, both heart and brain are strong, leading to perfection. When both are small, deficiency results. A small head with a large body makes the heart dominant, causing rashness and anger. A large head with a small body indicates excess moisture, leading to poor understanding, weak heart, and confused actions. The passage concludes that these four causes (the second, third, fourth, plus an unstated first) are the temperamental factors explaining differences in how souls perceive. This conclusion ties the physical causes to psychological differences.

Key Terms
القلب
الدماغ
الكمال
النقصان
الطيش
الغضب
الأسباب المزاجية
الإدراكات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'perfection' (al-kamāl) refers to moral or spiritual perfection.
Correction: In this context, 'perfection' means an optimal temperamental balance, not moral excellence.

Checkpoint
Which combination leads to rashness and anger according to the passage?
A) Both head and body large.
B) Both head and body small.
C) Head small and body large.
D) Head large and body small.

Key Takeaway
The four combinations of head and body size are temperamental causes that lead to different states of the soul in perception.

?

The Animal Heart Power and the Restraint of Reason
Heart's animal power, chambers, and reason's moderating role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the animal heart power, its effects on emotions and character traits, and how human reason restrains it.

Core Idea
The heart's heat shapes our emotions; reason tames them.

This passage from the Matalib al-Aliyah explores the second major power within the human being: the animal heart power. It explains how this power generates emotions and influences traits like courage and cowardice. The author highlights the crucial role of reason in restraining these natural impulses, distinguishing humans from animals.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then read the translation. Notice the structural parallel with the brain's ventricles.
2
Understand the three chambers and spiritual bodies
Identify the left chamber's function: generating subtle spiritual bodies (spirits) that carry life and innate heat.
3
Analyze the three emotional states
See how the degree of innate heat produces recklessness, cowardice, or courage. The passage also extends this to activity/laziness and speed/slowness.
4
Contrast humans and beasts
Observe that beasts have the same power but lack rational restraint, while humans have reason to moderate their impulses.
5
Reflect on ethical implications
Consider how this framework explains moral responsibility: we can control our innate tendencies through reason.
This connects to broader Islamic ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني وهو القوة الحيوانية القلبية: فاعلم أنه كما حصل في الدماغ بطون ثلاثة فكذلك حصل في القلب بطون ثلاثة. فالبطن الأيسر منه يتولد فيه أجسام لطيفة روحانية تسري في الشريانات إلى أقاصي البدن، وتلك الأرواح تفيد الأعضاء قوة الحياة والحرارة الغريزية. وبهذه القوة يحصل الفرح والغم والغضب. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: هذه الحرارة إن كانت قوية حصل التهور. وإن كانت ناقصة حصل الجبن. وإن كانت معتدلة حصلت الشجاعة التي هي الصفة المحمودة. وهكذا القول في النشاط والكسل، والسرعة والإبطاء في الأفعال والأقوال. واعلم: أن هذه القوة حاصلة للسباع، إلا أنها خالية عن القوة النطقية المانعة، فلا جرم إذا حصلت هذه الأحوال للسباع، أقدمت على الفعل بأقصى الوجوه. أما الإنسان فله العقل الوازع من مقتضيات الطبيعة.
As for the second division—the animal heart power (al-quwwa al-ḥayawāniyya al-qalbiyya): Know that just as the brain has three ventricles, so too the heart has three chambers. In its left chamber are generated subtle spiritual bodies that flow through the arteries to the extremities of the body, and those spirits (al-arwāḥ) give the organs the power of life (quwwa al-ḥayāt) and innate heat (al-ḥarāra al-gharīziyya). With this power, joy, grief, and anger occur. Now that you know this, we say: If this heat is strong, recklessness (al-tahawwur) occurs; if it is deficient, cowardice (al-jubn) occurs; if it is moderate, courage (al-shajāʿa)—which is the praiseworthy quality—occurs. The same holds for activity and laziness, quickness and slowness in actions and words. Know that this power exists in beasts, but they lack the rational power (al-quwwa al-nuṭqiyya) that restrains. Hence, when these states occur in beasts, they act to the utmost degree. As for man, he has reason (al-ʿaql) that restrains from the demands of nature.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage describes the second major power: the animal heart power. The author draws a parallel between the brain's three ventricles and the heart's three chambers. The left chamber generates subtle spiritual bodies (spirits) that travel through arteries, giving life and innate heat to organs. This power is the basis of emotions like joy, grief, and anger. The degree of heat determines traits: strong heat causes recklessness, deficient heat causes cowardice, moderate heat yields courage. The same applies to activity vs. laziness and speed vs. slowness. The author emphasizes that beasts also have this power but lack rational power to restrain it, so they act on impulse. Humans, however, possess reason (ʿaql) that restrains natural impulses. This highlights the importance of rational control over emotional drives, a key theme in Islamic ethics.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The animal heart power is the same as the physical heart or involves literal animal spirits.
Correction: It is a technical term for the vital faculty of the heart that generates subtle spiritual bodies (arwāḥ) responsible for emotions and innate heat, not literal animals.

Checkpoint

D) The spiritual bodies.

Key Takeaway
The animal heart power drives emotions and traits; human reason restrains it, enabling ethical action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Think of a time you acted impulsively without restraint. What would it mean for you to train your reason to hold the reins of your animal heart power? What is one practical step you can take today to strengthen that restraint?

The Animal-Heart Power and the Temperament of Character
Explains the animal-heart power and its effects on character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the heart's natural heat, in interaction with the brain, determines character traits like courage and cowardice according to Islamic philosophical psychology.

Core Idea
Character is not just choice—it is shaped by the body's inner heat.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah delves into the second faculty: the animal-heart power. The author explains that just as the brain has ventricles, so does the heart, and that the left ventricle generates subtle spirits carrying life and heat. By examining the intensity of this heat, we can understand the roots of traits like courage and cowardice, and how the rational intellect can moderate them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's logical flow from description to application.
2
Identify the heart's structure and function
Note the three ventricles analogy and the generation of spiritual bodies in the left ventricle. Understand how this gives life and natural heat.
This sets the foundation for the character discussion.
3
Connect heat levels to character traits
The author states: strong heat → recklessness, deficient heat → cowardice, moderate heat → courage. Think about how this applies to other pairs like activity/laziness.
4
Contrast humans and beasts
Beasts have the same power but lack restraining intellect, so they act without limit. Humans have intellect to moderate impulses.
This highlights the role of reason.
5
Analyze heart-brain interaction
When both heart and brain are balanced, actions are optimal. Imbalance causes extremes. Note the special cases: hot heart with balanced brain, cold heart with balanced brain.
This shows the interplay between temperament and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني وهو القوة الحيوانية القلبية : فاعلم أنه كما حصل في الدماغ بطون ثلاثة فكذلك حصل في القلب بطون ثلاثة فالبطن الأيسر منه يتولد فيه أجسام لطيفة روحانية ، تسري في الشريانات ، إلى أقاصي البدن ، وتلك الأرواح تفيد الأعضاء قوة الحياة ، والحرارة الغريزية . وبهذه القوة يحصل الفرح والغم والغضب . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : هذه الحرارة. إن كانت قوية حصل التهور . وإن كانت ناقصة حصل الجبن ، وإن كانت معتدلة حصلت الشجاعة التي هي الصفة المحمودة ، وهكذا القول في النشاط والكسل ، والسرعة والإبطاء في الأفعال والأقوال . واعلم : أن هذه القوة حاصلة للسباع ، إلا أنها خالية عن القوة النطقية المانعة ، فلا جرم إذا حصلت هذه الأحوال للسباع، أقدمت على الفعل بأقصى الوجوه . أما الإنسان فله العقل الوازع من مقتضيات الطبيعة . وإذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : إنه إذا كان القلب والدماغ معتدلين ، حصلت هذه الأفعال على أصوب الوجوه وأحسنها، خالية عن الزيادة والنقصان . وإن كانا غير معتدلين. فإن كان ذلك لأجل استيلاء الحر عليهما : عظم الطيش والشطط، وإن كان بسبب استيلاء البرد عليهما عظم الجبن والخوف وقلت الحمية. وأما إن كان القلب قوي الحرارة، وكان الدماغ معتدلاً، فعند استيلاء الغضب على القلب، فإن الدماغ المعتدل يمنعه عن الإفراط في العمل، وإما إن كان القلب ضعيف الحرارة ، فصاحبه يكون جباناً . إلا أنه إذا كان الدماغ معتدلاً ، فالعقل قد يحمله على أفعال الشجاعة ، وإن كان الجبن حاصلاً في القلب .
As for the second division, which is the animal-heart power: Know that just as there are three ventricles in the brain, so there are three ventricles in the heart. In its left ventricle, subtle spiritual bodies are generated, which flow through the arteries to the extremities of the body. These spirits give the organs the power of life and natural heat. By this power, joy, grief, and anger occur. If you know this, we say: This heat—if it is strong, recklessness results; if it is deficient, cowardice results; if it is moderate, courage, which is the praiseworthy quality, results. The same applies to activity and laziness, swiftness and slowness in actions and words. Know that this power exists in beasts, except that they lack the restraining rational power. So when these states occur in beasts, they act to the utmost degree. As for humans, they have the restraining intellect from the demands of nature. If you know this, we say: If the heart and brain are balanced, these actions occur in the most correct and best ways, free from excess and deficiency. If they are unbalanced, if that is due to the predominance of heat over them, recklessness and extremeness become great; if due to the predominance of cold over them, cowardice and fear become great and zeal diminishes. If the heart is strong in heat and the brain is balanced, then when anger overwhelms the heart, the balanced brain prevents it from excess in action. If the heart is weak in heat, its owner is cowardly. However, if the brain is balanced, the intellect may drive him to acts of courage, even if cowardice resides in the heart.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage classifies and explains the second faculty, the animal-heart power (al-quwwa al-hayawaniyya al-qalbiyya). The author begins by noting that just as the brain has three ventricles, so does the heart. The left ventricle generates subtle spiritual bodies that travel through arteries, giving organs life and natural heat. This power is the seat of emotions like joy, grief, and anger. The author then correlates the intensity of the heart's heat with character traits: strong heat leads to recklessness (tahawwur), deficient heat to cowardice (jubn), and moderate heat to courage (shuja'a), which is praiseworthy. The same analysis applies to other paired traits like activity/laziness and speed/slowness. Next, the author contrasts humans and beasts: beasts possess this power but lack the rational power that restrains impulses, so they act without limit. Humans have the restraining intellect (al-'aql al-wazi'). The discussion then turns to the interaction between heart and brain temperaments. When both are balanced, actions are optimal. Imbalance due to heat causes recklessness; due to cold causes cowardice. A special case: if the heart is hot but the brain balanced, the brain moderates angry impulses. If the heart is cold (cowardly) but the brain balanced, the intellect can still compel courageous actions despite the heart's disposition.

Correction: This passage shows that the heart's natural heat significantly shapes tendencies toward recklessness, cowardice, or courage, though the intellect can moderate these.

The Appetitive-Liver Power and the Causes of Hunger
Defines the appetitive faculty and explains the physiological causes of hunger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physiological explanation of hunger according to this classical text, including the role of black bile, the spleen-stomach duct, and how body and stomach size affect appetite.

Core Idea
Hunger is a chemical sensation from black bile flowing to the stomach, not just emptiness.

In this passage, the author moves from defining the appetitive faculty to a detailed physiological account of hunger. He explains that hunger arises from a specific mechanism involving natural black bile flowing from the spleen to the stomach mouth. The intensity of hunger depends on body size, stomach capacity, and duct width.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument about hunger.
2
Identify the three factors affecting hunger
Look for the three variables the author lists: body size, stomach size, and duct width. Notice how each combination leads to greater or lesser hunger.
3
Understand the role of black bile and the duct
The author emphasizes that hunger is caused by black bile flowing from the spleen to the stomach mouth via a duct, creating a stinging sensation. This is the core mechanism.
4
Reflect on the classical perspective
Consider how this explanation differs from modern understanding of hunger. Note the reliance on humoral theory (black bile) and anatomical connections.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثالث : وهو القوة الشهوانية الكبدية : فاعلم أن الشهوة إما في المأكول والمشروب ، أو في الفرج. أما الشهوة في المأكول والمشروب . فنقول : البدن ، إما أن يكون كبيراً ، وإما أن يكون صغيراً ، وكذلك المعدة إما أن تكون كبيرة أو صغيرة ، وأيضاً : فإن الإحساس بالجوع إنما يحصل بسبب أن بين الطحال، وبين فم المعدة مجرى ينصب فيه قسط من السوداء الطبيعية إلى فم المعدة ، فيحصل هناك لذع ودغدغة . وذلك هو الإحساس بالجوع . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : ذلك المجرى إما أن يكون واسعاً أو ضيقاً . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن كان البدن كبيراً ، والمعدة كبيرة ، وكان ذلك المجرى واسعاً ، فإنه يكثر جوع هذا الإنسان ، لأن البدن الكبير يتحلل منه أجزاء كثيرة ، والمعدة الكبيرة متسعة للغذاء الكثير ، والسوداء الكثيرة المنصبة في ذلك المجرى الواسع توجب الإحساس العظيم بالجوع ، فلما اجتمعت هذه الأسباب ، عظم الجوع . فهذا الإنسان يأكل مقداراً كبيراً في المرة الواحدة ، ويكفيه ذلك زمان مديداً ، وإما إن كان البدن صغيراً ، والمعدة صغيرة ، والمجرى ضيقاً ، فههنا يقل الجوع . لأن البدن لما كان صغيراً ، كان المتحلل منه قليلا ، ولما كانت المعدة صغيرة لم تتسع للطعام الكثير ، ولما كان المجرى ضيقاً لم ينصب إلى فم المعدة إلا القليل من السوداء ، فعند اجتماع هذه الأسباب يقل الجوع .
The third division: the appetitive-liver power. Know that appetite is either in food and drink, or in the genitals. As for appetite in food and drink, we say: The body is either large or small, and similarly the stomach is either large or small. Also, the sensation of hunger occurs only because between the spleen and the mouth of the stomach there is a duct through which a portion of natural black bile flows to the mouth of the stomach, causing a stinging and tickling sensation there; that is the sensation of hunger. If you know this, we say: That duct is either wide or narrow. And if you know this, we say: If the body is large, the stomach large, and that duct wide, then this person's hunger increases, because the large body has many parts that break down, the large stomach can hold much food, and the abundant black bile flowing into that wide duct causes a great sensation of hunger. When these causes combine, hunger becomes great. So this person eats a large amount at one time, and it suffices him for a long period. If the body is small, the stomach small, and the duct narrow, then hunger decreases. Because when the body is small, what breaks down from it is little; when the stomach is small, it cannot hold much food; and when the duct is narrow, only a little black bile flows to the mouth of the stomach. So when these causes combine, hunger decreases.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the third faculty, the appetitive-liver power (al-quwwa al-shahwaniyya al-kabidiyya). The author first divides appetite into two types: for food/drink and for sexual intercourse. He then focuses on the appetite for food and drink and provides a physiological explanation of hunger. The sensation of hunger arises from the flow of natural black bile (al-sawda' al-tabi'iyya) from the spleen to the mouth of the stomach via a duct, causing a stinging/tickling sensation. The intensity of hunger depends on three factors: body size, stomach size, and the width of the duct. A large body has more tissue breaking down, a large stomach can accommodate more food, and a wide duct allows more black bile to flow—all leading to greater hunger. Conversely, a small body, small stomach, and narrow duct lead to less hunger. The passage concludes by noting that a person with these large features eats a large meal that suffices for a long time, while the opposite condition leads to small appetites.

الشهوة
المأكول والمشروب
الإحساس بالجوع
السوداء الطبيعية
المجرى
المعدة
الطحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hunger is caused by an empty stomach.
Correction: The author explains hunger arises from black bile flowing to the stomach mouth via a duct from the spleen, not just emptiness.

Checkpoint

A) An empty stomach
B) Heat in the liver
C) Flow of black bile to the stomach mouth
D) Contraction of the diaphragm

Key Takeaway
The intensity of hunger is determined by the combination of body size, stomach size, and width of the duct carrying black bile to the stomach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel hungry, do you ever pause to consider the intricate mechanism Allah designed—the duct, the bile, the body size—that triggers this sensation, or do you treat hunger as a mere inconvenience to be silenced?

?
If your appetite differs from others, do you become envious or impatient, forgetting that Allah has tailored even this aspect to your creation? Will you now thank Him for the precise balance He has given you?

How Temperament Determines Anger and Courage
Explanation of how heart and brain temperaments determine anger and courage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how heart temperature and brain moderation interact to produce anger and courage, and how reason can counteract cowardice.

Core Idea
Reason can override temperament to produce courage even in a cowardly heart.

In this passage, the author explains the physical basis of anger and courage through the temperaments of the heart and brain. A hot heart naturally fuels anger, but a moderate brain reins it in. Conversely, a cold heart breeds cowardice, yet a sound mind can still compel brave acts. This reveals a layered relationship between body and intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Notice the key terms: الغضب, القلب, الدماغ, الحرارة, العقل, الشجاعة, الجبن.
This passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two cases: (1) a hot heart with moderate brain, (2) a weak heart with moderate brain. Find each and note the outcomes.
The second scenario includes a twist: reason can override cowardice.
3
Reflect on the role of intellect
Consider why the author emphasizes that the moderate brain can compel courageous acts despite cowardly nature. How does this highlight the power of reason?
This ties into broader Islamic psychology: ‘aql (intellect) can guide behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معتدلاً ، فعند استيلاء الغضب على القلب قوي الحرارة ، وكان الدماغ معتدلاً ، فعند استيلاء الغضب على القلب ، فإن الدماغ المعتدل يمنعه عن الإفراط في العمل، وإما إن كان القلب ضعيف الحرارة ، فصاحبه يكون جباناً . إلا أنه إذا كان الدماغ معتدلاً ، فالعقل قد يحمله على أفعال الشجاعة ، وإن كان الجبن حاصلاً في القلب .
Moderately, so when anger overcomes the heart [if the heart is] strong in heat, and the brain is moderate, then when anger overcomes the heart, the moderate brain prevents it from excess in action. And if the heart is weak in heat, then its possessor is a coward. However, if the brain is moderate, then reason may compel him to acts of courage, even if cowardice resides in the heart.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the relation between physical temperaments (heart and brain) and psychological traits of anger and courage. The author explains two scenarios: (1) A hot heart with a moderate brain leads to moderation of anger because the brain restrains excess. (2) A weak (cold) heart leads to cowardice, but if the brain is moderate, reason can still compel courageous acts despite the inherent cowardice. Thus, the intellect ('aql) has a corrective role over temperamental predispositions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that temperament fully determines behavior—e.g., a cowardly heart always makes one a coward.
Correction: The passage shows that even if cowardice resides in the heart (due to weak heat), a moderate brain and reason can compel courageous acts, so behavior is not solely determined by temperament.

Key Takeaway

The Physical Causes of Hunger and Appetite
Detailed analysis of how body size, stomach size, and the spleen duct affect hunger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical causes of hunger according to Islamic medical theory, including the role of black bile and the interaction of body size, stomach size, and duct width.

Core Idea
Hunger is caused by black bile stinging the stomach mouth, influenced by body and stomach size.

In this passage, the author explains the physical mechanism underlying the sensation of hunger. He describes how the appetitive power of the liver relates to the body's size, the stomach, and a duct from the spleen that carries black bile. By understanding these factors, we gain insight into the medieval medical perspective on appetite.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the explanation. Note the key terms like 'spleen', 'black bile', and 'duct'.
2
Identify the Three Main Factors
The author identifies three variables: body size, stomach size, and duct width. Notice how each combination affects hunger intensity.
Large body + large stomach + wide duct = intense hunger; small body + small stomach + narrow duct = mild hunger.
3
Understand the Role of Black Bile
The sensation of hunger is directly linked to the flow of black bile from the spleen to the stomach mouth, causing a sting. This is the immediate cause.
4
Consider Additional Factors
The author mentions that liver condition (heat/cold, moisture/dryness, size) and food qualities also influence hunger, making it a complex interplay.
These are listed as further considerations beyond the three main variables.
5
Reflect on the Complexity
The passage emphasizes that hunger is not a simple phenomenon but depends on many bodily and dietary conditions. Think about how this aligns with or differs from modern understanding.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثالث : وهو القوة الشهوانية الكبدية : فاعلم أن الشهوة إما في المأكول والمشروب ، أو في الفرج . أما الشهوة في المأكول والمشروب . فنقول : البدن ، إما أن يكون كبيراً ، وإما أن يكون صغيراً ، وكذلك المعدة إما أن تكون كبيرة أو صغيرة ، وأيضاً : فإن الإحساس بالجوع إنما يحصل بسبب أن بين الطحال، وبين فم المعدة مجرى ينصب فيه قسط من السوداء الطبيعية إلى فم المعدة ، فيحصل هناك لذع ودغدغة . وذلك هو الإحساس بالجوع . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : ذلك المجرى إما أن يكون واسعاً أو ضيقاً . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن كان البدن كبيراً ، والمعدة كبيرة ، وكان ذلك المجرى واسعاً ، فإنه يكثر جوع هذا الإنسان ، لأن البدن الكبير يتحلل منه أجزاء كثيرة ، والمعدة الكبيرة متسعة للغذاء الكثير ، والسوداء الكثيرة المنصبة في ذلك المجرى الواسع توجب الإحساس العظيم بالجوع ، فلما اجتمعت هذه الأسباب ، عظم الجوع . فهذا الإنسان يأكل مقداراً كبيراً في المرة الواحدة ، ويكفيه ذلك زمان مديداً ، وإما إن كان البدن صغيراً ، والمعدة صغيرة ، والمجرى ضيقاً ، فههنا يقل الجوع . لأن البدن لما كان صغيراً ، كان المتحلل منه قليلا ، ولما كانت المعدة صغيرة لم تتسع للطعام الكثير ، ولما كان المجرى ضيقاً لم ينصب إلى فم المعدة إلا القليل من السوداء ، فعند اجتماع هذه الأسباب يقل الجوع . وإذا عرفت هذين الطرفين ، عرفت الحال في سائر الأقسام . وأيضاً يجب اعتبار حال الكبد في الحرارة [ والبرودة ] والرطوبة واليبوسة ، واعتبار حاله في الصغر والكبر، واعتبار حاله في كون الأوردة الناشئة منه واسعة أو ضيقة ؟ وأيضاً يجب اعتبار حال الأغذية والأشربة في كيفياتها البسيطة والمركبة . وإذا اعتبرت مجموع هذه الأحوال البسيطة والمركبة : كثرت جداً
The third type: the appetitive hepatic power. Know that appetite is either for food and drink or for the genital. As for appetite for food and drink, we say: the body is either large or small, and similarly the stomach is either large or small. Also, the sensation of hunger occurs because between the spleen and the mouth of the stomach there is a duct through which flows a portion of natural black bile to the mouth of the stomach, causing a sting and tickle there. That is the sensation of hunger. If you know this, we say: that duct is either wide or narrow. And if you know this, we say: if the body is large, the stomach large, and that duct is wide, then this person's hunger increases, because the large body has many parts that break down, the large stomach is capacious for much food, and the abundant black bile flowing into that wide duct causes great sensation of hunger. So when these causes come together, hunger becomes great. This person eats a large amount at one time, and it suffices him for a long time. And if the body is small, the stomach small, and the duct narrow, then hunger decreases. Because when the body is small, the broken-down parts are few; when the stomach is small, it cannot accommodate much food; and when the duct is narrow, only a little black bile flows to the mouth of the stomach. So when these causes come together, hunger decreases. And if you know these two extremes, you know the situation for the remaining cases. Also, one must consider the condition of the liver in heat and cold, moisture and dryness, and consider its condition in smallness and largeness, and consider the condition of the veins originating from it, whether wide or narrow. Also, one must consider the condition of foods and drinks in their simple and compound qualities. And if you consider all these simple and compound conditions, they become very numerous.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the physical causes of hunger, which falls under the appetitive power. The author begins by stating that the appetitive power is located in the liver. Appetite is for food/drink or for sex. For food, the explanation involves three variables: body size, stomach size, and the width of a duct from the spleen to the stomach mouth that carries black bile (sawda'). The sensation of hunger is caused by the sting of black bile. Large body + large stomach + wide duct = great hunger; small body + small stomach + narrow duct = little hunger. The author then adds further considerations: the liver's temperament (hot/cold, moist/dry), size, and the veins' width, plus the qualities of food and drink. When all these conditions are combined, they become very numerous, implying that hunger is influenced by many factors.

Differences in Appetites and Sexual Desire Due to Temperament
Explains how dominant humors affect food preferences and how temperament affects sexual desire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how temperament influences food preferences and sexual desire according to classical humoral theory.

Core Idea
Temperament determines cravings and desires, but reason can moderate.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah explains why people have different food preferences and varying levels of sexual desire. It attributes these differences to the balance of humors and temperaments. As you read, notice how the author provides specific examples and then generalizes a principle. This ties back to the broader theme that bodily states affect the soul's inclinations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the terms for humors and temperaments.
2
Identify the two main topics
The passage covers two distinct areas: food preferences and sexual desire. Note how each is explained.
3
Note specific humoral associations
For food, thick phlegm leads to craving hot/pungent foods; dryness leads to fatty/sweet. For sexual desire, hot-moist is best, cold-dry worst.
The author says 'apply the same reasoning to the rest' — so this is a general principle.
4
Reflect on the general conclusion
The final sentence states that differences in souls arise from differences in temperaments. This is the key takeaway.
Recall earlier lessons that reason can intervene, so this is about tendencies, not absolute determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اختلاف الناس في الشهوات . فمن كانت البلاغم اللزجة غالبة على معدته ، كان ميله إلى الأشياء الحارة والحريفة والقابضة والعفصية ، ومن كان التيبس غالباً على معدته ، كان ميله إلى الدسم والحلو . وقس الباقي عليه . وأما اختلاف الناس في شهوة الباه . فالمزاج الحار الرطب يناسبه من كل الوجوه ، والبارد [ اليابس يضاده من كل الوجوه . وأما الحار اليابس والبارد ] الرطب فك المتوسط . فهذا هو الضبط الكلي في بيان أن أحوال النفوس كيف تصير مختلفة بسبب اختلاف الأمزجة [ والله أعلم ]
As for the differences among people in appetites: whoever has thick phlegm dominating his stomach, his inclination is toward hot, pungent, astringent, and bitter things; and whoever has dryness dominating his stomach, his inclination is toward fatty and sweet things. And apply the same reasoning to the rest. As for the differences among people in sexual desire: the hot-moist temperament suits it in every way, while the cold-dry opposes it in every way. As for the hot-dry and cold-moist, they are intermediate. This is the comprehensive principle in explaining how the states of souls become different due to differences in temperaments. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on temperaments by addressing two further aspects: the variation in food preferences and the variation in sexual desire. For food preferences, the author notes that if thick phlegm (balāghim) dominates the stomach, the person craves hot, pungent, astringent, and bitter foods. If dryness (yubs) dominates, the person craves fatty and sweet foods. The reader is to analogize for other humors. For sexual desire, the most suitable temperament is hot-moist; cold-dry is the opposite; hot-dry and cold-moist are intermediate. The final sentence is a general conclusion: the entire discussion shows that differences in souls (ahwāl al-nufūs) arise from differences in temperaments (amzija). The author ends with 'God knows best' as a humble acknowledgment of divine wisdom.

Temperament and Desires
Explains how differences in temperament cause variations in desires and states

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how differences in bodily temperaments (hot/cold, wet/dry) lead to variations in desires and psychological states, and how this principle is applied analogically.

Core Idea
Your temperament shapes your desires: hot-moist favors sexual drive; cold-dry opposes it.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah explains the connection between physical constitution (temperament) and psychological tendencies. By examining the liver, veins, and diet, the author shows how variations in heat, cold, moisture, and dryness influence what people crave—from food to sexual desire. The lesson introduces a comprehensive principle for understanding these differences.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Start by reading the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the author's method: he lists factors (liver, veins, food) and then gives examples of desires.
The passage builds on a previous discussion of 'two extremes'—focus on the examples given.
2
Identify the key factors
The author mentions considering the liver's qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness, size), the width of veins, and the qualities of food and drink. These are the simple and compound states that multiply.
This sets the background for understanding why desires differ.
3
Analyze the desire examples
Two specific cases are given: (1) viscous phlegm in stomach leads to craving hot, pungent, astringent things; (2) dryness leads to craving fatty and sweet things. Notice the pattern: opposite qualities attract or balance.
The phrase 'wa qis al-baqi ʿalayh' (and analogize the rest to this) shows this method is generalizable.
4
Examine sexual desire
The passage then links sexual desire to the four basic temperaments: hot-moist suits it fully, cold-dry opposes it fully, hot-dry and cold-moist are intermediate. This illustrates the principle with a clear classification.
This is a direct application of the same logic to a different aspect of psychology.
5
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that this is the comprehensive principle for explaining how states of souls differ due to temperament differences. Reflect on how this principle can be extended to other psychological states.

And if you know these two extremes, you know the situation in the rest of the categories. Also, one must consider the state of the liver in heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, and consider its state in smallness and largeness, and consider its state regarding the veins emerging from it—whether wide or narrow? Also, one must consider the state of foods and drinks in their simple and compound qualities. And if you consider the entirety of these simple and compound states, they become very numerous. As for the difference among people in desires: whoever has viscous phlegm dominating his stomach, his inclination is toward hot, pungent, astringent, and tannic things. Whoever has dryness dominating his stomach, his inclination is toward fatty and sweet things. And analogize the rest to this. As for the difference among people in sexual desire: the hot-moist temperament suits it in every respect, and the cold-dry opposes it in every respect. As for the hot-dry and the cold-moist, they are like the intermediate. So this is the comprehensive principle in explaining how the states of souls become different due to the differences of temperaments. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage explains how differences in bodily temperaments lead to differences in psychological states and desires. It begins by stating that if one knows the two extremes (presumably referring to a previous classification), one knows the rest of the categories. It then instructs to consider the liver's state in heat, cold, moisture, dryness, size, and the width of its veins, as well as the qualities of foods and drinks. These considerations multiply greatly if all combinations are taken into account. Next, it discusses differences in desires: those with viscous phlegm in the stomach incline toward hot, sharp, astringent, and tannic things; those with dryness incline toward fatty and sweet. Similarly, sexual desire is analyzed: the hot-moist temperament is fully suitable, the cold-dry is fully opposed, and the hot-dry and cold-moist are intermediate. The conclusion is that this is the comprehensive principle for explaining how states of souls become different due to differences in temperaments. The passage follows an enumerative and analogical method, using the known extremes to derive the rest.

The Unity of the Soul: Aristotle and Galen
Opens chapter on soul's unity, cites Aristotle and Galen, presents first proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical debate on the unity of the soul and the first argument from self-evident knowledge.

Core Idea
Your awareness of 'I' proves the soul is one, not many.

This lesson introduces a foundational discussion in Islamic psychology: Is the soul one or multiple? We begin with Aristotle's unitary soul and Galen's tripartite model, then examine the first proof from self-evident knowledge of the self.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two positions presented.
The passage ends mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of the first proof.
2
Identify the two views
Aristotle holds that the soul is one from which multiple faculties emanate. Galen holds there are three distinct souls (rational, irascible, appetitive) with different loci.
3
Understand the first proof
The soul is defined as that to which everyone points by saying 'I'. Self-evident knowledge tells us this referent is one, without multiplicity.
This is a direct argument from introspection.
4
Examine the objection and reply
An objection suggests that the 'one' could be composed of many parts. The author sets it aside, asserting that the necessary knowledge of oneness suffices.
The reply is brief and does not fully engage the objection.
5
Reflect on self-evident knowledge
Consider why the author considers the knowledge of the self as 'self-evident' (badihi) and how this supports the soul's unity.
This is a key concept in Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في بيان أن النفس واحدة
ذهب ( أرسطاطاليس (۱) ، أصحابه : إلى أن النفس واحدة وتنبعث منها قوى مختلفة كثيرة ، بحسب الأفعال المختلفة. وهذا هو الحق الذي لا شك
وقال ( جالينوس : : النفوس الثلاثة :
النفس النطقية . ومتعلقها الدماغ . والنفس الغضبية . ومتعلقها القلب . والنفس الشهوانية . ومتعلقها الكبد
والذي يدل على أن لكل إنسان نفساً واحدة وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن المراد بالنفس ما يشير إليه كل أحد بقوله « أنا ، والعلم البديي حاصل بأن ذلك الشيء واحد ، ليس فيه تعدد البتة . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال (۳) : المشار إليه لكل أحد بقوله ( أنا ، وإن كان واحداً ، إلا أن ذلك الواحد يكون مركباً من أشياء كثيرة ؟
قلنا : إنه لا حاجة بنا في هذا المقام إلى إبطال هذا السؤال . بل نقول : المشار إليه بقولي ( أنا معلوم بالضرورة : أنه شيء واحد . فأما أن ذلك
Chapter Three on explaining that the soul is one. Aristotle (1) and his followers held that the soul is one and from it emanate many different faculties, according to different actions. This is the truth that is not doubted. Galen said: The three souls: the rational soul, and its locus is the brain; the irascible soul, and its locus is the heart; and the appetitive soul, and its locus is the liver. That which indicates that every human being has one soul are [several] aspects: The first proof: That what is meant by 'soul' is that to which everyone points by saying 'I', and self-evident knowledge obtains that that thing is one, having no multiplicity whatsoever. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say (3): that which is pointed to by everyone in saying 'I', although it is one, yet that one is composed of many things? We say: We have no need in this context to refute this question. Rather, we say: That which is pointed to by my saying 'I' is known necessarily to be one thing. As for that one...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Three on the unity of the soul. It first presents the position of Aristotle and his followers: the soul is one, and from it emanate many different faculties (quwa) corresponding to different actions. The author affirms this as the indisputable truth. Then it mentions Galen's contrasting view: there are three souls — the rational (natiqa) with the brain as its locus, the irascible (ghadabiyya) with the heart, and the appetitive (shahwaniyya) with the liver. After stating these views, the author signals that there are several proofs (wujuh) for the soul's unity. The first proof (al-hujja al-ula) is presented: the soul is that to which everyone points by saying 'I' (ana). Self-evident knowledge (al-ilm al-badihi) tells us that this referent is one, absolutely without multiplicity. A possible objection is then raised: why can't this 'one' be composed of many things? The author replies that we do not need to refute this objection here; it suffices to assert that the referent of 'I' is necessarily known to be one thing. At this point the text breaks off, leaving the rest of the proof incomplete. The passage thus defines the soul in terms of self-awareness and argues for its unity based on immediate knowledge, while acknowledging and setting aside a potential objection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the soul could be multiple substances, as Galen suggested.
Correction: The author argues that self-evident knowledge of the 'I' shows the soul is one, not multiple.

Checkpoint

A) The soul has different faculties.
B) Everyone points to a single thing when saying 'I' through self-evident knowledge.
C) Aristotle and Galen both agreed on unity.
D) The soul is composed of many parts.

Key Takeaway
The soul is known as one through self-evident awareness of the self, the 'I'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I' to yourself in your quietest moments, do you truly feel that you are one, or do you sense internal fragmentation? What does this lesson demand of you in terms of taking ownership of every part of your soul—your intellect, your anger, your desires—as all belonging to the same single self? Will you commit to seeing your inner conflicts not as wars between different souls but as struggles within one soul that must be unified under God's guidance?
?
The author says self-evident knowledge tells you that the 'I' is one. But look at your life: are your actions consistent with that unity? Think of a recent moment when you acted against what you truly believe. How can you, starting today, align your outward behavior with the deep, undeniable oneness of your soul?
?
Galen's view of three separate souls might feel convenient—it lets you blame your anger on your 'irascible soul' or your lust on your 'appetitive soul'. But if your soul is truly one, you cannot outsource responsibility. Which part of yourself have you been excusing as 'not really you'? How will you reclaim it as your own and bring it under the authority of your rational soul's submission to Allah?

Author dismisses the first argument as irrelevant
Author dismisses the first argument as unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author dismisses the question of whether the one is composite or a true unity as unnecessary for the current discussion.

Core Idea
The nature of the one's unity is not the focus here; it is unnecessary for the argument.

The author begins by referencing a common question about the nature of the 'one'—whether it is composite or a true unity. However, he quickly sets this aside, signaling that the real issue lies elsewhere. This introductory remark clarifies the scope of the discussion and prevents unnecessary digression.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a single sentence with a clear structure.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities for the 'one': it is either composed of many things or it is a true unity in itself. Identify these two options in the Arabic.
The phrase 'هل هو واحد متركب من أشياء كثيرة، أو هو في نفسه واحد، وحدة حقيقية' captures this.
3
Understand the dismissal
The author then says 'فلا حاجة إليه في هذا المقام' (so there is no need for it in this context). Consider why he considers this question unnecessary. What might be the main point he wants to focus on?
The commentary will elaborate on this.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Think about what the author might be trying to prove. If this question is irrelevant, then the real argument must be something else. This passage sets the stage for the main proof.

Proof from Interconnection of Perception, Desire, and Anger
The author argues that perception, desire, and anger must belong to a single substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the causal interconnection of perception, desire, and anger proves they belong to a single substance.

Core Idea
Perception, desire, and anger must belong to one substance because perception directly triggers desire and anger.

This lesson presents a concise argument from classical Islamic philosophy that uses the causal relationship between perception and emotions to prove the unity of the soul. The argument shows that if perception, desire, and anger were in separate substances, perception could not influence desire and anger. Since it does, they must share a single substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the logical structure: a hypothetical assumption and its consequence.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the reductio assumption
Find the part where the author supposes that the perceiver, desirer, and anger-substance are different. Understand the consequence: perception would have no effect on desire or anger.
This is the 'if' part of the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author then points out that in reality, perception does cause desire and anger. Therefore, the perceiver must be identical to the desirer and anger-substance. This is the 'but' and 'therefore'.
The argument is a classic conditional proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشهوة، وقد يصير سبباً لحصول الغضب. فلو كان الجوهر مدركاً شيئاً مغايراً للجوهر الذي يغضب، وللجوهر الذي يشتهي، فحين أدرك صاحب الإدراك، لم يكن من هذا الإدراك له أثر ولا خبر عند صاحب الشهوة، ولا عند صاحب الغضب، فوجب أن لا يترتب على ذلك الإدراك، لا حصول الشهوة ولا حصول الغضب. وحيث حصل هذا التأثير واللزوم، علمنا: أن صاحب الإدراك هو بعينه صاحب الشهوة وصاحب الغضب.
Desire, and it may become a cause for the occurrence of anger. So if the substance that perceives were something other than the substance that becomes angry and the substance that desires, then when the possessor of perception perceived, there would be no effect or knowledge of this perception with the possessor of desire, nor with the possessor of anger. So it would be necessary that neither the occurrence of desire nor the occurrence of anger would follow from that perception. But since this effect and necessary connection occurred, we know that the possessor of perception is identical to the possessor of desire and the possessor of anger.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents an argument demonstrating that perception, desire, and anger must inhere in a single substance, i.e., one soul. The argument begins by observing that desire can sometimes cause anger, showing their close connection. It then supposes, for reductio, that the substance which perceives is distinct from the substance which becomes angry and the substance which desires. In that case, when the perceiver perceives something, there would be no effect or awareness of that perception in the separate substances of desire and anger. Consequently, the perception could not trigger desire or anger. But in reality, perception does cause desire and anger. Therefore, the perceiver must be identical to the one who desires and the one who becomes angry. Hence, all three attributes belong to a single essence.

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
إدراك (perception)
شهوة (desire)
غضب (anger)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different mental faculties (perception, desire, anger) imply different substances.
Correction: Distinct attributes can inhere in one substance. The causal connection shows they belong to a single essence.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude from the fact that perception can cause desire and anger?
A) Perception is the same as desire and anger.
B) The perceiver, desirer, and anger-substance are one and the same.
C) Desire and anger are independent of perception.
D) The soul has multiple parts.

Key Takeaway
Perception, desire, and anger must belong to one substance because perception causally influences desire and anger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden desire or anger after seeing something, do you ever pause to realize that the same 'you' who perceived is also the one who desires or rages? How does that awareness affect your response in that moment?
?

?

Fifth Proof: Unity of Sensation, Desire, and Anger
Sanusi defines man and argues that sensation, desire, and anger belong to a single essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof for the unity of the soul, based on the definition of man as a sentient, volitional being.

Core Idea
Perception, desire, and anger are all attributes of the same soul.

This passage presents a concise argument that the soul is a single entity responsible for sensation, desire, and anger. The author defines man in a way that logically forces this conclusion. We will unpack each step to see how the definition leads to the unity of these attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source Arabic once, then its translation. Notice the logical flow from definition to conclusion.
The passage uses compact language; each phrase builds the argument.
2
Understand the definition of man
The author defines man as a body with a soul that is sentient and volitional. Identify the key components: body, soul, sensation, will.
This definition is the foundation for the proof.
3
Trace the identity argument
The text asserts that the possessor of sensation is identical to the possessor of will. See how this follows from the definition of the soul.
This is the critical step: it unifies the faculties.
4
See how will splits into desire and anger
Will is specified: attraction becomes desire, repulsion becomes anger. Connect this to the broader point that these are not separate substances.
Desire and anger are two modes of the same will.
5
Conclude that perception, desire, anger belong to one essence
The proof ends with the conclusion that these three are attributes of a single essence. Confirm that the argument holds together.
This is the 'desired' outcome: unity of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: إن حقيقة الإنسان: إنه جسم، ذو نفس، حساس، متحرك بالإرادة. فشرط النفس أن تكون حساسة وأن تكون متحركة بالإرادة، فصاحب الحس هو بعينه صاحب تلك الإرادة والإرادة إن كانت إرادة الجذب فهي الشهوة، وإن كانت إرادة الدفع فهي الغضب. فهذا يدل على أن الإدراك والشهوة والغضب صفات ثلاثة لذات واحدة. وهو المطلوب.
The fifth proof: The reality of man is that he is a body, possessing a soul, sentient, moving by will. So the condition of the soul is that it be sentient and that it be moving by will. So the possessor of sensation is identical to the possessor of that will. And will, if it is a will of attraction, is desire, and if it is a will of repulsion, is anger. So this indicates that perception, desire, and anger are three attributes of a single essence. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the unity of the soul, based on the definition of man. The author defines man as a body that possesses a soul which is sentient and moves by will. From this definition, he draws two conditions: the soul must be sentient and must be volitional. Then he argues that the subject of sensation is necessarily the same as the subject of will, since both are essential to the soul. Will is further specified: when it seeks to attract something, it is called desire (shahwa); when it seeks to repel something, it is called anger (ghadab). Thus, perception (sensation), desire, and anger are three different attributes that belong to one and the same essence (dhat). This proves the point desired: the unity of the soul.

Key Terms
جسم (body)
نفس (soul)
حساس (sentient)
إرادة (will)
شهوة (desire)
غضب (anger)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensation, desire, and anger are separate faculties in separate substances.
Correction: The definition of man shows that these are attributes of the same soul; the same subject has sensation and will, and will manifests as desire or anger.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, what is the relationship between sensation and will?
A) They belong to different substances.
B) The possessor of sensation is the same as the possessor of will.
C) Will is a type of sensation.
D) Sensation is a type of will.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that sensation, desire, and anger are not separate faculties but three attributes of the same soul, based on the definition of man as a sentient, volitional body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your anger and desires are not separate from your soul, but are expressions of your own will, what does it mean for you to truly own these emotions rather than feeling controlled by them? What is one situation today where you can consciously choose your response instead of reacting automatically?

The proof says the soul is both sentient and volitional. Are you living as though your will is active in shaping your perceptions, or do you let your perceptions dictate your will? What would it look like for you to exercise your will over your sensations starting tomorrow?

Sixth Proof: One Perceiver Must Be One Soul
The author appeals to a prior decisive proof that one perceiver of all things must be a single soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the sixth proof establishes that a single soul must be the perceiver of all things, refuting the idea of distributing attributes among distinct essences.

Core Idea
One perceiver of all things must be a single soul.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from the text, which appeals to a previously established decisive demonstration. The proof asserts that the entity which perceives all perceptibles, and which possesses power, will, and agency, must be one single soul. This directly refutes the view that attributes are distributed among multiple distinct essences. As you read the passage, note how the author relies on an earlier argument and does not reproduce its full details here.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

2
Identify the core claim

3
Understand the refutation

4
Recognize the reliance on prior proof

This proof directly refutes the position of those who claim that different attributes—like perception, desire, anger, power, and will—are distributed among multiple distinct essences. The phrase 'والله أعلم' (God knows best) is a traditional closing acknowledging divine omniscience.

Objection from Multiple Souls in Plants, Animals, Humans and Answer
The author refutes the objection that different souls exist as separate substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument for separate souls based on observed hierarchy is refuted by the principle that different quiddities can share equivalent effects.

Core Idea
Different essences can produce the same effect.

In this passage, the author presents an objection to the unity of the soul based on the observed hierarchy of souls in plants, animals, and humans. The objectors argue that since each level has distinct souls absent in lower levels, they must be separate substances. The author then provides a sophisticated answer grounded in the metaphysics of quiddities, showing that sharing effects does not imply identity of essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the ladder-like argument: plants have only vegetative soul, animals add concupiscent/irascible, humans add rational. Each is claimed to be an independent substance.
Notice the inductive structure: from presence/absence to separate substances.
2
Read the answer
Notice the shift to quiddity theory: different realities (ماهيات مختلفة) can share equivalent effects (آثار متساوية).
The answer does not deny the observable hierarchy; it reinterprets its significance.
3
Apply the answer
See how the human soul can subsume functions of lower souls (e.g., managing nourishment) without being multiple substances.
The human soul is different in essence but can perform the same vegetative and irascible functions.
4
Reflect on the principle
The key takeaway: effects do not determine essence. A higher reality can encompass lower functions.
This principle is foundational in Islamic metaphysics for understanding the unity of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بتعدد النفوس: بأن قالوا: إنا وجدنا النفس المديرة لأمر الغذاء حاصلة في النبات، وهي خالية عن النفس الغضبية. ورأينا النفس الشهوانية والغضبية حاصلة في الحيوان، وهي خالية عن النفس النطقية، والقوة الفكرية. ثم رأينا هذه الثلاثة حاصلة للإنسان. فعلمنا: أن كل واحدة من هذه الثلاثة جوهر مستقل بنفسه، منفرد بذاته، إلا أنها اجتمعت في الإنسان. والجواب: إنه ثبت في مباحث الماهيات: أن الحقائق المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في آثار متساوية. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: النفس النباتية مخالفة للنفس الإنسانية في الماهية، إلا أن النفس الإنسانية مشاركة للنفس النباتية في تدبير الغذاء، وإن كانت مخالفة لها في الذات والماهية، وفي سائر الآثار؟ فإن النفس الإنسانية تقوى على تدبير الأحوال النطقية، والنفس النباتية لا تقوى على ذلك. وهذا الجواب في القوة الغضبية.
And those who claim multiplicity of souls argued: We found the soul that manages the affair of nourishment present in plants, and it is devoid of the irascible soul. And we saw the concupiscent and irascible soul present in animals, and it is devoid of the rational soul and the intellectual power. Then we saw these three present in man. So we knew that each one of these three is an independent substance, unique in itself, except that they have come together in man. The answer: It is established in investigations of quiddities that different realities are not prevented from sharing in equivalent effects. And if this is established, we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the vegetative soul is different from the human soul in quiddity, but the human soul shares with the vegetative soul in the management of nourishment, even though it is different from it in essence and quiddity and in other effects? For the human soul is capable of managing rational states, while the vegetative soul is not capable of that. And this answer applies to the irascible power.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objectors (those who claim multiplicity of souls) argue based on observed hierarchy: plants have a nutritive soul but lack an irascible soul; animals have both concupiscent and irascible souls but lack a rational soul; humans have all three. Therefore, each is an independent substance, and in humans they merely coexist. The answer draws on the science of quiddities (māhiyyāt): different realities can share in equivalent effects (āthār mutasāwiya). Thus, the human soul can be different in quiddity from the vegetative soul, yet still perform the same function of managing nourishment. Similarly, the human soul can perform the functions of the irascible power without being a separate substance. The key point is that sharing effects does not imply identity of essence; a higher soul can subsume the functions of lower souls.

Key Terms
تعدد النفوس
نفس نباتية
نفس شهوانية
نفس غضبية
نفس نطقية
ماهية
آثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Performing the same function implies the same substance.
Correction: Different essences can share equivalent effects; a higher soul can perform lower functions without being multiple substances.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objectors' argument for multiple souls?
A) The human soul is irrational.
B) Plants and animals lack some souls that humans have, so each soul is separate.
C) The soul is one in all beings.
D) The irascible soul is the same as the rational soul.

Key Takeaway
Different essences can produce the same effects; thus the human soul's multiple functions do not imply multiple substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel torn between your desires, your anger, and your reason, do you treat these as separate 'selves' inside you? How would your life change if you truly owned them all as aspects of your single soul, with your rational soul leading the way?
?
The human soul can manage nourishment and rational states. Are you feeding your soul with spiritual nourishment (knowledge, worship, reflection) as much as you feed your body? What is one concrete step you can take today to prioritize your rational soul over base desires?
?
If you truly believed that your soul is a single substance given by Allah, would you still allow your desires or anger to take control, claiming 'that's just how I am'? How can you remind yourself daily that your essence is one, capable of integrating all forces under divine guidance?
