One Soul, Diverse Acts: The Role of Instruments
Sanusi answers an objection about one soul producing diverse acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the unity of the soul is compatible with the diversity of its acts, and how the concept of instruments resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
One soul, many acts—different instruments explain the diversity.

This passage addresses a common objection against the unity of the soul: if the soul is one, how can it produce such varied acts? Sanusi's answer is elegantly simple—the diversity lies in the instruments, not the soul itself. This short but powerful argument completes the discussion on the soul's unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and literal translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal English translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
The passage is only two sentences long but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Find the objector's question ('How can a single soul be a source of different acts?') and the author's response ('Why is it not permissible... due to different instruments?').
The answer is given as a rhetorical question implying the permissibility.
3
Understand the role of instruments
Reflect on how instruments (ālāt) allow one agent to produce many acts. For example, the same soul uses the hand for grasping and the eye for seeing.
This explains why the unity of the soul is not contradicted by the multiplicity of effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: فإن النفس الواحدة كيف تكون مصدراً للأفعال المختلفة؟ فنقول: لم لا يجوز أن يصح ذلك بسبب الآلات المختلفة؟ فهذا تمام الكلام في إثبات وحدة النفس.
If they say: How can a single soul be a source of different acts? We say: Why is it not permissible for that to be valid due to different instruments? So this is the completion of the discourse on establishing the unity of the soul.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection and its concise answer. The objector asks: how can one single soul produce diverse acts that are seemingly different in nature? The answer is straightforward: the diversity of acts does not require a diversity of agents; one agent can produce different acts through different instruments (ālāt). For example, the same soul uses the hand for grasping, the eye for seeing, etc. Thus, the unity of the soul is not contradicted by the multiplicity of its effects. The author then declares that this concludes the discussion on proving the unity of the soul.

Key Terms
نفس واحدة (single soul)
مصدر (source)
أفعال مختلفة (diverse acts)
آلات (instruments)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many different acts require many different souls or agents.
Correction: One agent can produce many acts through different instruments, so the diversity of acts does not imply a multiplicity of souls.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain that a single soul can produce diverse acts?
A) The soul has multiple parts.
B) Through different instruments.
C) The acts are not truly diverse.
D) The soul changes its nature.

Key Takeaway
One soul can produce many acts through different instruments, preserving its unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time you felt fragmented, as if different impulses came from different 'selves.' This lesson teaches one soul with many instruments. What instrument—habit, desire, fear—is causing you to act against your deepest convictions? Commit to one change today that aligns that instrument with your soul's unity.
?
The objection assumes that diversity requires multiple sources. Where in your life do you blame a 'divided heart' for your inconsistency, rather than seeing one soul using broken or misdirected instruments? What step will you take to repair that instrument?
?
If your soul is one, what single fruit do you want your life to produce through every action? Write down one concrete act for today that will direct all your instruments—your hands, tongue, mind—toward that single purpose.

Objection and Answer on the Unity of the Soul
Refuting the objection that one soul cannot produce diverse actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that a single soul cannot produce diverse actions by appealing to the principle of shared effects and different instruments.

Core Idea
One soul can produce many actions through different instruments.

In this passage, the author tackles a key objection against the unity of the soul: if there are three distinct souls (vegetative, animal, human), how can they combine in one human? And if the soul is one, how can it produce different actions? The answer draws on the principle that different realities can share common effects, and that a single substance can produce diverse effects through different instruments. Let's examine this argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
2
Identify the initial objection
Notice the objection: three independent substances are combined in one human. The author presents this as the challenge.
The objection assumes each soul is independent.
3
Understand the quiddity principle
The author references the study of quiddities to establish that different realities can share in equal effects. This principle is crucial for the answer.
This is a general metaphysical rule.
4
Apply the principle to the souls
See how the author applies it: the vegetative and human souls differ in quiddity but share the management of nutrition. The human soul has additional rational capabilities.
5
Examine the second objection and answer
The second objection asks how one soul can be the source of different actions. The answer: through different instruments. This completes the argument for unity.
This directly addresses the main concern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل واحدة من هذه الثلاثة [ جوهر ] مستقل بنفسه ، منفرد بذاته ، إلا أنها اجتمعت في الإنسان . والجواب : إنه ثبت في مباحث الماهيات : أن الحقائق المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في آثار متساوية . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : النفس النباتية مخالفة للنفس الإنسانية في الماهية ، إلا أن النفس الإنسانية مشاركة للنفس النباتية في تدبير الغذاء ، وإن كانت مخالفة لها في الذات والماهية ، وفي سائر الآثار ؟ فإن النفس الإنسانية تقوى على تدبير الأحوال النطقية، والنفس النباتية لا تقوى على ذلك وهذا الجواب في القوة الغضبية فإن قالوا : فإن النفس الواحدة. كيف تكون مصدراً للأفعال المختلفة ؟ . فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يصح ذلك بسبب الآلات المختلفة ؟ فهذا تمام الكلام في إثبات وحدة النفس
Each one of these three [substances] is independent in itself, unique in its essence, but they came together in the human. The answer: It is established in the investigations of quiddities that different realities are not prevented from sharing in equal effects. When this is established, we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the vegetative soul is different from the human soul in quiddity, yet the human soul shares with the vegetative soul in managing nutrition, even though it differs from it in essence and quiddity, and in the rest of effects? For the human soul is capable of managing rational states, while the vegetative soul is not capable of that. And this answer pertains to the irascible faculty. For if they say: How can one soul be the source of different actions? We say: Why is it not permissible for that to be valid through different instruments? This completes the discourse on establishing the unity of the soul.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the unity of the soul. The author first states an objection: each of the three souls (vegetative, animal, human) is independent, yet they are combined in humans. The answer begins by referencing the study of quiddities, where it is established that different realities can share in common effects. Then the author applies this: the vegetative and human souls differ in quiddity, but the human soul participates with the vegetative soul in managing nutrition, while differing in other respects like rational functions. This answer is then applied to the irascible faculty as well. A further objection is raised: how can one soul be the source of different actions? The answer is that this is possible through different instruments. The passage concludes that this complete argument establishes the unity of the soul.

The Heart as the Primary Organ and the Two Views
Stating the two views on the primary organ and affirming the heart as the true one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical views on the primary organ of the soul and why the heart is affirmed as the true locus.

Core Idea
The heart is the absolute primary organ; the soul's first attachment is to it.

In this passage, the author introduces the central question: Which organ is the primary locus of the soul? He presents two competing doctrines: the majority view of prophets, saints, and sages (and Aristotle) that the heart is the absolute primary organ, and Galen's view that there are three souls attached to three different organs. The author then declares the first view as true and hints at proofs to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the garbled first line—commentary will clarify the intended meaning.
Pay attention to the two doctrines introduced.
2
Identify the two views
Locate the first view (prophets, saints, Aristotle) and the second view (Galen and physicians). Note the key terms for each.
The first view involves one soul attached to heart; the second involves three souls.
3
Observe the author's chosen position
Read the final sentence: 'The chosen view is that this is false, and the truth is the first doctrine.' Understand why the author rejects Galen's view.
The proofs that follow will reinforce this position.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the heart is considered the primary organ in Islamic tradition and philosophy. This sets the stage for the ensuing proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في بيان أن المتعاق الأمل النفس هو قلب وأن العضو الرئيسي المطلق هو القلب مذهب جمهور المحققين من الأنبياء والأولياء والحكماء : أن القلب هو العضو الرئيسي المطلق لسائر الأعضاء ، وأن النفس متعلقة به أولاً ، وبواسطة ذلك التعلق تصير متعلقة بسائر الأعضاء . وهذا هو مذهب أرسطاطاليس ، وأتباعه من القدماء والمتأخرين . ومذهب : جالينوس ، وأتباعه من الأطباء : أن الإنسان عبارة عن مجموع نفوس ثلاثة : النفس الشهوانية ، وتعلقها الأول بالكبد . والنفس الغضبية ، وتعلقها الأول بالقلب . والنفس النطقية الحكيمة ، وتعلقها الأول بالدماغ . وهذه الأعضاء الثلاثة ، كل واحد منها مستقل بنفسه ، منفرد بخواصه وأفعاله و المختار : أن هذا باطل . والحق هو القول الأول . ويدل عليه وجوه :
Chapter Four: On explaining that [the locus of the soul] is the heart and that the absolute primary organ is the heart. The doctrine of the majority of verifiers from among the prophets, saints, and sages is that the heart is the absolute primary organ for the rest of the members, and that the soul is attached to it first, and through that attachment it becomes attached to the rest of the members. This is the doctrine of Aristotle and his followers among the ancients and moderns. And the doctrine of Galen and his followers among the physicians is that the human being consists of a collection of three souls: the appetitive soul, whose primary attachment is to the liver; the irascible soul, whose primary attachment is to the heart; and the rational wise soul, whose primary attachment is to the brain. And these three organs, each one of them is independent in itself, unique in its properties and actions. The chosen view is that this is false, and the truth is the first doctrine, and it is supported by proofs:
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a classification and judgment passage. It opens chapter four, stating the thesis that the heart is the primary organ and the locus of the soul. Then it presents two views: first, the view of most verifiers (prophets, saints, sages) and Aristotle that the heart is the primary organ and the soul attaches to it first. Second, the view of Galen and physicians that there are three souls with different primary organs: appetitive to liver, irascible to heart, rational to brain. The author then states his chosen view: the Galenic view is false, and the first view is true, and it will be supported by proofs (which follow in subsequent text).

The Heart as First Organ: Proof from Innate Heat
Author argues heart forms first due to innate heat and soul's attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the heart must form first because it is the source and gathering place of innate heat, and that medical evidence shows the soul's primary attachment is to the heart.

Core Idea
The heart forms first because it alone can generate and concentrate the heat needed for the body's creation.

In this passage, the author builds a concise proof that the heart is the first organ to develop in the body. The argument rests on two foundations: the necessity of innate heat for bodily formation, and medical observations about the soul's attachment. As you read, notice how each step is marked by the phrase 'فوجب' (so it is necessary), showing logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the flow from the need for heat to the conclusion about the heart.
Pay attention to the repeated use of 'فوجب'.
2
Identify the first argument
Locate the sentence that states the body cannot form without innate heat, and that heat must be gathered. Ask yourself why this points to the heart.
The key term is 'الحرارة الغريزية' (innate heat).
3
Identify the second argument
Find the part about medical experiments. What do they show about the soul's attachment? How does this support the priority of the heart?
The experiments involve 'الروح' (spirit).
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine both arguments: the heart is necessary for heat and is the first attachment point of the soul. What final conclusion does the author draw?
The conclusion is that the heart is 'العضو الرئيسي المطلق' (the absolute principal organ).
5
Reflect on the reasoning style
The author uses a deductive style with conditional necessity. Notice how each step is connected by logical connectors like 'فوجب'.
This is typical of rational proofs in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في بيان أن أول الأعضاء حدوثاً هو القلب : إن البدن لا يتكون ولا يتولد إلا بواسطة الحرارة الغريزية، وهذه الحرارة إنما تقوى وتكمل إذا كانت مجتمعة ، ومجمعها هو القلب . فوجب أن تكون القلب سابقاً على تكون سائر الأعضاء. فثبت : أن النفس واحدة ، وثبت : أن أول الأعضاء حدوثاً هو القلب . ودلت التجارب الطبية : على أن المتعلق الأول للنفس هو الروح ، فوجب أن يكون تعلق النفس بالقلب ، قبل تعلقها بسائر الأعضاء ، فوجب أن يكون العضو الرئيسي المطلق ، هو القلب
And the second aspect in explaining that the first of the organs to occur is the heart: The body is not formed nor generated except by means of the innate heat, and this heat only becomes strong and complete when it is gathered, and its gathering place is the heart. So it is necessary that the formation of the heart precedes the formation of the other organs. So it is established that the soul is one, and it is established that the first of the organs to occur is the heart. And medical experiments indicate that the first attachment of the soul is to the spirit, so it is necessary that the attachment of the soul to the heart precedes its attachment to the other organs, so it is necessary that the absolute principal organ is the heart.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that the heart is the first organ to develop. The argument is based on the theory of innate heat: the body cannot form without heat, and heat requires a gathering place, which is the heart. Thus, the heart must form first. Additionally, medical experiments show that the soul's first attachment is to the spirit (al-ruh), which resides in the heart, reinforcing that the heart is the principal organ. The proof concludes that the heart is the absolute principal organ.

Second Proof: The Heart as Seat of Knowledge, Will, and Power
Author argues that the heart is the locus of knowledge, will, and power, and rebuts Galen's objection about anger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the heart is the seat of knowledge, will, and power, and how the author refutes Galen's objection that the heart only houses anger.

Core Idea
The heart is not just for emotions—it is also the seat of knowledge, will, and power.

This passage presents the second proof in a series. The author appeals to common human experience: we feel understanding coming from the heart. From this, he builds a logical chain that places knowledge, will, and power in the same locus. He then addresses Galen's objection, showing that even anger requires perception, so the heart must be cognitive. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof chain
Read the passage and trace the argument from common experience (people find understanding from the heart) to the conclusion that knowledge resides in the heart, then deduce that will and power must also reside there.
Note the logical dependency: will depends on knowledge, and power depends on will.
2
Examine Galen's objection
Identify what Galen concedes (heart is locus of anger) and what he denies (heart as locus of perception). Understand the limits of his admission.
Galen's objection is a common alternative view that limits the heart's role to emotional functions.
3
Analyze the response
The author uses the definition of anger (repulsion of what is harmful) to show that repulsion requires perception of harm. Therefore, if the heart is the seat of anger, it must also be the seat of perception and knowledge.
This refutation turns Galen's own admission against him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن العقلاء يجدون الفهم والإدراك والعلم من ناحية القلب . فعلمنا : أن القلب محل للعلم بواسطة تعلق النفس بالقلب ، وإذا كان محل العلم هو القلب وجب أن يكون محل الإرادة هو القلب لأن المريد للشيء يجب أن يكون عالما به ، وذلك الذي هو المريد يجب أن يكون بعينه عالماً . وإذا كان محل الإرادة هو القلب ، وجب أن يكون محل القدرة هو القلب . لأن ذلك الذي هو القادر يجب أن يكون بعينه هو المريد . فثبت : مجموع كل هذه الصفات هو القلب . قال جالينوس : مسلم أن القلب محل الغضب . فأما أنه محل الإدراك والشعور ، فباطل . وجوابه : أن الغضب عبارة عن دفع المنافي، ودفع المنافي لا يصح إلا بعد الشعور بكونه منافياً ، فلما سلم أن القلب محل الغضب ، لزمه تسليم أن القلب محل العلم والإدراك
The second proof: Rational people find understanding, perception, and knowledge from the direction of the heart. So we know that the heart is the locus of knowledge by means of the soul's attachment to the heart. And if the locus of knowledge is the heart, then it must be the locus of will, because the one who wills something must know it, and the one who wills must be the same as the one who knows. And if the locus of will is the heart, then it must be the locus of power, because the one who is powerful must be the same as the one who wills. So it is established that the sum of all these attributes is the heart. Galen said: 'I admit that the heart is the locus of anger, but as for it being the locus of perception and sensation, that is false.' The response: Anger is the repulsion of what is harmful, and repulsion of what is harmful cannot be valid except after perceiving that it is harmful. So since he admitted that the heart is the locus of anger, it follows that he must admit that the heart is the locus of knowledge and perception.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, which is based on common experience: people feel understanding and knowledge coming from the heart. The author then argues that since knowledge is in the heart, will must also be there because the one who wills must know what he wills. Similarly, power must be there because the one who has power must be the same as the one who wills. Thus the heart is the seat of all these attributes. The passage then addresses Galen's objection that the heart is only the seat of anger, not perception. The author responds that anger requires perception of what is harmful, so if the heart is the seat of anger, it must also be the seat of perception and knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم
الإرادة
القدرة
الغضب
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The heart is only the seat of anger and emotions, not of cognitive functions like knowledge and perception.
Correction: The author argues that since anger requires perception of harm, the heart must also be the seat of knowledge and perception.

Checkpoint

C) Because will and knowledge are the same thing
D) Because Galen said so

Key Takeaway

Third Proof: The Heart as Source of Perception and Motion
Author argues that the animal soul's attachment to the heart makes the heart the source of perception and voluntary motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that the animal soul's attachment to the heart necessitates the heart as the source of sensation and voluntary motion, and how this refutes Galen's theory.

Core Idea
The animal soul makes the heart the center of perception and movement, not the brain.

This lesson presents a concise but powerful argument from the Islamic theological tradition that the heart, not the brain, is the primary seat of sensory perception and voluntary motion. By analyzing the nature of the animal soul and its attachment to the heart, the author demonstrates why Galen's alternative view is untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the argument begins with an accepted premise about the animal soul.
2
Analyze the logical structure
The argument is a conditional: if the animal soul is sensitive and voluntarily moving, and it attaches to the heart, then the heart becomes the source. Identify the premise and the conclusion.
3
Contrast with Galen's view
The proof explicitly invalidates Galen's claim that the brain is the source. Reflect on the significance of this disagreement in the history of science and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لا نزاع في أن النفس الحيوانية يجب كونها حساسة متحركة بالإرادة ، فإذا تعلقت النفس الحيوانية بالقلب ، وجب أن تكون تلك النفس حساسة متحركة بالإرادة ، وذلك يقتضي أن يصير القلب منبعاً للإدراك والشعور وللقوة المحركة أيضاً. وذلك يبطل قول جالينوس ، إن منبع الإدراك والحركة بالإرادة هو الدماغ
The third proof: There is no dispute that the animal soul must be sensitive and voluntarily moving. So when the animal soul attaches to the heart, that soul must be sensitive and voluntarily moving, and that requires that the heart becomes the source of perception, sensation, and motive power as well. And that invalidates the statement of Galen that the source of perception and voluntary motion is the brain.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. It begins with an accepted premise: the animal soul is defined as being sensitive and capable of voluntary motion. When this soul attaches to the heart, the heart must then become the source of these functions. Therefore, the heart, not the brain, is the source of perception and voluntary movement, contradicting Galen's view.

Heat vs Cold: Heart as Source of Sensation
Author argues that sensation comes from heat, so the heart is its source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from heat and cold that the heart is the source of sensation and voluntary motion.

Core Idea
Sensation requires heat; the heart provides heat, so the heart is the seat of sensation.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from classical Islamic theology regarding the seat of sensation. The author uses the physical properties of heat and cold, along with medical experiments, to argue that the heart is a more plausible source of sensation and voluntary motion than the brain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the premises
Identify the two main premises: (1) sensation and voluntary motion require heat, cold hinders them; (2) medical experiments confirm this. Ask yourself why cold would hinder sensation.
3
Examine the conclusion
The author concludes that since the heart is the source of heat and the brain is the source of cold, the heart is more appropriately the source of sensation. Reflect on the logic: if heat is necessary, the source of heat is the most likely source.
This argument relies on the assumed properties of heart and brain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن الحس والحركة الإرادية، إنما تحصل بالحرارة : وأما البرودة فعائقة عنهما، ويدل على صحة ما ذكرناه : التجارب الطبية ، وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : القلب منبع للحرارة، والدماغ منبع للبرودة . فجعل القلب منبعاً للحس والحركة الإرادية أولى من جعل الدماغ مبدأ لهما
The fourth proof: Sensation and voluntary motion are only obtained by heat, while cold prevents them. Medical experiments indicate the correctness of what we have mentioned. And if this is established, we say: The heart is the source of heat, and the brain is the source of cold. So making the heart the source of sensation and voluntary motion is more appropriate than making the brain their principle.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof based on the physical properties of heat and cold. Sensation and voluntary motion require heat, and cold impedes them. Medical experiments confirm this. The heart is known to be the source of heat, while the brain is the source of cold. Therefore, it is more logical to attribute sensation and voluntary motion to the heart rather than to the brain.

Key Terms
الحرارة
البرودة
الحس
الحركة الإرادية
التجارب الطبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the heart is a more appropriate source of sensation than the brain?
A) Because the heart is warmer
B) Because the brain is colder
C) Because sensation requires heat
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The heart is the source of sensation because it provides heat, which is necessary for sensation and voluntary motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof treats the heart as the source of heat that enables sensation and motion. Consider your own heart: when has it been spiritually ‘heated’ with love for Allah and zeal for good deeds, and when has it been ‘cold’ and unmoved? What specific conditions in your life have either fueled or quenched that heat? Be honest—what needs to change to keep your heart 'hot' in worship?
?
The author says cold prevents sensation. In your relationship with Allah, have you ever felt a spiritual ‘coldness’ that made you numb to the sweetness of Iman or the urgency of obedience? If so, what was the source of that cold? What step will you take today to rekindle the heat of your heart through sincere reflection or action?

The Fifth Proof: Pointing to the Chest
Author argues that pointing to the chest proves the self is in the heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the universal gesture of pointing to the chest when saying 'I' is used as evidence that the self is located in the heart.

Core Idea
Your own hand reveals where your self resides.

We often gesture without thinking. When we say 'I', many of us point to our chest. The author of this text sees this as more than a habit—it is a window into our innate knowledge. In this lesson, we examine this simple but profound proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a premise (the gesture) and a conclusion (innate knowledge).
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The premise is that everyone points to their chest when saying 'I'. The conclusion is that this shows everyone knows by necessity that the self is in the heart. Identify how the author connects them.
3
Reflect on the necessity claim
The author says this knowledge is 'by necessity'—meaning it is innate, not learned. Think about whether the gesture feels instinctive or cultural. The passage implies it is universal.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that the gesture is merely cultural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : كل أحد إذا قال ( أنا ) فإنه يشير إلى صدره ، وإلى ناحية قلبه . وهذا يدل على أن كل أحد يعلم بالضرورة أن المشار إليه بقوله : أنا هو حاصل في القلب ، لا في سائر الأعضاء
The fifth proof: Everyone, when he says 'I', points to his chest, to the direction of his heart. And this indicates that everyone knows by necessity that the referent of his saying 'I' is present in the heart, not in the other organs.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, which appeals to universal human behavior. Whenever someone says 'I', they instinctively point to their chest, toward the heart. This spontaneous gesture indicates that people innately know that their true self (the referent of 'I') is located in the heart, not in other parts of the body.

Key Terms
أنا
بالضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pointing to the chest when saying 'I' is just a cultural habit.
Correction: The author argues that this pointing is universal and instinctive, showing it is based on necessary knowledge, not culture.

Checkpoint

A) That the self is located in the heart
B) That people are proud of their hearts
C) That the heart is the strongest muscle
D) That speech originates in the chest

Key Takeaway
The universal gesture of pointing to the chest when saying 'I' reveals an innate knowledge that the self resides in the heart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I' to yourself or others, do you ever feel a connection to your physical chest? What does the way you gesture reveal about your actual belief regarding where your 'self' resides?
?
The proof claims this pointing is innate, not learned. If that is true, why do so many people live as if the heart is just an organ, ignoring the spiritual reality? Have you ever silenced an intuition from your heart? What would change if you took this proof seriously?
?
If everyone already knows by necessity that the self is in the heart, what does your daily neglect of your heart's condition say about your relationship with your own soul? What concrete step will you take today to attend to your heart?

The Heart as the Source of Speech: The Sixth Proof
Author argues that speech originates from the heart via breath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from speech production points to the heart as the seat of the rational soul.

Core Idea
Speech reveals the soul's location, and breath points to the heart.

This lesson examines the sixth proof in the series: that speech, as the clearest effect of the rational soul, must originate from the organ that controls breath—the heart. By tracing the mechanism of sound production, the argument connects the rational soul to the heart rather than the brain.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how each claim builds on the previous one.
The argument is a causal chain, not a list of premises.
2
Trace the causal chain
Identify each step: rational soul → speech → sound → breath expulsion → heart. Reflect on why each step is necessary for the proof.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فوجب أن يكون' indicating a necessary conclusion.
3
Evaluate the contrast with the brain
The author concludes that attributing speech to the heart is more appropriate than to the brain. Consider why the brain is dismissed as having no need for breath.
This contrast strengthens the argument for the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن أظهر آثار النفس الناطقة هو النطق ، فوجب أن يكون معدن النفس الناطقة ، هو الموضع الذي منه تنبعث آلة النطق ، لكن آلة النطق هو الصوت ، والصوت إنما يتولد من إخراج النفس ، وإخراج النفس إنما يحصل بفعل القلب لأن القلب يستدخل النسيم الطيب للروح فإذا تسخن ذلك النسيم واحترق ، أخرجه إلى الخارج . وإذا كان إدخال النسيم وإخراجه مقصوداً للقلب ، ومصلحة له ، كان إسناد هذا الفعل إلى القلب ، أولى من إسناده إلى الدماغ، الذي لا حاجة به البتة إلى النفس
The sixth proof: The most manifest effect of the rational soul is speech. So it is necessary that the mine of the rational soul is the location from which the instrument of speech emanates. But the instrument of speech is sound, and sound is only generated by the expulsion of breath, and the expulsion of breath is only accomplished by the action of the heart because the heart draws in the pleasant air for the spirit, and when that air heats up and burns, it expels it outward. And since the drawing in and expulsion of air is intended for the heart and is beneficial for it, then attributing this action to the heart is more appropriate than attributing it to the brain, which has absolutely no need for breath.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof. It starts from the premise that speech is the clearest effect of the rational soul. Therefore, the source (mine) of the rational soul must be where the instrument of speech (sound) originates. Sound is produced by expelling breath, and breath is expelled by the heart: the heart draws in air for the spirit, heats it, and expels it. Since this process is for the heart's benefit, speech should be attributed to the heart, not to the brain, which does not need breath.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
النطق
الصوت
إخراج النفس
النسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The brain is the source of rational speech.
Correction: This argument shows that speech depends on breath, which is controlled by the heart, not the brain.

Checkpoint
What does this argument use as evidence that the heart is the source of speech?
A) The heart produces sound
B) The heart expels breath which generates sound
C) The brain is too cold for speech
D) The lungs are connected to the heart

Key Takeaway
The argument links speech to breath and breath to the heart, thereby locating the rational soul in the heart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your speech is the most evident effect of your rational soul, what do the words you spoke today reveal about the state of your soul?
?
The author argues that speech originates from the heart via breath. When you are about to speak, do you pause to ensure your heart is pure before your tongue moves? How often do you speak without your heart's consent?
?
Your breath is tied to your heart's motion. Have you ever considered that each word you speak requires a heartbeat? What might it mean that your speech is literally powered by your heart? Will you vow to let your heart rule your tongue from now on?

Galen's First Proof: The Larynx as the Instrument of Sound
Galen presents his objection and the first proof involving the larynx.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's objection to the heart-origin theory of voice and his first proof establishing the larynx as the primary instrument of sound through a trachea experiment.

Core Idea
The larynx is the gateway to voice, not the heart.

Galen challenges the earlier argument that voice originates from the heart by asserting that it actually comes from the brain. To support this, he presents a series of evidences. The first evidence is anatomical: the larynx is the primary instrument of sound, demonstrated by a simple experiment involving the trachea. This passage introduces that experiment and its implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice that Galen is directly responding to a previous proof that voice comes from the heart.
The passage is incomplete; we only have the beginning of Galen's objection.
2
Identify Galen's main claim
Galen states clearly: sound does not emanate from the heart, but from the brain. This is the central objection.
3
Understand the first proof
Galen says the first evidence is that the larynx is the primary instrument (الآلة الأولى) for sound. This is the claim he then proves with an experiment.
4
Analyze the experiment
Galen describes an experiment: if you perforate the trachea below the larynx, the animal loses its voice because the air disperses and does not reach the larynx. This shows the larynx is essential.
The text ends before the conclusion, but the logic is clear.
5
Connect to the brain's role
Ask yourself: if the larynx is the instrument, what controls it? Galen implies the brain, not the heart, is the source of voice. This sets up the rest of his argument.
This lesson only covers the first proof; later proofs will elaborate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب جالينوس عن هذه الحجة : بأن الصوت لا ينبعث من القلب ، بل من الدماغ. ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الآلة الأولى للصوت هي الحنجرة . والدليل عليه : أنك إذا خرقت قصبة الرئة أسفل من الحنجرة ، لم تسمع لهذا الحيوان صوتاً لأنك إذا فعلت بهذا الحيوان هذا الفعل ، لم يصل الهواء الخارج إلى الحنجرة بل تفرق وانتشر . وإذا لم يصل الهواء إلى الحنجرة، وكانت الحنجرة هي الآلة للصوت
Galen responded to this proof: Sound does not emanate from the heart, but from the brain. Evidence for that is through several aspects: The first: The primary instrument of sound is the larynx. The proof for that is that if you perforate the trachea below the larynx, you will not hear sound from this animal, because when you do this action to the animal, the outward air does not reach the larynx but disperses and spreads. And when the air does not reach the larynx, and the larynx is the instrument for sound, ...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of Galen's objection to the sixth proof. Galen asserts that sound does not come from the heart but from the brain. He then gives the first of several proofs: the larynx is the primary instrument of sound. To demonstrate this, he describes an experiment: if you cut the trachea below the larynx, the animal loses its voice because the air cannot reach the larynx. This shows that the larynx is essential for sound, implying that the brain, not the heart, controls speech. The text cuts off before further aspects or the author's response.

Key Terms
الحنجرة
قصبة الرئة
الهواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Voice originates from the heart.
Correction: Galen argues that voice comes from the brain, and the larynx is the primary instrument, not the heart.

Checkpoint

A) The heart
B) The larynx
C) The brain
D) The lungs

Key Takeaway
Galen uses the larynx experiment to argue that voice originates from the brain, not the heart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on feelings or common beliefs rather than seeking evidence to understand the world around you? When has a simple observation changed what you thought was true? Will you commit to examining one assumption this week?
?
Galen conducted an experiment to prove where sound comes from. What belief do you hold that you have never tested? What would it take for you to examine it honestly?
?
The larynx is a small but essential part of your body that allows you to speak. Have you ever thanked Allah for the precise design that enables you to communicate? How often do you use this gift to speak truth and goodness?

The Ninth Proof: Heart as Seat of Intellect
Razi presents the ninth proof from Quran that the heart is the seat of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses directly establish the heart as the seat of intellect and the opposite of knowledge.

Core Idea
The heart is the center of intellect, not just emotion.

In this passage, al-Razi presents a scriptural proof for the heart as the locus of intellect. He cites numerous Quranic verses that link reasoning and understanding to the heart, and also verses that attribute ignorance and disease to the heart. This cumulative evidence is meant to establish a physical and spiritual basis for cognitive faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two main parts: direct verses linking heart to reasoning, and verses attributing opposites of knowledge to the heart.
The proof is divided into two clear stages.
2
Identify the two categories of verses
Find the first set of verses that directly connect the heart to reasoning (قلوب يعقلون بها, قلوب لا يفقهون بها). Then find the second set that attribute disease, sealing, rust, locks, etc. to the heart.
The second set is longer and uses the argument from opposites.
3
Reflect on the argument's logic
Consider why al-Razi uses both positive and negative attributions. If the heart is described as the instrument of understanding and also as the subject of ignorance, it must be the seat of intellect.
This is a standard method in kalam: argument from concomitants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إن محل العقل ، هو القلب لقوله تعالى : ( أفلم يسيروا في الأرض ، فتكون لهم قلوب يعقلون بها ) وقال : ( لهم قلوب لا يفقهون بها ) وأيضاً : فإنه تعالى أضاف أضداد العلم إلى القلب ، فقال : ( في قلوبهم مرض ) وقال : ( ختم الله على قلوبهم ) وقال : ( وقولهم : قلوبنا غلف بل طبع الله عليها بكفرهم ) وقال : ( يحذر المنافقون أن تنزل عليهم سورة ، تنبئهم بما قلوبهم ) وقال : ( يقولون بألسنتهم ما ليس في قلوبهم ) . وقال : ( كلا بل ران على قلوبهم ) وقال : ( أفلا يتدبرون القرآن ؟ أم على قلوب أقفالها ) وقال : ( فإنها لا تعمى الأبصار ، ولكن تعمى القلوب التي في الصدور )
The ninth proof: Indeed, the locus of intellect is the heart, due to His statement (the Most High): 'Have they not traveled through the earth, so that they might have hearts by which to reason?' And He said: 'They have hearts with which they do not understand.' And also: He (the Most High) attributed the opposites of knowledge to the heart, saying: 'In their hearts is disease,' and said: 'Allah has set a seal upon their hearts,' and said: 'And their saying: Our hearts are wrapped; rather, Allah has stamped them with their disbelief,' and said: 'The hypocrites fear that a surah might be revealed informing them of what is in their hearts,' and said: 'They say with their tongues what is not in their hearts,' and said: 'No, but rust has covered their hearts,' and said: 'Do they not reflect on the Quran? Or are there locks upon their hearts?' and said: 'For indeed it is not the eyes that become blind, but it is the hearts within the breasts that become blind.'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof (al-ḥujja al-tāsiʿa) that the seat of intellect (al-ʿaql) is the heart (al-qalb). The proof is scriptural, drawing on Quranic verses in two stages. First, the author cites verses that directly connect the heart to the faculty of reasoning and understanding: 'Have they not traveled through the earth, so that they might have hearts by which to reason?' (Surah al-Hajj, 22:46) and 'They have hearts with which they do not understand' (Surah al-Aʿraf, 7:179). These verses show that the heart is the instrument of 'aql and fiqh (deep understanding). Second, the author argues from opposites: since God attributes the opposites of knowledge to the heart—such as disease (marad), sealing (khatm), wrapping (ghulf), rust (rān), and locks (aqfāl)—the heart must be the locus of knowledge itself. A list of verses is provided to support this: 'In their hearts is disease' (Surah al-Baqara, 2:10); 'Allah has set a seal upon their hearts' (Surah al-Baqara, 2:7); 'And their saying: Our hearts are wrapped; rather, Allah has stamped them with their disbelief' (Surah al-Nisa, 4:155); 'The hypocrites fear that a surah might be revealed informing them of what is in their hearts' (Surah al-Tawba, 9:64); 'They say with their tongues what is not in their hearts' (Surah al-Fath, 48:11); 'No, but rust has covered their hearts' (Surah al-Mutaffifin, 83:14); 'Do they not reflect on the Quran? Or are there locks upon their hearts?' (Surah Muhammad, 47:24); and 'For indeed it is not the eyes that become blind, but it is the hearts within the breasts that become blind' (Surah al-Hajj, 22:46). The cumulative effect of these verses is to establish that the heart is the subject of both knowledge and its privations, making it the seat of cognition.

Key Terms
القلب
العقل
محل العقل
أضداد العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The brain is the seat of intellect.
Correction: This proof from the Quran indicates that the heart is the locus of intellect, and it is to the heart that both understanding and its opposite (ignorance) are attributed.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, what does the verse 'إنما تعمى القلوب' indicate?
A) The eyes are the seat of understanding
B) The heart is the locus of blindness to truth
C) The brain is the seat of intellect
D) The heart is only for emotions

Key Takeaway
The Quranic evidence collectively points to the heart as the seat of intellect and cognition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which condition of the heart described in these verses—disease, seal, rust, or locks—do you recognize in yourself? What would it take for you to ask Allah to remove that barrier today?
?
You recite the Quran with your tongue, but does your heart reflect on it? The verse says there are locks upon hearts that prevent tadabbur. What is one lock you have allowed, and what key will you use to remove it?
?
The hypocrites say with their tongues what is not in their hearts. Is there any area of your life where your outward words or actions do not match the true state of your heart? How will you realign them starting now?

The Heart in Prophetic Hadith: Seat of Knowledge and Divine Control
Razi provides hadith evidence that the heart is the seat of knowledge and divine control.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how prophetic hadiths establish the heart as the locus of intellect, faith, and divine control over human actions.

Core Idea
The heart is the command center of human cognition and actions, directly turned by Allah.

This lesson presents four key hadiths cited by Imam al-Razi as evidence that the heart, not the brain, is the seat of rational knowledge and the target of divine decree. Each tradition uniquely points to the heart's central role in faith, motives, and submission to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith on the lump of flesh
Begin with the famous hadith: 'Verily, in the body there is a lump of flesh; if it is sound, the whole body is sound; if corrupt, the whole body is corrupt. Behold, it is the heart.' Note how it explicitly states the heart is the absolute rational being.
This hadith establishes the heart as the controlling organ.
2
Examine the Usama ibn Zayd incident
Read the story where the Prophet asked Usama why he killed a man who said the shahada, and then said 'Did you split open his heart?' Reflect that true faith resides in the heart, not just on the tongue.
This indicates the heart is the locus of knowledge (maḥall al-maʿrifa).
3
Reflect on the supplication 'O Turner of hearts'
The Prophet prayed 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' This shows that the heart is subject to being turned and is the center of religious conviction.
It emphasizes divine control over the heart.
4
Understand the metaphor of the two fingers
The hadith 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the Most Merciful' is explained by Razi as referring to the two motives (for action and abstinence) created by God in the heart, showing that God disposes of hearts by creating these motives.
This proves the heart is the locus of motives and impulses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قوله عليه السلام : ( ألا إن في الجسد مضغة ، إذا صلحت صلح الجسد كله، وإذا فسدت فسد الجسد كله . ألا وهي القلب ) وهذا تصريح بأن العاقل المطلق : هو القلب . وأن سائر الأعضاء تبع له . وروي أن « أسامة بن زيد ، لما قتل الكافر الذي قال : « لا إله إلا الله ، قال له [ النبي ] عليه السلام : « لم قتلته ؟ ، فقال : لأنه قال هذه الكلمة عن خوف . فقال عليه السلام : ( هلا شققت عن قلبه ؟ ، وهذا يدل على أن محل المعرفة : هو القلب . وكان عليه السلام يقول : « يا مقلب القلوب ، ثبت قلبي على دينك ) وقال عليه السلام : ( قلب المؤمن بين إصبعين من أصابع الرحمن ) وعنى به : أن صدور الفعل تارة ، والترك أخرى من العبد ، متوقف على أن يحصل في القلب داعي الفعل ، أو داعي الترك وحصول هذه الدواعي من الله تعالي . فعبر النبي عليه السلام عن تينك الداعيتين بالإصبعين . يعني : كما أن الرجل إذا أخذ شيئاً بين إصبعين ، فإنه يقلبه كيف يشاء ، فكذلك القلب مسخر بين هاتين الداعيتين اللتين يخلقهما الله تعالى فيه . فهو تعالى يتصرف في قلوب العباد بواسطة خلق هذه الدواعي فيها. وهذا يدل على أن محل الدواعي والبواعث : هو القلب
The tenth proof: His statement (peace be upon him): 'Verily, in the body there is a lump of flesh; if it is sound, the whole body is sound, and if it is corrupt, the whole body is corrupt. Behold, it is the heart.' This is an explicit statement that the absolute rational being (al-ʿāqil al-muṭlaq) is the heart, and that all other limbs are subordinate to it. And it is narrated that Usama ibn Zayd, when he killed the disbeliever who said 'There is no god but Allah,' the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to him: 'Why did you kill him?' He said: 'Because he said that word out of fear.' So he (peace be upon him) said: 'Did you split open his heart?' This indicates that the locus of knowledge (maḥall al-maʿrifa) is the heart. And he (peace be upon him) used to say: 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' And he (peace be upon him) said: 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful.' And by this he meant that the occurrence of action sometimes, and abstinence other times from the servant, depends upon the presence in the heart of the motive for action or the motive for abstinence, and the occurrence of these motives is from Allah the Most High. So the Prophet (peace be upon him) referred to these two motives as the two fingers. That is, just as a man, when he takes something between two fingers, turns it as he wills, so too the heart is subjugated between these two motives which Allah creates in it. Thus He (the Most High) disposes of the hearts of the servants by means of creating these motives in them. And this indicates that the locus of motives and impulses (al-dawāʿī wa al-bawāʿith) is the heart.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof (al-ḥujja al-ʿāshira) for the heart as the seat of intellect and knowledge, drawn from multiple prophetic traditions (aḥādīth). The author begins with the well-known hadith: 'Verily, in the body there is a lump of flesh (muḍgha); if it is sound, the whole body is sound, and if it is corrupt, the whole body is corrupt. Behold, it is the heart.' This hadith is presented as an explicit declaration (taṣrīḥ) that the absolute rational being (al-ʿāqil al-muṭlaq) is the heart, and all other limbs are subordinate (tabaʿ) to it. Next, the author cites the story of Usama ibn Zayd: when he killed a man who had uttered the shahada, the Prophet asked why, and Usama said the man said it out of fear. The Prophet then said, 'Did you split open his heart?' This indicates that true faith resides in the heart, and that the heart is the locus of knowledge (maḥall al-maʿrifa). Third, the author mentions the Prophet's supplication: 'O Turner of hearts (ya muqallib al-qulūb), make my heart firm upon Your religion.' This shows that the heart is subject to being turned and that it is the center of religious conviction. Fourth, the author discusses the hadith: 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful.' He provides an interpretation: the two fingers represent the two motives (dāʿī al-fiʿl and dāʿī al-tark) that are created by God in the heart, causing the servant to act or refrain. Just as a person turns an object held between two fingers, God turns the heart by creating these motives. Thus the heart is the locus of motives and impulses (al-dawāʿī wa al-bawāʿith). All these traditions converge on the conclusion that the heart is the seat of intellect, knowledge, action, and divine control.

Key Terms
القلب
المضغة
محل المعرفة
مقلب القلوب
الدواعي والبواعث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'between two fingers' is literal, implying God physically holds the heart.
Correction: Razi clarifies it is a metaphor for the divine creation of motives in the heart, which cause the servant to act or refrain.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth proof, what does the hadith 'قلب المؤمن بين إصبعين' indicate about the heart?
A) It is the source of free will
B) It is subject to God's control through created motives
C) It is physically manipulated by God
D) It is independent of divine action

Key Takeaway
The heart is the center of intellect, faith, and motivation, subject to Allah's direct control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the heart of the believer is between two fingers of the Most Merciful—meaning Allah directly creates your motives to act or abstain. When you struggle with a sin, do you truly believe that your desire for that sin was placed in your heart by Allah for a reason, and that He can replace it with taqwa if you ask? What will you specifically ask of Him today regarding your own heart's turning?
?
The Prophet did not accept Usama's assumption about the man's heart; he demanded evidence from the heart itself. How many people have you condemned based on outward appearances without knowing their hearts before Allah? Will you commit to leaving final judgments of others' faith to Allah, who alone knows the heart?
?
Razi argues that the heart is the absolute rational being, the center of knowledge—not just emotions. If your heart is the seat of your intellect, how often do you feed it with the knowledge of Allah's names and attributes, or do you let it be occupied with worldly distractions? What one change can you make this week to prioritize heart-based learning over mere mental information?

Three Counterexamples to Galen's Premise
Aristotelians raise three counterexamples to Galen's premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Aristotelians used empirical counterexamples to challenge Galen's premise about nerve origin.

Core Idea
Three empirical counterexamples can refute a weak premise.

The Aristotelians respond to Galen by denying that the brain is the origin of nerves. They argue that Galen's premise (strength and multiplicity indicate origin) is not demonstrative and provide three counterexamples to refute it. This lesson explores those counterexamples and the author's assessment of them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Pay attention to the structure: a denial, a breakdown of Galen's evidence, and three counterexamples.
The passage uses specific terminology like 'عصبة مجوفة' (hollow nerve).
2
Identify the argument structure
Notice that the Aristotelians first deny Galen's conclusion, then critique his evidence by requiring an additional premise, and finally refute that premise with three counterexamples.
The argument moves from denial to a two-part response, of which the first part (the premise being non-demonstrative) is the focus here.
3
Analyze each counterexample
Examine each of the three counterexamples: the optic nerve, the seed and tree analogy, and the web-like tissue in the brain. Understand how each contradicts the idea that strength and multiplicity indicate origin.
These counterexamples rely on observable phenomena.
4
Consider the author's evaluation
Note the author's remark that these counterexamples are 'forced' (متكلفة) and that Galen could reply. Reflect on what this implies about the strength of the objections.
The author does not fully endorse the counterexamples but presents them as part of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن أصحاب أرسطاطاليس أجابوا عن هذه الحجة على مقامين : المقام الأول : لا نسلم : أن الدماغ هو المنبت للعصب . وأما دليله جالينوس عليه ، وهو أن الأعصاب كثيرة وقوية عند الدماغ ، وقليلة وصغيرة عند القلب . فقد أجابوا عنه من وجهين : الأول : قالوا : إن المقدمة الواحدة لا تنتج المقصود . بل هذه المقدمة التي ذكرتموها لا بد من ضم مقدمة أخرى إليها . وهي أن يقال : القوة والكثرة لا يحصلان إلا عند المبدأ ، والقلة والصغر ، لا يحصلان إلا عند البعد من المبدأ . إلا أن هذه المقدمة غير برهانية ، بل هي منقوضة بأمور ثلاثة : أولها : إن العصبة المجوفة الحاملة للقوة الباصرة ، تكون دقيقة جداً عند المنبت ، فإذا دخلت النقرة التي تكون فيها الحدقة ، غلظت تلك العصبة واتسعت ، وهذا نقض على تلك المقدمة التي عول عليها جالينوس . وثانيها : إن الحبة التي يتولد منها ساق الشجرة ، تكون أصغر بكثير من ساق الشجرة . فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال : إن العصبة الصغيرة التي في القلب ، تكون كالحبة التي منها انشعبت الأعصاب الكثيرة في الدماغ ؟ وثالثها : لو صح ما ذكره جالينوس لوجب أن يقال : إن أصل العروق الضوارب هي النسيجة الشبيهة بالشبكة التي في الدماغ ، لا القلب لأن في تلك النسيجة من العروق والضوارب ، عدد لا يحصى . وهي في غاية المشابهة لعروق الشجرة . واعلم أن هذه النقوض التي ذكروها على جالينوس : وجوه متكلفة . وله جالينوس أن يجيب عنها . ولولا ظهورها لأوردناها
Know that the followers of Aristotle responded to this argument in two stations: The first station: We do not concede that the brain is the sprouting place of the nerve. As for Galen's evidence for it, namely that the nerves are many and strong near the brain, and few and small near the heart, they responded to it in two faces: The first: They said: A single premise does not produce the desired conclusion. Rather, this premise that you have mentioned must have another premise joined to it, which is to say: Strength and multiplicity only occur at the origin, and weakness and smallness only occur at a distance from the origin. However, this premise is not demonstrative, but is refuted by three things: First: The hollow nerve carrying the visual power is very thin at the origin, but when it enters the socket where the pupil is, that nerve thickens and widens, and this is a refutation of that premise which Galen relied upon. Second: The seed from which the tree trunk grows is much smaller than the tree trunk. So why may it not also be said that the small nerve which is in the heart is like the seed from which the many nerves in the brain branched? Third: If what Galen mentioned were correct, it would be necessary to say that the origin of the pulsating veins is the web-like tissue in the brain, not the heart, because in that tissue there are countless veins and pulsating vessels, and they are extremely similar to the branches of a tree. And know that these refutations that they mentioned against Galen are forced faces. And Galen can respond to them. And if they were not obvious, we would have mentioned them.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the first station (مقام) of the Aristotelians' response to Galen. They first deny that the brain is the origin of nerves. Then they break down Galen's evidence (many and strong nerves near the brain, few and small near the heart) into two faces. The first face argues that Galen's single premise is insufficient; it requires an additional premise that strength and quantity indicate the origin. But this added premise is not demonstrative and is refuted by three empirical counterexamples: (1) the optic nerve is thin at the brain but thickens near the eye, (2) a seed is smaller than the tree it produces, so a small nerve in the heart could be the origin of many nerves in the brain, and (3) the web-like tissue in the brain has countless pulsating veins, making it a better candidate for origin than the heart. The author notes that these counterexamples are forced and Galen could answer them, but they are obvious enough not to require elaboration.

Key Terms
المقام الأول
المقدمة الواحدة
النقوض
العصبة المجوفة
القوة الباصرة
الحبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Counterexamples automatically disprove a premise decisively.
Correction: The author notes that these counterexamples are 'forced' (تكلف) and that Galen could answer them, indicating they are not necessarily conclusive.

Checkpoint
What is the first counterexample against the premise that strength and multiplicity indicate the origin?
A) The optic nerve is thin at the brain but thickens near the eye
B) The heart has many nerves
C) The spinal cord is the true origin
D) The lungs control breathing

Key Takeaway
The three counterexamples aim to show that Galen's premise about strength and multiplicity indicating origin is not universally true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a counterargument to a belief you hold dearly—like about the soul's origin—do you honestly examine it, or do you dismiss it as 'forced' to protect your comfort? Can you recall a specific instance where you did the latter? What would true intellectual humility before Allah require of you next time?
?
This passage shows scholars critiquing each other's evidence with precision. Your faith is built on proofs and reasoning. How much time do you spend truly understanding the logical foundations of your beliefs, versus simply accepting what you've been taught? Commit today to studying one proof from the Qur'an or sunnah in depth this week.
?
The Aristotelians offered counterexamples that the author calls 'forced.' Sometimes your own arguments for the truth may seem weak to others. Are you more concerned with being right or with seeking the truth, even if it means admitting your reasoning is flawed? Set a specific step to check your own arguments against counterpoints.

Second Face: Heart as Origin of Nerves via Arteries
Second face enumerates two premises to show the heart is the source of nerves via arteries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Aristotelian argument that the heart, not the brain, is the ultimate origin of nerves, and the two premises required to support this conclusion, including the evidence for the first premise that arteries and nerves share the same genus.

Core Idea
The heart is argued to be the true source of nerves because arteries and nerves are made of similar stuff.

In this passage, the Aristotelians concede a key premise about multiplicity and strength only occurring at the origin, but they turn the argument around: they claim the heart, not the brain, is that origin. They build their case on the similarity between arteries and nerves, and on a plausible mechanism of transformation. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the two supporting premises they must prove.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the structure: concession, conclusion, and then the two premises.
The passage is part of a longer argument; focus on the second face.
2
Identify the overall structure
Outline the concession, the conclusion, and the two premises. Notice how the author signals the need for premises: 'in the establishment of this statement requires proving premises'.
3
Examine the evidence for the first premise
Study the two pieces of evidence: the similarity of fibrous fragments and the ligature experiment. Ask yourself what each piece proves about the nature of nerves and arteries.
The ligature experiment demonstrates that nerves are not inherently sensitive.
4
Consider the second premise as far as the text allows
Read how arteries branch, become thin, enter the brain, and twist to form nerve-like structures. Note that the text cuts off mid-explanation.
This premise is incomplete, but the main idea is transformation through branching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : سلمنا أن الكثرة والقوة لا يحصلان إلا عند المبدأ . إلا أنا نقول : لا نزاع أن القلب منبت الشرايين. ثم إن اجرام الشرايين من جنس أجرام الأعصاب . وإذا كان كذلك ، أمكن أن تكون الأعصاب متولدة من تلك الشرايين. وبهذا التقدير يكون القلب منبتا ومنشأ للعصب . فيفتقر في تقرير هذا الكلام إلى إثبات مقدمات : المقدمة الأولى : إن العروق من جنس الأعصاب . فالدليل عليه : أن أجرام العروق تتفشى وتنقسم إلى الشظايا الليفية التي لا حس لها ، وهي بيض لدنة ، عديمة الدم ، صلبة غير حساسة في أنفسها ، والأعصاب كذلك في جميع الصفات . والدليل على أن الأعصاب غير حسّاسة في أنفسها : إنك إذا شددت العصبة برباط قوي ، شدا قوياً ، صار ما هو أسفل من موضع الشد : عديم الحس . وذلك يدل على أن العصب غير حساس في نفسه ، وإنما يجري إليه الحس من موضع آخر. فثبت : أن الشرايين والأعصاب من جنس واحد . وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي في بيان أن الأعصاب والشرايانات ، لما كانت من جنس واحد ، أمكن تولد الأعصاب من الشرايانات : فهو أنا نقول : إن هذا الشرايانات لما إنقسمت وتشعبت ، ودقت وصغرت ، ونفدت في الدماغ ، التف بعضها على بعض ، وانضمت أجزاؤها واتصلت . فصارت على صورة الأعصاب. وتمام الكلام فيه : أن هذه الشرايين حين انفصلت عن القلب ،
The second face: We concede that multiplicity and strength only occur at the origin. However, we say: There is no dispute that the heart is the origin of the arteries. Then, the bodies of the arteries are of the same genus as the bodies of the nerves. And if so, it is possible that the nerves are generated from those arteries. By this estimation, the heart is the sprouting place and origin of the nerve. So the establishment of this statement requires proving premises: The first premise: That the veins are of the same genus as the nerves. The evidence for it: That the bodies of the veins spread out and divide into fibrous fragments that have no sensation, and they are white, supple, bloodless, hard, and not sensitive in themselves, and the nerves are likewise in all qualities. The evidence that the nerves are not sensitive in themselves: If you tie a nerve tightly with a strong ligature, what is below the tie becomes insensate. This indicates that the nerve is not sensitive in itself, but sensation reaches it from another place. So it is established that the arteries and nerves are of one genus. As for the second premise: It is in explaining that since the nerves and arteries are of one genus, it is possible for the nerves to be generated from the arteries. So we say: When these arteries divided and branched, became thin and small, and passed into the brain, some of them twisted around each other, their parts joined and connected, and they became in the form of nerves. The full discussion of it: That these arteries, when they separated from the heart, ...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the second face (الوجه الثاني) of the first station. The Aristotelians now concede the premise that strength and multiplicity indicate the origin, but argue that the heart, not the brain, is the true source. Their reasoning: the heart is the origin of arteries, and arteries are of the same kind as nerves, so nerves could be generated from arteries within the brain. To support this, they must prove two premises. The first premise is that veins (or arteries) and nerves are of the same genus, evidenced by their similar fibrous structure and the fact that nerves are not inherently sensitive (as shown by the ligature experiment where tying a nerve causes loss of sensation below the tie). The second premise is that nerves can form from arteries through the branching and twisting of arteries inside the brain, transforming into nerve-like structures. The argument is cut off at the end of the page.

Key Terms
الوجه الثاني
القلب
الشرايين
تولد
المقدمة الأولى
المقدمة الثانية
الشظايا الليفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The brain is the only origin of nerves because nerves are observed to originate there.
Correction: This argument shows that nerves could be generated from arteries which originate from the heart, so the heart is the ultimate origin, making the brain a secondary site of transformation.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise needed to support the second face of the Aristotelian argument?
A) That the brain is the source of nerves
B) That veins and nerves are of the same genus
C) That the heart pumps blood
D) That nerves are sensitive

Key Takeaway
The heart is argued to be the true origin of nerves because arteries share the same genus as nerves and can transform into them in the brain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The ligature experiment shows that nerves are not inherently sensitive—sensation comes from elsewhere. In your daily life, do you attribute your feelings and perceptions solely to your physical senses, or do you acknowledge a deeper source within you? How can you train yourself to listen to that deeper source?
?
The Aristotelians argue that the heart is the origin of nerves and thus of sensation. What does this imply about the seat of your moral and spiritual awareness? Are you governed by the 'brain' (rational analysis) or by the 'heart' (intuitive knowledge)? Be honest: which has more influence on your decisions, and what needs to change?
?
If the heart is truly the source of nerves and sensation, then your 'gut feelings' might have a real origin. When have you ignored a heart-felt conviction and later regretted it? What practical step can you take this week to trust your heart's guidance over mere intellectual reasoning?

The Artery-to-Nerve Transformation Theory
Presents the artery-to-nerve transformation argument and its conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that nerves originate from transformed arteries and that this leads to the heart being the source of nerves.

Core Idea
Nerves are transformed arteries that have fulfilled their function.

In this passage, the author presents a biological theory: arteries initially carry blood and animal spirit to the brain, but once inside the brain, they become redundant and transform into nerves. This leads to the claim that the heart, as the origin of arteries, is also the origin of nerves. The attribute this view to the philosopher Khurusis (Erasistratus) and claims to have supported it with data. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the author explains how arteries become nerves after reaching the brain.
Notice the logical steps: arteries carry blood and spirit to brain, then become unnecessary, then transform.
2
Identify the conclusion
The author concludes that since the heart is the source of arteries, and arteries become nerves, the heart is the source of nerves.
This is the main claim of the argument.
3
Note the attribution
The author attributes this view to Khurusis, a philosopher and colleague of Aristotle, and claims to have supported it with his own data.
The author asserts that this argument completely refutes Galen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان يحتاج إليها في أن تكون حاملة للدم والروح الحيواني إلى جوهر الدماغ، فلما صغرت ونفذت في جوهر الدماغ، وحصل هذا المقصود، حصل الاستغناء عنها في هذا العمل، فلا جرم صرفت إلى بعض وصارت على صورة الأعصاب؛ وبهذا الطريق صارت الشريانات أعصاباً. وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: القلب هو المنبت للشريانات، وهذه الشريانات هي التي تولدت الأعصاب منها، فحينئذ يكون المنبت للأعصاب، هو القلب. وهذا قول ذكره خروسيس، وكان من أفاضل الحكماء وكان شريكاً لأرسطاطاليس في العلم. ونصرناه نحن بهذه البيانات. وبهذا الطريق يسقط كلام جالينوس بالكلية.

وجوه:
واعلم: أن جالينوس أجاب عن هذا الكلام من وجهين: الأول: قال: الدليل على أن الأعصاب ليست من جنس الشريانات
الأول: إن الشريانات نابضة، والأعصاب ليست نابضة.
الثاني: إن الشريانات مجوفة، والأعصاب ليست كذلك.
الثالث: إن الشريانات محتوية على الدم. بدليل أنها إذا ثقبت، جلب ذلك الثقب على صاحبه من انفجار الدم أمراً صعباً، والعصب كله لا دم فيه.
الرابع: إن الشريانات مؤلفة من طبقتين: إحداهما: تتحلل إلى أجزاء ذاهبة في العرض على الاستدارة، والأخرى تتحلل إلى أجزاء تذهب على الاستقامة في الطول. وأما الأعصاب فهي تتحلل إلى ليف أبيض عديم الدم، ذاهب على الاستقامة في الطول.
الخامس: إنك إذا شددت العصبة، حصل عدم الحس والحركة الإرادية، ولا يبطل منه حركة النبض. وإن شددت الشريانات، بطل النبض، ولم يبطل الحس والحركة.
السادس: إن العصب قد يمسك عن فعله كثيراً. والشريانات أفعالها دائمة. وهي النبض.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه: أن الشريانات ليست من جنس الأعصاب.
والوجه الثاني في الجواب عن ذلك الكلام: قال جالينوس: إن أصل الشريان المتشعب من القلب ينقسم إلى قسمين: قسم يصعد إلى جانب الرأس، وقسم ينزل إلى أسفل البدن، والقسم النازل إلى أسفل البدن، لا شك أنه ينقسم ويتفرق إلى الشعب الصغار الدقاق، ثم إنها بعد دقتها، ما صارت أعصاباً، فوجب أن يكون الحال كذلك في الشريانات الصاعدة إلى أعلى البدن.
هذا حاصل كلام جالينوس في إبطال قول خروسيس على ما ذكره في كتابه المسمى بـ (آراء بقراط وأفلاطون) في أوراق كثيرة.
It needed to be a carrier of blood and animal spirit to the substance of the brain. When it became small and penetrated into the substance of the brain, and this purpose was achieved, it became unnecessary for this function, so it was turned into something else and became in the form of nerves; and by this way, the arteries became nerves. And if this is established, we say: The heart is the source of the arteries, and these arteries are those from which the nerves are generated, then the source of the nerves is the heart. This is a statement mentioned by Khurusis (Erasistratus?), who was among the excellent philosophers and was a partner of Aristotle in knowledge. And we have supported it with these data. And by this way, the statement of Galen falls entirely.

Faces: Know that Galen answered this statement in two ways: The first: He said: The proof that nerves are not of the genus of arteries: First: Arteries are pulsating, and nerves are not pulsating. Second: Arteries are hollow, and nerves are not so. Third: Arteries contain blood, as evidenced that if they are punctured, that puncture brings upon its owner a difficult matter from the eruption of blood, and the nerve has no blood in it. Fourth: Arteries are composed of two layers: one dissolves into parts going transversely in a circular manner, and the other dissolves into parts going straight in length. As for nerves, they dissolve into a white fiber devoid of blood, going straight in length. Fifth: If you tie a nerve, the loss of sensation and voluntary movement occurs, but the movement of the pulse does not stop from it. And if you tie arteries, the pulse stops, but sensation and movement do not stop. Sixth: The nerve may cease its function often, while the actions of arteries are continuous, which is the pulse. So by these points it is established that the arteries are not of the genus of nerves.

The second way in answering that statement: Galen said: The origin of the artery branching from the heart divides into two parts: a part ascending to the side of the head, and a part descending to the lower part of the body. The part descending to the lower part of the body, no doubt, divides and branches into small thin branches, and then after their fineness, they do not become nerves. So it must be that the same is the case for the arteries ascending to the upper part of the body.

This is the gist of Galen's statement in refuting the statement of Khurusis as he mentioned in his book called (Opinions of Hippocrates and Plato) in many pages.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage first presents a biological argument that nerves originate from arteries. The author explains that arteries, after carrying blood and animal spirit to the brain, become unnecessary and transform into nerves. This leads to the conclusion that the heart is the ultimate source of nerves, a view attributed to Khurusis (Erasistratus), a philosopher and colleague of Aristotle. The author claims to have supported this view with data and asserts that it refutes Galen entirely. Then the text presents Galen's two answers. The first answer consists of six numbered points showing that arteries and nerves are different: arteries pulsate, are hollow, contain blood, have a two‑layered structure, and when tied, affect pulse differently from nerves; nerves cease activity while arteries pulse continuously. The second answer argues that since descending arteries do not become nerves, ascending ones likewise should not. The passage ends by summarizing Galen's refutation as found in his book on Hippocrates and Plato.

Key Terms

روح حيواني
خروسيس
أرسطاطاليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author is presenting a proven fact.
Correction: The author is presenting a theoretical argument that will be critiqued by Galen; it is not a settled conclusion.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the ultimate source of the nerves?
A) The brain
B) The heart
C) The arteries themselves
D) The animal spirit

Key Takeaway
The author argues that nerves are transformed arteries, making the heart the origin of the nervous system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage presents an ancient theory that says what is no longer needed (arteries) transforms into something else (nerves). In your own life, what overused roles or habits have you held onto, even after they have fulfilled their purpose? What might you need to transform instead of cling to?
?
The author claims to have completely refuted Galen’s position. When you are convinced you are right, how do you ensure you are not ignoring valid evidence from another perspective? Can you name a belief you hold so strongly that you might dismiss opposing views without fair consideration?
?
The author builds his argument through logical deduction, not direct observation. Reflect on a time when you relied on pure reasoning to make a major decision. Did you ever mistake a logical-sounding argument for truth? How can you test your assumptions before acting?
