Galen's First Objection: Six Differences
Lists six empirical differences between arteries and nerves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the six empirical differences Galen lists to prove that nerves and arteries are fundamentally different.

Core Idea
Galen uses six clear physical criteria to distinguish nerves from arteries.

Galen responds to the claim by listing six specific differences between arteries and nerves. Each difference is observable and testable: pulsation, hollowness, blood content, layered structure, response to tying, and continuity of function. These points aim to show that arteries and nerves are not the same genus.

Guided Path
1
Read the six points
Read Galen's first objection which lists six numbered differences. Notice the order and the specific evidence for each.
2
Group the differences
Group the differences into categories: structure (hollowness, layers), content (blood), behavior (pulsation, response to tying, continuity).
This helps to see the range of evidence.
3
Understand the conclusion
After listing the six points, Galen concludes that arteries and nerves are not of the same genus.
This conclusion directly opposes the transformation theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان يحتاج إليها في أن تكون حاملة للدم والروح الحيواني إلى جوهر الدماغ، فلما صغرت ونفذت في جوهر الدماغ، وحصل هذا المقصود، حصل الاستغناء عنها في هذا العمل، فلا جرم صرفت إلى بعض وصارت على صورة الأعصاب؛ وبهذا الطريق صارت الشريانات أعصاباً. وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: القلب هو المنبت للشريانات، وهذه الشريانات هي التي تولدت الأعصاب منها، فحينئذ يكون المنبت للأعصاب، هو القلب. وهذا قول ذكره خروسيس، وكان من أفاضل الحكماء وكان شريكاً لأرسطاطاليس في العلم. ونصرناه نحن بهذه البيانات. وبهذا الطريق يسقط كلام جالينوس بالكلية.

وجوه:
واعلم: أن جالينوس أجاب عن هذا الكلام من وجهين: الأول: قال: الدليل على أن الأعصاب ليست من جنس الشريانات
الأول: إن الشريانات نابضة، والأعصاب ليست نابضة.
الثاني: إن الشريانات مجوفة، والأعصاب ليست كذلك.
الثالث: إن الشريانات محتوية على الدم. بدليل أنها إذا ثقبت، جلب ذلك الثقب على صاحبه من انفجار الدم أمراً صعباً، والعصب كله لا دم فيه.
الرابع: إن الشريانات مؤلفة من طبقتين: إحداهما: تتحلل إلى أجزاء ذاهبة في العرض على الاستدارة، والأخرى تتحلل إلى أجزاء تذهب على الاستقامة في الطول. وأما الأعصاب فهي تتحلل إلى ليف أبيض عديم الدم، ذاهب على الاستقامة في الطول.
الخامس: إنك إذا شددت العصبة، حصل عدم الحس والحركة الإرادية، ولا يبطل منه حركة النبض. وإن شددت الشريانات، بطل النبض، ولم يبطل الحس والحركة.
السادس: إن العصب قد يمسك عن فعله كثيراً. والشريانات أفعالها دائمة. وهي النبض.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه: أن الشريانات ليست من جنس الأعصاب.
والوجه الثاني في الجواب عن ذلك الكلام: قال جالينوس: إن أصل الشريان المتشعب من القلب ينقسم إلى قسمين: قسم يصعد إلى جانب الرأس، وقسم ينزل إلى أسفل البدن، والقسم النازل إلى أسفل البدن، لا شك أنه ينقسم ويتفرق إلى الشعب الصغار الدقاق، ثم إنها بعد دقتها، ما صارت أعصاباً، فوجب أن يكون الحال كذلك في الشريانات الصاعدة إلى أعلى البدن.
هذا حاصل كلام جالينوس في إبطال قول خروسيس على ما ذكره في كتابه المسمى بـ (آراء بقراط وأفلاطون) في أوراق كثيرة.
It needed to be a carrier of blood and animal spirit to the substance of the brain. When it became small and penetrated into the substance of the brain, and this purpose was achieved, it became unnecessary for this function, so it was turned into something else and became in the form of nerves; and by this way, the arteries became nerves. And if this is established, we say: The heart is the source of the arteries, and these arteries are those from which the nerves are generated, then the source of the nerves is the heart. This is a statement mentioned by Khurusis (Erasistratus?), who was among the excellent philosophers and was a partner of Aristotle in knowledge. And we have supported it with these data. And by this way, the statement of Galen falls entirely.

So it must be that the same is the case for the arteries ascending to the upper part of the body.

Galen's Second Objection: The Symmetry Argument
Presents Galen's second argument using symmetry of arterial branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's argument that since descending arteries do not become nerves, ascending arteries also should not.

Core Idea
If descending arteries don't become nerves, why would ascending ones?

Galen offers a second objection based on symmetry. The artery from the heart divides into ascending and descending branches. Since the descending branch, after many divisions, does not transform into nerves, the ascending branch should not either. This argument attacks the idea that transformation occurs only in the brain.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of the artery
Galen states that the artery branching from the heart divides into two parts: one ascending to the head, one descending to the lower body.
2
Follow the descending branch
The descending branch divides into many small branches but does not become nerves. Galen uses this as evidence.
This is a key observation: no transformation occurs in the lower body.
3
Apply the analogy
Galen argues that the ascending branch should behave similarly: if it doesn't become nerves in the lower body, it shouldn't in the upper body either.
The argument is that there is no special reason for transformation only in the brain.

Refuting Galen's First Answer on Arterial Properties
Author refutes Galen's first answer by questioning property persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument against Galen's claim that the properties of arteries persist in the fine fragments that become nerves.

Core Idea
Properties do not persist where transformation occurs.

In this lesson, the author begins his critical response to Galen's objections. He focuses on the first answer and questions the persistence of arterial attributes after the arteries branch into the brain. Through an analogy with the brain spirit, he suggests that transformation brings new properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow, then read the translation. Notice the author's confident tone as he declares the answers weak.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the rhetorical question.
2
Identify the author's challenge
The author states that Galen's attributes occur only from the heart to the brain. He then asks: why do these attributes remain in the fine fragments? This is the core of the refutation.
The challenge is based on timing and transformation.
3
Analyze the analogy of the brain spirit
The author compares the arterial fragments to the brain spirit: generated in the heart, it undergoes changes before and after entering the brain. This supports the idea that properties change, so Galen's list is not fixed.
Think about how analogy strengthens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن هذين الجوابين عندي في غاية الضعف. أما الجواب الأول: فبيان ضعفه: أن الصفات التي ذكرها جالينوس للشريانات: صفات حاصلة لها من وقت انشعابها عن القلب، إلى وقت تشعبها إلى الأقسام الدقيقة، ونفوذها في جوهر الدماغ. فلم قلتم: إن هذه الصفات تبقى في تلك الشظايا الدقيقة بعد هذه الحالة؟ والدليل عليه: أن الروح الدماغي، لا شك أنه كان متولداً في القلب، ثم إنه تصاعد من القلب، وبقي في النسيجة المتولدة تحت الدماغ مدة، ثم إنه ينفذ في الدماغ، فيحدث له حال كونه في الدماغ، أحوال وصفات، غير الأحوال والصفات التي كانت حاصلة، حين كان في القلب.
And know: That these two answers are, in my opinion, extremely weak. As for the first answer: The exposition of its weakness: The attributes that Galen mentioned for the arteries are attributes that occur to them from the time of their branching from the heart until the time of their branching into fine divisions and their penetration into the substance of the brain. So why do you say: That these attributes remain in those fine fragments after this state? And the evidence for it: That the brain spirit (animal spirit?), no doubt, was generated in the heart, then it ascended from the heart, and remained in the tissue generated under the brain for a time, then it penetrates into the brain, and there occur for it, in its state of being in the brain, states and attributes other than the states and attributes that had occurred when it was in the heart.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the author's refutation of Galen's objections. The author first declares that both of Galen's answers are extremely weak. He then focuses on the first answer. The author argues that the six properties Galen lists for arteries (pulsation, hollowness, blood content, etc.) are properties that arteries have only from their origin at the heart until they branch into fine divisions and enter the substance of the brain. The author challenges Galen by asking: why do you claim that these properties persist in the fine fragments after this stage? He supports his challenge by noting that the brain spirit (animal spirit) is generated in the heart, ascends, rests in tissue under the brain, and then penetrates the brain; once inside the brain, it acquires new states and attributes different from those it had in the heart. By analogy, the arterial fragments that become nerves may also acquire new properties, so Galen's list of properties does not prove that nerves are not derived from arteries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Galen's list of arterial properties is fixed and unchanging, so nerves derived from arteries must retain those properties.
Correction: The author argues that properties change during transformation, as shown by the brain spirit; thus persistence is not automatic.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why are the attributes that Galen mentions for arteries not valid for the fine fragments that become nerves?
A) Because the fragments are too small to have properties
B) Because the fragments change after entering the brain, acquiring new attributes
C) Because Galen only studied large arteries, not small ones
D) Because the heart is not the source of arteries

Key Takeaway
The author challenges Galen's assumption that arterial properties persist in fine fragments, using the transformation of the brain spirit to argue that new attributes emerge upon entering the brain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you transition from one environment to another—like from home to school, or from comfort to challenge—do you assume that your inner qualities remain exactly as they were? What if, like the brain spirit, you actually acquire new attributes that change the way you think or act? How willing are you to examine whether your past traits still define you?
?
The author challenges a long-held assumption of Galen with a simple but profound observation: things change when they enter a new state. Is there an assumption in your life—about your faith, your character, or your relationships—that you have never questioned because 'it has always been that way'? What would it take for you to honestly test whether that assumption still holds?
?
If the properties of even the brain spirit are not fixed across states, why do you cling so tightly to your own labels or limitations? Ask yourself: 'What attributes am I holding onto that might actually be transformed if I allowed myself to enter a deeper state of knowledge or devotion?' Commit to finding one such attribute and actively seeking its transformation.

Aristotle vs. Galen on the Origin of Nerves
Aristotle's proof and Galen's response on nerve origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Aristotle argued for the heart as the origin of nerves, Galen's two counterarguments, and the author's critique of both positions.

Core Idea
Sense evidence alone doesn't settle the nerve origin debate; logical consistency matters too.

In this passage, the author presents a classical debate between Aristotle and Galen on whether nerves originate from the heart or brain. Aristotle uses logical arguments based on the need for solidity in voluntary motion, while Galen appeals to observation and biological complexity. The author then critically examines both positions, showing the limitations of each.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical exchange
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the structure: Aristotle's proof, Galen's two answers, and the author's critique.
Notice the author's own assessment at the end.
2
Identify Aristotle's proof elements
List the three types of solidity Aristotle attributes to the heart. Understand why he thinks the brain cannot be the origin.
3
Analyze Galen's two responses
Examine each response separately. Note that the first appeals to sense evidence, the second to the composite nature of muscles.
Consider the author's counterpoints to each.
4
Reflect on the author's critique
The author declares both answers weak. Identify his reasoning: sense only shows abundance, not origin; Galen's own evidence creates a contradiction with brain softness and nerve hardness.
This shows the dialectical method in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن أرسطاطاليس ، احتج على أن منبت العصب هو القلب بأن قال : الحركة الإرادية لا بد وأن تكون بآلة صلبة قوية ، والدماغ ليس بجرمه شيء من الصلابة والقوة ، فامتنع كونه منبتاً للعصب . وأما القلب ففيه أنواع من الصلابة. منها أن لحمه قوي شديد صلب من سائر اللحوم . ومنها : أنه فيه من الرباطات العصبية مقداراً كثيراً . ومنها : أنه بسبب كثرة حركته ، لا بد وأن يكون أقوى جرماً ، وإذا كان كذلك فقد ظهر أنه جعل القلب منبتاً للأعصاب ، التي هي آلات للحركات القوية ، أولى من جعل الدماغ منبتاً لها . وأجاب جالينوس عن هذه الحجة بوجهين : الأول : قال : إن هذا المستدل بنى كلامه على المقدمة القياسية . والحس دل على أن منبت الأعصاب هو الدماغ ، والقياس لا يلتفت إليه في معارضة الحس . الثاني : إن المباشر لتحريك الأعضاء ، ليس هو العصب فقط ، بل والعضلات. ومعلوم أن العضلات مركبة من الأعصاب والرباطات والأغشية واللحوم، وهي مستندة إلى الأعضاء الصلبة ، والأعصاب يفيدها الحس والقوة على الحركة ، وأما ما يختلط بها من الرباطات والأغشية ، فيفيدها القوة والشدة والأمن من الانقطاع . وعلى هذا التقدير فلا يمتنع أن يكون الدماغ منبتاً للأعصاب . واعلم : أن هذا الجواب عندي ضعيف . أما الجواب الأول : فجوابه : إن الحس لم يدل إلا على كثرة الأعصاب وقوتها عند الدماغ. وقد بينا أن هذا القدر لا يدل على كون الدماغ منبتاً للأعصاب . وأما الجواب الثاني : فضعيف أيضاً. لأن جالينوس استدل بغلظ العصب وكثرته عند الدماغ، على أن المنبت هو الدماغ . وأصحاب أرسطاطاليس ، قالوا : هذا الوجه الثاني الذي ذكره إن دل على قوله فكون الدماغ في غاية اللين وكون العصب في غاية القوة ، يمنع من كون الدماغ منبتا للعصب ، ولما حصل التعارض فيه سقط كلام جالينوس ، فهذا تمام الكلام في أن منبت العصب هو القلب أو الدماغ
Know that Aristotle argued that the origin of nerves is the heart by saying: Voluntary motion must be by a solid strong instrument, and the brain has no solidity or strength in its substance, so it is impossible for it to be the origin of nerves. As for the heart, it has types of solidity. Among them: its flesh is strong, very solid, more than the rest of the flesh. Among them: it contains a large amount of nerve ligaments. Among them: because of the frequency of its motion, it must be stronger in substance. And if that is so, then it has become clear that making the heart the origin of nerves, which are instruments for strong motions, is more appropriate than making the brain the origin. Galen answered this argument in two ways: First: He said: This arguer built his speech on a syllogistic premise. But the senses have indicated that the origin of nerves is the brain, and syllogism is not heeded in opposition to the senses. Second: The direct mover of the limbs is not only nerves, but also muscles. And it is known that muscles are composed of nerves, ligaments, membranes, and flesh, and they are supported by solid organs. The nerves give them sensory and motor power, while what mixes with them of ligaments and membranes gives them strength, firmness, and security from breaking. On this basis, it is not impossible for the brain to be the origin of nerves. Know that this answer is weak in my view. As for the first answer: Its response: The senses only indicated the abundance and strength of nerves near the brain. And we have explained that this amount does not indicate that the brain is the origin of nerves. As for the second answer: It is also weak. Because Galen argued by the thickness and abundance of nerves at the brain that the origin is the brain. And the followers of Aristotle said: This second point that he mentioned, if it indicates his statement, then the fact that the brain is extremely soft and the nerve is extremely strong prevents the brain from being the origin of nerves. And when the conflict occurred, Galen's speech fell. This is the conclusion of the discussion on whether the origin of nerves is the heart or the brain.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the physical origin of nerves, comparing Aristotle's and Galen's views. It begins by stating Aristotle's proof: voluntary motion requires a solid, strong instrument; the brain lacks solidity, while the heart possesses it in three ways (flesh, ligaments, motion), so the heart is the more appropriate origin. Galen then offers two counterarguments. First, he argues that sense evidence (observation) shows the brain is the origin, and syllogistic reasoning cannot override direct observation. Second, he notes that muscles, not just nerves, move limbs; muscles are composite and rely on solid organs, and nerves provide sensation and motion, while other tissues provide strength. Thus, the brain can be the origin. The author then critiques both responses. Against the first, he argues that sense only shows abundance and strength of nerves near the brain, not that the brain is the origin. Against the second, he states that Galen's own evidence (thickness of nerves at the brain) is countered by the brain's softness and the nerve's hardness, creating a contradiction that invalidates Galen's claim. The passage concludes that the debate remains between heart and brain.

Key Terms
العصب
القلب
الدماغ
الحركة الإرادية
الصلابة
القياس
الحس
العضلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sense evidence alone (observation of nerve abundance near the brain) is sufficient to prove the brain is the origin.
Correction: The author shows that sense only indicates abundance, not origin, and logical contradictions can override empirical observations.

Checkpoint
According to Aristotle's argument, why must the origin of nerves be the heart?
A) Because the heart is the center of emotions.
B) Because the heart is solid and strong, needed for voluntary motion.
C) Because the brain is too cold.
D) Because Galen proved it.

Key Takeaway
The origin of nerves remains debated between heart and brain; empirical and logical arguments both have limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are presented with two opposing arguments—one based on your direct observation and another based on logical reasoning—do you tend to dismiss the latter without careful examination? In what area of your life has this bias led you to a conclusion that later proved incomplete? This lesson shows that sense evidence can be ambiguous. Will you commit to testing your assumptions with both reason and observation before settling on a truth?
?
The author points out that Galen's own evidence (nerve thickness near the brain) contradicts his conclusion when considered alongside the brain's softness. Are there beliefs you hold where your own evidence actually undermines your conclusion? Perhaps you believe something about your own character based on external appearances, but a deeper look reveals inconsistency. Are you willing to reexamine such a belief today, even if it forces you to admit uncertainty?

Refuting Galen's Doubt on the Brain as Source
Author refutes Galen's claim that brain must be source of sensory power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes Galen's argument that the brain must be the source of sensory and motor power by presenting a plausible alternative scenario.

Core Idea
The brain as instrument does not prove it is the source.

Galen argued that since the brain is the origin of nerves (the instruments of sensation and motion), it must be the source of those powers. The author counters by granting the premise but denying the conclusion, offering a coherent alternative where the heart is the source and the brain merely conveys the instrument. This lesson examines that refutation and its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Understand Galen's argument
Read the first part of the passage where the author restates Galen's reasoning: the brain is the origin of nerves, and nerves are the instruments of sensory and motor power, therefore the brain is the source of those powers.
Note the key terms: المنبت (origin) and آلة (instrument).
2
See the author's concession and question
The author concedes that the brain is the origin of nerves but asks why that necessarily makes the brain the source of the powers themselves.
The distinction between 'origin of the instrument' and 'source of the power' is crucial.
3
Grasp the counter-possibility
The author proposes an alternative: sensory and motor power could be generated in the heart, while the brain sends a fixed instrument (nerve) to the heart, allowing the heart to benefit from that power.
If this scenario is possible, then Galen's proof is not definitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الثاني في الجواب عن شبهة جالينوس : فهو أن نقول : سلمنا أن الدماغ هو المنبت للعصب ، الذي هو آلة للقوة الحساسة ، والحركة الإرادية . فلم قلتم : إنه يلزم من هذا المعنى كون الدماغ ، معدناً لقوة الحس والحركة ؟ . بيانه : أنه لا يبعد أن تكون قوة الحس والحركة ، متولدة في القلب . إلا أن الدماغ يرسل إليه آلة ثابتة منه إلى القلب ، فيستفيد بتلك الآلة قوة الحس والحركة من القلب . وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال قائماً ، فقد سقطت الحجة التي ذكرها جالينوس والله أعلم
As for the second topic in answering Galen's doubt: We say: We grant that the brain is the origin of the nerve, which is the instrument of sensory power and voluntary motion. So why do you say that this meaning necessarily entails that the brain is the source of sensation and motion? Its clarification: It is not unlikely that the power of sensation and motion is generated in the heart, but the brain sends to it a fixed instrument from it to the heart, so it benefits by that instrument the power of sensation and motion from the heart. And if this possibility stands, then the proof that Galen mentioned has fallen. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Galen's argument that if the brain is the origin of nerves (which are instruments of sensation and motion), then the brain must be the source (معدن) of sensory and motor power. The author responds by granting that the brain is the origin of nerves but denying the necessary conclusion. He presents a counter-possibility: sensory and motor power could be generated in the heart, while the brain merely sends a fixed instrument (nerve) to the heart, allowing the heart to benefit from that instrument to receive or utilize the power generated in itself. Although the wording is ambiguous, the argument is that the brain's role could be that of a conduit or instrument rather than the source. Since this possibility is logically coherent, it undermines Galen's proof that the brain must be the source. The author concludes that Galen's proof collapses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author shows that this inference is not necessary; a plausible alternative exists where the heart is the source and the brain merely sends an instrument.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes Galen by showing that the brain could be merely an instrument for a power whose source is the heart, thus breaking the necessary link between origin of nerves and source of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author concedes a point but refuses to accept the conclusion it supposedly forces. Is there a belief you hold that you have never really examined because you think, 'If this premise is true, then that must follow'? Could the premise be true without forcing the conclusion? Are you willing to sit with that unsettling possibility and explore alternatives?
?
You have just seen that a respected figure like Galen can make an error by mistaking an instrument for a source. Where in your own spiritual life have you confused a means for the source—for example, treating a religious practice as the origin of faith rather than a channel for it? What would it mean to return the source to its rightful place and treat the instrument as just that?

The Brain as Condition, Not Source
Razi argues that the brain is a condition, not the source, for powers of sensation and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the brain is a condition for the manifestation of powers, not their source.

Core Idea
The brain enables actions but does not produce them.

In this passage, Razi engages with Galen's view that the brain is the leader of the senses. He presents his own position: the powers reside in the heart, and the brain is merely a condition for their manifestation. He supports this with a logical proof based on the attachment of the rational soul to the heart. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
The passage contains a clear argument structure: claim, proof, conclusion.
2
Identify Razi's claim and proof
Find the sentence stating Razi's view and the proof that follows. Note how he uses the concepts of species and genus.
3
Understand the distinction
Reflect on the difference between a source (origin) and a condition (enabler). Razi's argument hinges on this distinction.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرئيس المطلق . فهو أيضاً لازم على ( جالينوس ، في قوله : الدماغ هو رئيس الحواس الخمسة [ الظاهرة (١) ] والباطنة والأقرب عندي : أن يقال : هذه القوى كانت موجودة في القلب . والدماغ شرط لظهور (۳) الأفعال عن تلك القوى . والدليل على صحة ما قلناه : أنه متى تعلقت النفس الناطقة بالقلب ، فقد حصلت الإنسانية ، ومتى حصلت الإنسانية فقد حصلت الحيوانية لا محالة ، لأن حصول النوع مع عدم الجنس محال ، والفصل المقوم لماهية الحيوان ، هو قوة الحس والحركة [ فـإذن كما تعلقت النفس بالقلب ، فقد حصلت قوة الحس والحركة (۳) ] الإرادية ، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون الدماغ شرطاً يحتاج إليه في ظهور الفعل عن تلك القوى . فهذا ما نقوله [ في هذا الباب (٤) ] والعلم بحقائق الأشياء على التمام والكمال ، ليس إلا الله تعالى .
The absolute leader. So it is also necessary for Galen, in his statement: the brain is the leader of the five [apparent] senses and the internal ones. And the closer to me is that it be said: these powers were present in the heart. And the brain is a condition for the manifestation of actions from those powers. And the proof for the correctness of what we have said is that whenever the rational soul attaches to the heart, then humanity is obtained, and whenever humanity is obtained, then animality is obtained without fail, because the attainment of the species with the absence of the genus is impossible, and the differentia that constitutes the essence of animal is the power of sensation and voluntary motion. So therefore, just as the soul attaches to the heart, then the power of sensation and motion is obtained. So nothing remains except that the brain is a condition that is needed for the manifestation of the action from those powers. This is what we say in this chapter. And knowledge of the realities of things in their perfection and completeness belongs only to God Most High.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the role of the brain in relation to the powers of the soul. Razi begins by referencing Galen's claim that the brain is the leader of the senses. He then gives his own view: the powers are in the heart, and the brain is only a condition (shart) for the manifestation of actions. He provides a proof: when the rational soul attaches to the heart, the species of humanity obtains, and humanity entails animality. The differentia of animal is the power of sensation and voluntary motion. Therefore, since the soul attaches to the heart, these powers are present in the heart. So the brain cannot be the source; it is merely a condition. The passage concludes with a pious statement.

Correction: Razi argues that the brain is only a condition for their manifestation; the powers themselves are in the heart.

Key Takeaway

Razi's Classification of Views on the Soul's Origin
Razi classifies the possible positions on whether the rational soul is eternal or created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi classifies the possible views on whether the rational soul is eternal or created, distinguishing four distinct positions.

Core Idea
Razi maps out all logical possibilities on the soul's origin using a simple dichotomy.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents Razi's systematic classification of positions on the origin of the rational soul. Before engaging with arguments for or against each view, Razi first clarifies the conceptual landscape, showing that there are exactly four logical possibilities. This classification is essential for understanding the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to how Razi structures the possibilities.
The passage uses a dichotomous method: first eternal vs. created, then further subdivisions.
2
Identify the two dichotomies
Notice the two levels of classification: (1) eternal or created, and if eternal: necessary or possible; if created: pre-existent or non-pre-existent.
This yields exactly four positions.
3
Recognize the fourth position
The fourth position—created souls not existing before bodies—is attributed to Aristotle and his followers. Understand that this is one logical option among four.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع في أن النفوس الناطقة محدثة أو قديمة؟ وفيه أقوال : وضبطها : أن يقال : إنها [ إما قديمة أو حادثة . فإن كانت قديمة فإما أن يقال إنها ] واجبة لذواتها غنية عن السبب والمؤثر . وإما أن يقال : إنها [ ممكنة لذواتها واجبة بإيجاب ، سيبها وعلتها . وأما إن قلنا : إنها محدثة. فإما أن يقال إنها ] وإن كانت محدثة إلا أنها كانت موجودة قبل هذه الأبدان ، كما ورد في بعض الأخبار : ( إن الله تعالى خلق الأرواح قبل الأجساد بألفي عام ، وإما أن يقال : إن هذه الأرواح ما كانت موجودة قبل أبدائها . وهذا هو قول ( أرسطاطاليس ) وأتباعه من المتقدمين والمتأخرين
The seventh [section] concerning whether the rational souls are created or eternal. In it are statements. And their summary is that it is said: they are either eternal or created. If they are eternal, then either it is said that they are necessary in themselves, independent of cause and effect; or it is said that they are possible in themselves, necessary by the necessitation of their cause. And if we say that they are created, then either it is said that, although they are created, they existed before these bodies, as is related in some reports: 'God Most High created the souls two thousand years before the bodies'; or it is said that these souls did not exist before their bodies. And this is the statement of Aristotle and his followers among the ancients and the moderns.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible positions on the origin of rational souls. Razi begins by stating the question: are souls created or eternal? He then enumerates the possibilities in a dichotomous manner. First, souls are either eternal or created. If eternal, they are either necessary in themselves or possible in themselves but necessitated by a cause. If created, they either pre-exist their bodies (as some reports indicate) or they did not exist before their bodies (the view of Aristotle and his followers). This classification sets up the arguments to follow.

Key Terms
النفوس الناطقة
قديمة
حادثة
واجبة
ممكنة

Misconception: One might think that all created souls are the same, lacking further distinction.
Correction: Razi shows that created souls can be either pre-existent (existing before bodies) or non-pre-existent, a crucial distinction in the debate.

Checkpoint

A) two
B) four
C) three
D) six

Key Takeaway
Razi's classification reveals four and only four logical positions on the soul's origin, setting the stage for theological debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Four Objections Against the Claim That Nothing in Nature Is Idle
Four objections against the argument that souls are not idle without bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised against the argument that souls are not idle, and why they challenge the premise that nothing in nature is idle.

Core Idea
Four objections challenge the claim that souls are never idle, from tiny creatures to unproven premises.

In this passage, the author presents four distinct objections that opponents raise against the view that souls cannot be idle without bodies. These objections range from the perception of tiny insects to the doctrine of fetal souls after death. The author lists them without immediate refutation, showing the strength of the arguments against his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: each objection is introduced by 'الاعتراض'.
The passage begins mid-argument with 'فلا نسلم'.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection argues that small animals like fleas have perception and pleasure, so they are not idle. This challenges the idea that lack of benefit implies idleness.
Consider why even minimal perception might be enough to avoid idleness.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection suggests that before acquiring bodies, souls might have been attached to parts of the heavens or elements, obtaining pleasure through those instruments.
This objection provides an alternative way for souls to be active without bodies.
4
Understand the third objection
The third objection points to fetal souls after death: according to the opponent's doctrine, these souls remain forever without pleasure, which contradicts the claim that nothing is idle.
This is an internal critique using the opponent's own views.
5
Understand the fourth objection
The fourth objection concedes that souls might be idle but questions the premise 'nothing in nature is idle', noting that it was asserted without proof.
This objection attacks the foundation of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا نسلم ، لأنها لما كانت موصوفة بالحياة، وجب أن يحصل لها إدراك وشعور ببعض الأشياء ، ولعلها تلتذ بذلك القدر من الإدراك ، وحينئذ لا تكون معطلة . ألا ترى أنا نجد البق والبعوض وحيوانات أخرى أصغر منها بكثير موجودة . ولا نقول : بأنها لما قلت انتفاعاتها ولـذاتها ، كانت معطلة . بل نقول : إنها لما فازت بذلك القدر الحقير من الخير والمنفعة ، خرجت عن أن تكون معطلة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟ الاعتراض الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنها قبل الأبدان ، كانت متعلقة بجزء معين من السموات ، أو بجزء من أجزاء العناصر ، وكانت تنال الخيرات واللذات بسبب تلك الآلات ؟ . الاعتراض الثالث : أليس من مذهبكم : أن نفوس الأجنة ، إذا فارقت أبدانها ، فإنها تبقى أبد الآباد من غير أن يحصل لها لذة وخير ومنفعة ؟ وذلك يبطل قولكم : إنه لا معطل في الطبيعة الاعتراض الرابع : سلمنا أن تلك النفوس كانت معطلة . فلم قلتم : إنه لا معطل في الطبيعة ؟ فإن هذه المقدمة ذكروها ، وما رأيت أحداً منهم قواها بشبهة ، فضلا عن حجة
We do not concede, because when they [the animals] are described as alive, it is necessary that they have perception and awareness of some things, and perhaps they take pleasure in that amount of perception, and then they are not idle. Do you not see that we find fleas, mosquitoes, and other animals much smaller than them existing? And we do not say that because their benefits and pleasures are few, they are idle. Rather we say: because they have attained that small amount of good and benefit, they have left being idle. So why is it not permissible that the matter be so? The second objection: Why is it not permissible to say that before bodies, they were attached to a specific part of the heavens, or to a part of the elements, and they attained goods and pleasures through those instruments? The third objection: Is it not from your doctrine that the souls of fetuses, when they leave their bodies, remain forever without obtaining pleasure, good, or benefit? And that invalidates your statement: there is nothing idle in nature. The fourth objection: We grant that those souls were idle. So why did you say: there is nothing idle in nature? For they mentioned this premise, and I have not seen any of them strengthen it with a suggestion, let alone a proof.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents four objections against an earlier argument (not fully shown) that nothing in nature is idle (معطل). The opponent first objects that even small creatures like fleas have some perception and pleasure, so they are not idle. Then the second objection suggests that souls before bodies might have been attached to parts of the heavens or elements and thus obtained pleasure. The third objection points to fetal souls after death: according to the opponent's own doctrine, these souls remain forever without pleasure, which would contradict the claim that nothing is idle. Finally, the fourth objection concedes that souls might be idle but questions the premise that nothing in nature is idle, noting that this premise was asserted without evidence. The author does not immediately respond here.

Key Terms
معطلة (idle)
إدراك (perception)
التذاذ (pleasure)
نفوس الأجنة (fetal souls)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is refuting these objections in this passage.
Correction: The passage only lists the objections without providing any answer or refutation; the author is presenting the opponent's arguments.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the third objection?
A) Fetal souls after death are idle, contradicting the claim that nothing is idle.
B) Souls before bodies attach to heavenly parts.
C) Small animals have perception and pleasure.
D) The premise 'no idle thing' is unproven.

Key Takeaway
The four objections show that the claim that nothing in nature is idle faces serious challenges from small creatures, pre-bodily existence, fetal souls, and lack of proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent uses the example of fleas and mosquitoes to argue that even the smallest creatures have some perception and pleasure, so they are not idle. Look at the routine tasks in your daily life—what are the small, seemingly meaningless actions you do? Could they actually have hidden benefits or perceptions that you overlook? Commit today to finding one such activity and appreciating its purpose.
?
The fourth objection notes that the premise 'nothing is idle' was put forward without proof. Consider a personal belief you hold strongly about your faith or life. Has it been examined with evidence, or is it accepted because 'everyone says so'? Are you willing to question it to strengthen your conviction?

The Soul's Eternal Survival: The Argument from Its Cause
The author proves the soul's eternal survival after death by its independence from the body and the permanence of its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves the soul's eternal survival by linking it to the permanence of its cause, the Active Intellect.

Core Idea
The soul is independent of the body, so it outlives it, and its cause (Active Intellect) is eternal.

This passage presents a concise proof for the soul's immortality that relies on two key premises: the soul's independence from the body and the eternal nature of its cause. Understanding this argument is essential for grasping the philosophical basis of the soul's survival in this tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the English translation. Let the argument's flow come through.
Notice the structure: premise, premise, conclusion, further conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument rests on two premises: (1) the soul arises with the body, and (2) the soul is independent (self-sufficient) from the body. Find them in the text.
The first premise is in 'فعند حدوث البدن ، يجب حدوثه'. The second is in 'ثم إنه في ذاته غني عن هذا البدن'.
3
Trace the conclusion
From the premises, the author concludes that the death of the body does not cause the death of the soul. Then he adds a further conclusion: the soul must remain forever because its cause, the Active Intellect, is permanent.
The phrase 'لوجوب بقاء علته' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقبول تصرفه . فعند حدوث البدن ، يجب حدوثه ثم إنه في ذاته غني عن هذا البدن ، فلم يلزم من موت البدن موته. فوجب بقاؤه أبد الآباد ، لوجوب بقاء علته ، التي هي العقل الفعال
For acceptance of its action. So at the occurrence of the body, its (the soul's) occurrence is necessary. Then it is in itself independent of this body, so the death of the body does not necessitate its death. So its remaining forever is necessary, due to the necessity of the remaining of its cause, which is the Active Intellect.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the immortality of the soul. It begins by stating a premise: when the body comes into existence, the soul necessarily comes into existence. Then it argues that the soul is in itself (في ذاته) independent (غني) of the body. Therefore, the death of the body does not necessitate the death of the soul. Consequently, the soul must remain forever because its cause, the Active Intellect, is eternal and necessarily enduring. Thus the soul's survival follows from the permanence of its cause.

Key Terms
النفس
البدن
العقل الفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the soul's immortality is due to its own essence without need for a cause.
Correction: The text explicitly links the soul's eternal survival to the permanence of its cause (the Active Intellect), not to an inherent eternity.

Checkpoint

A) Because the body cannot die
B) Because its cause (the Active Intellect) remains forever
C) Because the soul is material
D) Because the soul desires the body

Key Takeaway
The soul's eternal survival is deduced from its independence from the body and the permanent nature of its cause, the Active Intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is truly independent of your body and will survive forever, what attachments to your body or worldly life are you holding onto that you need to release in light of this truth?
?
The argument from cause says your soul must remain forever because its cause is eternal. If that is certain, how should that certainty change the way you live today, starting this moment?
?
Your soul's eternal survival does not depend on your own effort but on a permanent cause beyond you. Does that humble you or make you more urgent about aligning your life with that cause?

The Basic Disagreement on Transmigration
Razi introduces the chapter and states the basic disagreement on transmigration.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The doctrine of transmigration is a point of contention: some affirm it, most deny it.

This short passage opens the eighth chapter of al-Matalib al-Aliyah, where Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins his discussion on transmigration. This sets the stage for the arguments and proofs that follow.

The First Difference Among Proponents of Transmigration
Razi introduces the first point of agreement and disagreement among transmigrationists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first point of agreement and the first point of disagreement among those who affirm the transmigration of souls.

Core Idea
Transmigrationists agree on animal hosts but split on plants and minerals.

Razi begins his treatment of transmigration by showing that even among its proponents, there is not complete uniformity. He identifies a shared core belief and a subsequent divergence. This passage highlights how the debate over transmigration is not monolithic, and sets the stage for further disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the first sentence states the universal agreement, the second introduces the point of difference.
The Arabic uses 'الأول' to indicate this is the first of several differences.
2
Identify the shared belief
Find the statement that all transmigrationists accept. The text says they all permit the transfer of human souls to the bodies of beasts and predators, and vice versa. Understand this as the common ground.
3
Identify the disputed issue
Look at the second part. The author asks whether transfer to plants and minerals is permissible. The answer: they differed. This is the first point of disagreement.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters. It shows that transmigration theories are not a single doctrine but a family of views. It also raises questions about the nature of souls and the types of bodies they can inhabit.

Second Difference: Eternal vs. Created World and Sub-Views
Razi explains the second division among transmigrationists regarding the world's eternity and subsequent views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second division among transmigrationists—between eternalists and creationists—branches into two distinct sub-views on the mechanism of soul transfer.

Core Idea
Among transmigrationists, the world's eternity divides views on soul migration.

Razi now presents the second point of disagreement: whether the world is eternal or created. Both sides agree on transmigration but differ on whether souls have always been migrating or began at creation. Within the creationist camp, two further views emerge: one based on love for matter, another on reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the second division and sub-views.
Notice the two groups: eternalists and creationists.
2
Examine the eternalist view
Identify the claim: souls existed before every body, with no beginning. This implies an infinite chain of transmigration.
Eternalists deny a first creation of souls.
3
Examine the creationist sub-views
There are two sub-views: (1) Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi's idea of love for matter, and (2) the idea of reward/punishment causing transfer to nobler or wretched bodies.
Al-Razi's view includes a final liberation when love ceases.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how these views differ on the role of attachment and moral accountability.
These are philosophical explanations of transmigration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن أهل التناسخ . فريقان : منهم من يقول بقدم العالم . ومنهم من يقول بحدوثه . أما القائلون بقدم العالم فقالوا : إن هذه النفوس كانت قبل كل بدن ، في بدن آخر ، لا إلى أول . وأما القائلون بحدوث العالم ، فقد أقروا بأن تتعلق النفوس بالأبدان أولاً . ثم اختلفوا . فقال ( محمد بن زكريا الرازي : : النفوس كانت مباينة عن الهيولى وغافلة عنه ، ثم اتفق لها التفات إلى الهيولى ، وعشق إليه ، فتعلقت به . فإذا فسد البدن ، وكان ذلك العشق باقياً، فهي تعود إلى بدن آخر ، ولا تزال تنتقل من بدن إلى بدن آخر ، إلى الوقت الذي يزول ذلك العشق ، وتحصل النفرة . فحينئذ تفارق ذلك البدن ، ولا تعود إلى بدن آخر . وقد استقصينا في شرح مذهبه ، في كتاب ( الحدوث والقدم وقال آخرون : إن هذه النفوس كانت كلفت بالطاعات، ومنعت عن المعاصي . فمن أطاع نقل روحه إلى بدن أشرف [ من بدنه (۱) ] وأعلى مما كان ، ليكون ذلك ثواباً له على تلك الطاعات ، ومن عصى نقل روحه إلى بدن شقي ، ليكون ذلك عقاباً له على ما كان عليه [ من المعاصي (۲) ]
And the second: The people of transmigration are two groups: among them are those who say the world is eternal, and among them are those who say it is created. As for those who say the world is eternal, they said: These souls were before every body, in another body, without beginning. And as for those who say the world is created, they affirmed that souls are attached to bodies initially. Then they differed. So Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi said: Souls were separate from matter (hayula) and heedless of it, then they happened to turn to matter and fell in love with it, so they became attached to it. When the body decays, if that love remains, they return to another body, and they keep transferring from body to another body until the time when that love ceases and aversion occurs. Then they depart that body and do not return to another body. We have elaborated in explaining his doctrine in the book 'Al-Huduth wa al-Qidam'. And others said: These souls were charged with acts of obedience and prohibited from acts of disobedience. So whoever obeyed, his soul is transferred to a body nobler than his body and higher than it was, as a reward for those acts of obedience; and whoever disobeyed, his soul is transferred to a wretched body, as a punishment for what he was upon of disobedience.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage describes the second point of disagreement among the proponents of transmigration. They divide into two main groups: those who believe the world is eternal and those who believe it is created. The eternalists hold that souls existed before every body in a previous body, with no first beginning. The creationists affirm that souls initially become attached to bodies at creation, but then they differ. Razi then presents two specific views among the creationists. The first is that of Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi, who argues that souls were originally separate from matter (hayula) and unaware of it, but then they turned towards matter and fell in love with it, leading to attachment. As long as that love persists, the soul transmigrates; when the love ceases and aversion sets in, the soul departs permanently. Razi notes that he has elaborated on this view in his book 'Al-Huduth wa al-Qidam'. The second view among creationists is that some souls are rewarded by being transferred to nobler bodies and punished by being transferred to wretched bodies, based on their obedience or disobedience.

Key Terms

قدم العالم
حدوث العالم
الهيولى
النفوس البشرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Punishment for sins
B) Its love for matter (hayula)
C) Divine command
D) Random chance

Key Takeaway
The second division among transmigrationists reveals diverse views on whether the world is eternal or created, affecting the explanation of soul transfer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi says souls are initially heedless of matter but then fall in love with it, causing endless wandering until that love dies. Is your heart in love with something that keeps you trapped in cycles of distraction? What would it take for that love to turn into aversion so your soul can be free?
?
The second variant teaches that your body's condition is a direct result of your deeds—obedience makes you noble, disobedience makes you wretched. If you truly believed that every action determined your next life's circumstances, would you live today any differently? What would you change?
?
Both views describe a soul trapped in a cycle—either by love or by karma. As a Muslim, you believe in a single life with a final judgment. Does that make you feel more accountable or less? Why?

First Proof That the Rational Soul Is Not Subject to Corruption
Avicenna's first proof that the rational soul is not corruptible, with its premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Avicenna's first proof that the rational soul is not corruptible, including the conditional argument and the two premises that support it.

Core Idea
A corruptible thing must be composite, but the rational soul is simple—so it cannot perish.

Avicenna opens his demonstration of the soul's immortality with a crisp conditional argument. If the soul could be destroyed, it would have to be made of parts, yet the rational soul is known to be simple. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of that proof and the two key premises that make it work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the conditional
Read the Arabic passage once. Then look for the statement that begins with 'If... then...' — this is the conditional that the proof aims to establish.
The conditional is: 'If the rational soul were subject to corruption, it would be composed of matter and form.'
2
Understand the first premise
The first premise states that the 'acceptance of corruption' must be distinct from the 'occurrence of corruption'. Reflect on the evidence given: acceptance occurs before the actual corruption.
3
Understand the second premise
The second premise states that the acceptance of corruption must be present at the very moment corruption occurs. This is taken as self-evident.
4
See how the premises lead to the conclusion
Combine the two premises to see why a corruptible thing would need to be composite. Then apply this to the soul: since the soul is simple, it cannot be corruptible.
The conclusion is that the soul is not subject to corruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : لو كانت النفس الناطقة قابلة للفساد ، لكانت مركبة من المادة (۱) والصورة . وهذا محال . فذاك محال .
بيان الشرطية موقوف على مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : إن كل ما يكون قابلاً للفساد ، [ فإن قبول الفساد (۲) ] يجب أن يكون مغايراً لحصول الفساد . بدليل أن قبول الفساد حاصل قبل حصول الفساد ، والحاصل قبل حصول شيء ، يجب أن يكون مغايراً لذلك الشيء ، فثبت : أن قبول الفساد [ مغاير للفساد .
والمقدمة الثانية : قبول الفساد ، لا بد وأن يكون حاصلاً حال حصول الفساد . وذلك معلوم بالضرورة .
The first proof: We say: If the rational soul were subject to corruption, it would be composed of matter (1) and form. But this is impossible. So that (i.e., the soul being corruptible) is impossible. The demonstration of the conditional depends on premises: The first premise: Everything that is subject to corruption, [the acceptance of corruption (2)] must be distinct from the occurrence of corruption. Evidence: The acceptance of corruption occurs before the occurrence of corruption, and what occurs before something must be distinct from that thing. So it is established that the acceptance of corruption is distinct from corruption. The second premise: The acceptance of corruption must [necessarily] be present at the time of the occurrence of corruption. And that is known by necessity.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) is not subject to corruption (fasād). The proof takes the form of a conditional syllogism: if the soul were corruptible, it would have to be composite (murakkaba) of matter and form, but composition in this sense is impossible for the rational soul, so its being corruptible is impossible. The author then clarifies that establishing this conditional requires two premises. The first premise states that the 'acceptance of corruption' (qubul al-fasād) is distinct from the 'occurrence of corruption' (ḥuṣūl al-fasād). This is argued on the basis that the acceptance occurs before the actual corruption, and what occurs before something must be distinct from that thing. The second premise states that the acceptance of corruption must be present at the very moment the corruption occurs, which is known by necessity. These two premises together support the conditional that if the soul were corruptible, it would need to be composite, because the acceptance and occurrence are distinct yet simultaneously present, implying a composition of potentiality and actuality.

Key Terms
نفس ناطقة
فساد
قبول الفساد
حصول الفساد
مادة
صورة
مغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this proof concerns physical corruption (like the body's decay), but here 'corruption' means perishing or ceasing to exist, and the argument is about the soul's metaphysical simplicity.
Correction: The proof is about the soul's potential to cease to exist entirely, not about physical decay. It draws on the metaphysical concept of composition (matter and form) and simplicity.

Checkpoint
What is the conditional (sharṭiyya) that the first proof aims to establish?
A) If the soul were corruptible, it would be composed of matter and form.
B) If the soul were composed of matter and form, it would be corruptible.
C) If the soul were not corruptible, it would be simple.
D) If the soul were simple, it would not be subject to corruption.

Key Takeaway
Avicenna's first proof shows that if the rational soul were corruptible, it would need to be composite, but since it is not, it is incorruptible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the rational soul is truly incorruptible and simple, what does that mean for your own constant fear of death or insignificance? Do you live as if you are an eternal being, or as if you are merely a body?
?
Avicenna proves your soul is not made of matter—so why do you still cling to material pleasures and possessions as if they define you? What would change if you truly believed your worth comes from your simple, immortal soul?
?
You have just read a rational proof that your soul cannot perish. Yet do you act as if your soul's fate after death is uncertain? If the soul is indestructible, then your choices today echo forever—are you prepared to face that reality, or do you prefer to ignore it?

The First Proof: Premises on Corruption
States the conditional and provides three premises leading to the conclusion that corruption implies composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical premises that establish the distinction between the capacity for corruption and its occurrence, and why these premises are necessary for proving the soul's incorruptibility.

Core Idea
Corruption requires a distinction between capacity and occurrence, and a substrate.

This lesson unpacks the foundational premises of the first argument that the rational soul is not subject to corruption. The author begins by stating a conditional: if the soul were corruptible, it would be composite. To prove this, he first establishes three premises about the nature of corruption itself. Understanding these premises is crucial to following the overall proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening conditional
Read the first line: 'If the rational soul were susceptible to corruption, then it would be composed of matter and form.' Notice that this is a conditional claim whose consequent is later shown to be impossible.
2
Study the three premises
Read each premise carefully. The first premise distinguishes capacity from occurrence. The second states capacity must be present at the time of corruption. The third says capacity is not self-subsistent but requires a subject.
Compare the examples given for each premise.
3
Understand the deduction to composition
Follow how the premises lead to the conclusion that if the soul were corruptible, it would have to have matter and form. Identify the role of the substrate (matter) in the analogy of clay and pot.
This sets the stage for the next lesson where composition is shown to be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : لو كانت النفس الناطقة قابلة للفساد ، لكانت مركبة من المادة والصورة . وهذا محال . فذاك محال .
بيان الشرطية موقوف على مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : إن كل ما يكون قابلاً للفساد ، فإن قبول الفساد يجب أن يكون مغايراً لحصول الفساد . بدليل أن قبول الفساد حاصل قبل حصول الفساد ، والحاصل قبل حصول شيء ، يجب أن يكون مغايراً لذلك الشيء ، فثبت : أن قبول الفساد مغاير للفساد .
والمقدمة الثانية : قبول الفساد ، لا بد وأن يكون حاصلاً حال حصول الفساد . وذلك معلوم بالضرورة .
والمقدمة الثالثة : إن قبول الفساد مفهوم غير مستقل بنفسه . بل لا بد من فرض شيء آخر يكون ذلك الشيء موصوفاً بذلك القبول .
وإذا ثبتت هذه المقدمات نقول : الموصوف بقبول الفساد إما أن يكون هو عين ذلك الشيء أو جزء من أجزائه. والأول باطل ، لأن قابل الفساد لا بد وأن يكون حاصلا حال حصول الفساد ، ووجود الشيء يمتنع أن يكون حاصلا حال حصول فساده. وإذا بطل هذا القسم ، تعين القسم الثاني ، وهو أن تكون تلك الماهية لها جزء ، ويكون ذلك الجزء موصوفا بقبول الفساد. وإمكان الفساد هو بعينه إمكان الحصول ، فكل ما يصح عليه الفساد وله جزء ، يكون ذلك الجزء محلاً لإمكان وجوده ، ولإمكان عدمه . وذلك الجزء هو المادة . كالطين الذي هو المادة للكوز ، بسبب أن المنطين محل لإمكان حدوث صورة الكوز ، ولإمكان عدمها . فثبت : أن النفس لو كانت قابلة للفساد ، لكانت مركبة من المادة والصورة .
وبيان أن كونها مركبة من المادة والصورة محال : هو أن تلك المادة ، إن كانت قابلة للفساد ، افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى ، ولزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل . وهما محالان. وإما الانتهاء إلى مادة لا مادة لها ، وحينئذ لا يكون قابلا للفساد . لما بينا : أن كل ما يقبل الفساد ، فله مادة . فالذي لا مادة له ، وجب أن لا يقبل الفساد . وعلى هذا التقدير فتلك المادة جوهر مجرد قائم بذاته . ولا معنى للنفس إلا ذلك . فلما كانت تلك المادة غير قابلة للفساد ، ثبت : أن النفس لا تقبل الفساد .
The first argument: that we say: If the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) were susceptible to corruption (qābila li-l-fasād), then it would be composed of matter (mādda) and form (ṣūra). And this is impossible (muḥāl). Therefore that [the antecedent] is impossible.
The demonstration of the conditional depends on premises:
The first premise: Every thing that is susceptible to corruption, [the capacity for corruption] must be different from the occurrence of corruption. By evidence that the capacity for corruption exists before the occurrence of corruption, and that which exists before something must be different from that thing. So it is established: that the capacity for corruption is different from corruption.
The second premise: The capacity for corruption must exist at the time of the occurrence of corruption. And that is known necessarily (bi-ḍ-ḍarūra).
The third premise: The capacity for corruption is a concept not self-subsistent. Rather, it is necessary to posit another thing, that thing being described by that capacity.
And when these premises are established, we say: The thing described by the capacity for corruption is either identical to that thing [the corruptible thing] or a part of its parts. The first is false, because the subject of corruption must exist at the time of the occurrence of corruption, and the existence of a thing is impossible to exist at the time of its corruption. And when this division is false, the second division is determined, namely that that essence (māhiyya) has a part, and that part is described by the capacity for corruption. And the possibility of corruption is identical to the possibility of existence; so everything that can undergo corruption and has a part, that part is a substrate for the possibility of its existence and for the possibility of its non-existence. And that part is matter. Like the clay (al-ṭīn) which is the matter for the pot, because the clay is a substrate for the possibility of the occurrence of the form of the pot and for the possibility of its non-existence. So it is established: that if the soul were susceptible to corruption, it would be composed of matter and form.
And the demonstration that it is impossible for it to be composed of matter and form is: that that matter, if it were susceptible to corruption, would depend on another matter, and either circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would follow. And both are impossible. Or it would end at a matter that has no matter, and then it would not be susceptible to corruption. Because we have shown: every thing that is susceptible to corruption has matter. So that which has no matter must not be susceptible to corruption. And on this supposition, that matter is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) subsistent by itself. And there is no meaning of the soul except that. So since that matter is not susceptible to corruption, it is established: that the soul does not admit corruption.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof that the rational soul is not subject to corruption (fasād). The author begins by stating the conditional claim: if the soul were corruptible, it would have to be composed of matter and form—which is impossible, so the soul must be incorruptible. The proof rests on three carefully defined premises.

First premise: The capacity for corruption (qabūl al-fasād) is distinct from the actual occurrence of corruption. The reason given is that the capacity exists before the event, and what exists before something must be different from it.

Second premise: The capacity for corruption must be present at the moment corruption occurs. This is said to be self-evident (bi-ḍ-ḍarūra).

Third premise: The capacity for corruption is not a self-subsistent concept; it requires something else to be described by it—a subject or substrate.

Using these premises, the author argues: The entity described by the capacity for corruption is either (1) identical to the thing that undergoes corruption, or (2) a part of it. Option (1) is impossible because the thing itself cannot exist at the moment of its own corruption (its existence would have to persist at its own destruction). So option (2) must hold: the corruptible thing has a part that serves as the substrate for the capacity. This part is what makes corruption possible—it is matter. For example, clay is the matter of a pot; it supports the possibility of the pot's form coming to be and ceasing to be. Thus, if the soul were corruptible, it would have to have matter and form—a material component.

Next, the author proves that such composition is impossible for the soul. Suppose the hypothetical matter itself were corruptible: then it too would require another matter, leading to an infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. If instead we reach a matter that has no matter (a simple, immaterial substrate), then that substrate would not be corruptible (since corruption requires matter). That simple substance is precisely what the soul is. Therefore, the soul does not admit corruption.

The proof is a classic demonstration that the soul is a simple, immaterial entity, aligning with the Aristotelian and Neoplatonic tradition that the rational soul is incorruptible.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
الفساد
قبول الفساد
المادة
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the capacity for corruption and the corruption itself are identical or occur simultaneously.
Correction: The first premise shows that the capacity must exist before the occurrence, so they are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, what is the relationship between the capacity for corruption (qabūl al-fasād) and the occurrence of corruption (ḥuṣūl al-fasād)?
A) They are identical in all respects.
B) The capacity occurs simultaneously with the occurrence.
C) The capacity is distinct from and prior to the occurrence.
D) The capacity is a subset of the occurrence.

Key Takeaway
The capacity for corruption is distinct from its occurrence and requires a substrate, implying that any corruptible thing must be composed of matter and form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that if your soul were corruptible, it would be composed of matter and form. But if your soul is immaterial, what does that mean for your fear of death? Do you live as if your soul is merely a product of your body, or as a trust from Allah that will return to Him?
?
The capacity for corruption requires a substrate—something that remains while change occurs. What 'substrate' in your life is the foundation for your spiritual states: is it your habits, your intentions, or something deeper? How would your actions change if you truly understood that your soul's core is beyond physical change?
?
In the example, clay is the matter that accepts both the shape of a pot and its breaking. If you are created from clay, what does that tell you about your own vulnerability to moral corruption? Are you arrogantly assuming your soul is 'incorruptible' in a spiritual sense, or are you actively guarding it against the rot of sin?

The Soul is Immaterial and Incorruptible
Demonstrates that composition of matter and form is impossible for the soul, concluding it is incorruptible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why composition of matter and form is impossible for the rational soul, and how the proof leads to the conclusion that the soul is a simple, immaterial substance not subject to corruption.

Core Idea
If matter itself were corruptible, it would lead to infinite regress; the soul is the final immaterial substrate.

In this lesson, we complete the first proof by examining why the composition of matter and form is impossible for the soul. The author argues that if the soul were composite, its material part would also require matter, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. The only way to avoid this is to posit a matter that has no matter—a simple, self-subsistent substance—which is precisely what the rational soul is. Hence, the soul is incorruptible.

Guided Path
1
Review the earlier conclusion
Recall that the previous lesson established that if the soul were corruptible, it would have to be composed of matter and form. Now we see why that composition is impossible.
2
Follow the regress argument
The author argues that if the matter of the soul were itself corruptible, it would require another matter, leading to either circularity or infinite regress. Understand why both are impossible.
The argument assumes that corruption requires matter, so an infinite chain of matters is contradictory.
3
Identify the endpoint
The only alternative is to reach a matter that has no matter, which is not corruptible. That matter is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) subsistent by itself, which is the soul itself. Therefore, the soul does not admit corruption.
This concludes the first proof.

Proof that Rational Souls are Incorruptible with Objection and Answer
The author refutes the corruptibility of souls and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's logical proof that rational souls cannot be corrupted and how he refutes the opposing argument based on possibility of non-existence.

Core Idea
The soul's lasting nature makes it impossible to be conditioned by what does not last.

This passage presents a compact but powerful argument for the incorruptibility of rational souls. The author first refutes a prior claim by analyzing whether the supposed conditions for the soul's existence are permanent or transient. He then anticipates an objection from those who argue that since the soul is originated, it must be receptive to non-existence, and provides a concise rebuttal. Mastering this argument helps clarify the distinction between possibility of origination and possibility of corruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation without stopping to analyze. Let the author's flow of argument sink in.
The passage contains both a proof and an objection-and-answer.
2
Identify the two-horned argument in the proof
Notice how the author sets up a dilemma: either the conditions persist or they don't. Work through each horn and see how each leads to a contradiction for the opponent.
This is a classic kalam strategy.
3
Examine the opponent's objection
Read the objection carefully. The opponent tries to force a regress or necessity by analyzing the receptivity to non-existence. Understand the two possibilities they offer.
4
Understand the author's answer
The author gives two responses: one is that the objection assumes the soul's origination (which is disputed), and the other is that the difference between possibility of origination and corruption has already been explained. Consider why this suffices.
The author does not repeat the earlier explanation; he merely points to it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : هذا أيضاً باطل . لأن تلك الشرائط، إما أن تكون باقية ، وإما أن لا تكون باقية . فإن كانت [باقية عاد التقسيم المذكور في كيفية عدمها بعد بقائها] وإن لم تكن باقية امتنع كونها شرائط لوجود النفوس . لأن النفوس باقية وتلك الأشياء غير باقية . وكون الباقي مشروط الوجود بما لا يبقى : محال . فثبت بهذا البيان والتقرير : أن النفوس الناطقة غير قابلة للعدم والفساد . وهو المطلوب. فوجب، واحتج القائلون بأنها قابلة للفساد : بأن قالوا : ثبت أنها حادثة، كونها قابلة للعدم . فقابلية العدم، إن كانت من اللوازم ، وجب بقائها أبداً فوجب كونها قابلة للعدم بعد وجودها . فتكون قابلة للفساد . وإن لم تكن تلك القابلية من [لوازم تلك الماهيات كانت صفة عارضة لها . فقابلية تلك القابلية ، إن كانت من اللوازم عاد الإلزام ، وإن لم تكن من] اللوازم كانت مسبوقة بقابلية أخرى إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال . والجواب : هذا الكلام بناء على أن النفوس حادثة ، والبحث فيه ما تقدم . ثم نقول : الفرق بين إمكان الحدوث وبين إمكان الفساد بعد الوجود : قد ذكرناه . فلا فائدة في الإعادة. [والله أعلم].
We say: this, too, is false. Because those conditions, either they are lasting or they are not lasting. If they are [lasting, the aforementioned division returns concerning the manner of their non-existence after their existence]; and if they are not lasting, it is impossible for them to be conditions for the existence of the souls. Because the souls are lasting and those things are not lasting. And the lasting being conditioned in existence by what does not last: is impossible. So it is established by this explanation and clarification: that the rational souls are not receptive to non-existence and corruption. And that is what was sought. So it is necessary. And those who say that they are receptive to corruption argued: by saying: it is established that they are originated, being receptive to non-existence. So the receptivity to non-existence, if it is from the necessary concomitants, then its remaining forever is necessary, so it is necessary that it is receptive to non-existence after its existence. Thus it becomes receptive to corruption. And if that receptivity is not from [the necessary concomitants of those essences, it is an accidental attribute of them. Then the receptivity of that receptivity, if it is from the necessary concomitants, the implication returns, and if it is not from] the necessary concomitants, it is preceded by another receptivity ad infinitum. And that is impossible. And the answer: this speech is based on the fact that the souls are originated, and the discussion about that has preceded. Then we say: the difference between the possibility of origination and the possibility of corruption after existence: we have already mentioned it. So there is no benefit in repetition. [And God knows best].
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the incorruptibility of rational souls, followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins by refuting the claim that the soul is subject to non-existence and corruption. He argues that the conditions for the soul's existence either persist or do not. If they persist, the earlier division applies. If they do not persist, they cannot be conditions for the soul because the soul is lasting while they are not, and it is impossible for a lasting thing to be conditioned by what does not last. Thus it is proven that rational souls are not receptive to non-existence or corruption. Then the opponents argue that since the soul is originated, it must be receptive to non-existence, and this receptivity is either a necessary concomitant or accidental. If necessary, the soul must be receptive to corruption; if accidental, then that receptivity requires another receptivity ad infinitum, which is impossible. The author answers that this argument presupposes the soul's origination, which is a point of contention. Moreover, the difference between the possibility of origination and the possibility of corruption after existence has already been explained, so there is no need to repeat it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if something is contingent in its origination, it must also be contingent in its corruption.
Correction: The author distinguishes between the possibility of origination and the possibility of corruption after existence; they are not the same, and the soul's lasting nature prevents corruption.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for the soul to be conditioned by something that does not last?
A) Because the soul is originated.
B) Because the soul is lasting and the condition is not.
C) Because the condition is impossible.
D) Because the soul is not receptive to non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The rational soul's lasting nature and the impossibility of a lasting entity being conditioned by transitory factors prove its incorruptibility, and the objection from origination fails because the possibility of origination differs from the possibility of corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your rational soul is truly incorruptible and lasting, while your body and all material possessions are transient, why do you invest so much effort in preserving what will not last, while neglecting the state of your soul?
?
The author argues that a lasting thing cannot be conditioned by what does not last. What transitory things in your life—such as your health, wealth, or reputation—are conditioning your spiritual state? Are you allowing temporary circumstances to dictate the direction of your eternal soul?
?
The objection about receptivity to corruption presupposes the soul's origination. But if you were to live as if your soul has no end, how would your fear of death and your daily priorities change starting today?
